Nikah — Marriage
beginner
6 min
Prohibition of Marriage and Gift for the Mukatab
Stating the restriction on the mukatab's marriage and gift

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on a mukatab's marriage and gift without permission and identify the underlying rationale.

Core Idea
A mukatab may not marry or gift without the master's permission because these acts are not means of acquisition and harm the master.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains a key restriction on the mukatab (a slave under a contract of manumission). While the mukatab is granted transactional freedom to earn his freedom, certain acts like marriage and gift-giving remain forbidden without the master's consent. Understanding this ruling illuminates the Hanafi approach to balancing the mukatab's autonomy with the master's financial interests.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
What type of act is being discussed (marriage and gift)? According to the passage, what is the default ruling for a mukatab regarding these acts? (Answer: prohibited without permission.)
The act falls under mu'amalat (transactions); the ruling is specific to the mukatab's status.
2
Understand the rationale (ʿilla)
Why are these acts prohibited? Identify the two reasons given: they are not a means of earning (laysa min jins al-iktisab) and they cause harm to the master by imposing dower and maintenance obligations.
The harm here is financial liability that the master must bear if the mukatab defaults.
3
Determine the condition for permissibility
Under what condition does the prohibition lift? The passage states: 'if the master gives him permission, it becomes permissible' (fa-idha adhina lahu al-mawla jaza). Identify permission (idhn) as the key condition.
Permission can be explicit or implicit; it removes the hajr (restriction).
4
Apply the concept of hajr
Why does the mukatab remain under hajr for these acts? Because the kitabah contract only authorizes acquisitions that help pay the consideration; marriage and gift are not such means. Understand how hajr protects the master's interests.
Hajr is not total incapacity but a targeted restriction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[0] قوله : لا تزوّجه إلا بإذنه ؛ لأنه ليس من جنس الاكتساب، وفيه ضرر للمولى بلزوم المهر والنفقة في رقبته ، وإنّما استفاد من التصرّف بعقدِ الكتابة ما كان سبباً إلى أداء بدلها، وهو ليس بوسيلة إلى أداء البدل، فيبقى على الحجر، فإذا أذن له المولى جازَ
لزوال الحجر حينئذ ، هذا في المكاتب.
[0] His statement: Do not marry him except with his permission, because it is not of the kind of acquiring property, and it contains harm to the master by the obligation of the dower and maintenance upon his neck. He only benefited from the transaction by the contract of kitabah that which is a means to pay its consideration, and it is not a means to pay the consideration, so he remains under restriction. So if the master gives him permission, it is permissible, because the restriction is then removed. This is regarding the mukatab.
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling that the mukatab cannot marry or give gifts without the permission of his master. The reason is that marriage and gift are not means of earning money (which is the purpose of the kitabah contract), and they impose financial obligations (dower and maintenance) on the master. Therefore, the mukatab remains under legal restriction (hajr) regarding such transactions. If the master grants permission, the restriction is lifted and the act becomes permissible.

Key Terms
الحجر (al-hajr): Legal restriction; here, the incapacity of the mukatab to engage in certain transactions without permission.
الإذن (al-idhn): Permission from the master for the mukatab to perform an act that would otherwise be restricted.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on a mukatab giving a gift without his master's permission?
A) Permissible
B) Recommended
C) Haram
D) Makruh

Key Takeaway
A mukatab's marriage and gift are prohibited without the master's permission because they are not means of earning and harm the master by imposing financial obligations.

The Female Mukataba's Marriage Without Permission: An Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute and extension

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a female mukataba marrying without her master's permission, explain Zufar's opposing view, and apply the ruling to the case of concubinage.

Core Idea
A female mukataba cannot marry without her master's permission because she is owned; her dower belongs to the master, not to her.

This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the marriage of a female mukataba (a slave with a manumission contract). The central question is whether she can enter into a marriage contract without her master's consent. The established Zahir al-Riwayah position, attributed to Abu Hanifa, prohibits it, while Zufar permits it by analogy to earning. The passage also extends the principle to concubinage, illustrating the school's consistent stance on ownership and permission.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed act and parties
Name the specific legal act (marriage without permission) and the two scholars involved: Abu Hanifa (prohibiting) and Zufar (permitting).
The dispute is intra-Hanafi; Zufar is a Hanafi scholar, not an outsider.
2
Follow each scholar's proof (dalil)
State Abu Hanifa's reason: she is owned by the master, so she cannot dispose of herself or her dower. State Zufar's reason: marriage is a form of earning (kash), so she may do it to acquire the dower.
The dower (mahr) is the crux: is it compensation for the person or for benefits?
3
Trace the Hanafi response to Zufar
Explain the rebuttal: the dower is in exchange for ownership of the essence (al-milk bi al-dhat), not for usufruct, so it belongs to the master, not the mukataba. Marriage is not a pure acquisition.
This is a talil-based argument differentiating marriage from earning.
4
Apply the post-emancipation scenario
Work through the consequence: if she is emancipated before the marriage is annulled, the marriage becomes valid because the master's right ceases. She has no option to cancel because she consented.
The marriage is not void ab initio; it is suspended pending the master's right.
5
Extend the ruling to concubinage
Just as a male mukatab cannot marry without permission, a female mukataba cannot take a concubine. The basis is that concubinage requires full ownership, which a slave does not have.
This extension is via qiyas: the illa is lack of ownership over the self.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما في المكاتبة هل يجوز لها التزوج بلا إذنه ؟ ففيه خلاف ؛ قال شيخ الإسلام : لا تتزوج المكاتبة بغير إذن مولاها ؛ لأنها مملوكة للمولى، وقال زفر ه: يجوز ذلك ؛ لأنه من باب الاكتساب. إنا نقول : المهر وجب في مقابلة الملك بالذات لا في المنافع، وهو حق السيد، فإن عتقت قبل أن يفسخ النكاح صح ذلك النكاح ؛ لأنه لا يفسدُ إلا لحق المولى، فإذا عُتِقَتْ زال حقه، فصح، ولا خيار لها ؛ لأنها باشرت العقد برضاها، ونفذ بعد الإعتاق. كذا في «العناية». ونحن نقول : كما لا يجوز تزوجه بلا إذن مولاه لا يجوز تسريه أيضاً، وإن كان مع إذنه كالفن والمدبر والمأذون، وهو اتخاذ الجارية للتمتع ؛ وذلك لأن مبنى حل التسري على ملك الرقبة، والرقيق مطلقاً لا يملك شيئاً من أحكام مالية المال، فلا ينفعهم إذنه ، وسيشير الشارح الله إلى هذا المعنى بُعَيْدَ هذا بقوله ؛ لأنّ كسب المكاتب موقوف، فلا يتعلق به ما لا يحتمل الفسخ. هكذا في «حاشية الجلبي».
As for the female mukataba, is it permissible for her to marry without his permission? There is a difference of opinion. Sheikh al-Islam said: The female mukataba does not marry without the permission of her master, because she is owned by the master. Zufar said: It is permissible, because it is from the category of earning. We say: The dower is obligated in exchange for ownership of the essence, not for benefits, and it is the right of the master. If she is emancipated before the marriage is annulled, that marriage becomes valid, because it is not invalid except due to the right of the master. When she is emancipated, his right ceases, so it is valid. She has no option, because she performed the contract with her consent, and it becomes effective after emancipation. Thus in al-Inayah. And we say: Just as his marriage is not permissible without the permission of his master, likewise taking a concubine is not permissible, even if with his permission, like the artisan, mudabbar, and ma'dhun. That is, taking a slave-girl for enjoyment. Because the basis of the permissibility of concubinage is ownership of the neck, and a slave absolutely does not own anything of the rulings of financial ownership, so their permission is of no benefit. The commentator will refer to this meaning later in his statement: Because the earning of the mukatab is suspended, so what cannot tolerate annulment is not attached to it. Thus in Hashiyat al-Jalabi.
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about whether a female mukataba (a slave who has a contract of manumission) can marry without her master's permission. The view attributed to Sheikh al-Islam (Abu Hanifa) is that she cannot, because she is still owned by the master. Zufar argues that she can, because marriage is a means of earning (since she may receive dower). The Hanafi response explains that the dower is owed in exchange for ownership of the person, not merely for benefits, and thus it belongs to the master. If she is emancipated before the marriage is annulled, the marriage becomes valid because the master's right is lifted. She has no option to cancel the marriage because she consented. The passage then extends the ruling: just as a male mukatab cannot marry without permission, a female mukataba cannot take a concubine (if she were male) – but here it applies analogously. The commentator notes that the slave's permission is ineffective because concubinage requires ownership of the slave, and the slave does not own property to transfer. This illustrates the general principle that a mukatab's earnings are suspended and cannot be used for irrevocable transactions.

Key Terms
مكاتبة (mukātaba): A female slave who has made a contract with her master to be emancipated upon payment of a sum, retaining certain rights over her earnings.
تزوج (tazawwuj): To enter into a marriage contract; specifically for a female mukataba, it requires master's permission.
مهر (mahr): The dower given by the husband to the wife; in this case, it is considered a right of the master because it is in exchange for ownership of the person.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what happens if a female mukataba marries without her master's permission and then is emancipated before the marriage is annulled?
A) The marriage is void from the beginning.
B) The marriage becomes valid upon emancipation, and she has the option to cancel.
C) The marriage becomes valid upon emancipation, and she has no option to cancel.
D) The marriage remains invalid even after emancipation.

Key Takeaway
A female mukataba's marriage without master's permission is prohibited; if she is freed before annulment, it becomes valid without her option to cancel.

Inclusion of a Mukatib's Child in His Kitabah
Ruling with proof on child's inclusion in kitabah via claim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the inclusion of a mukatib's child in his kitabah and explain the role of the mukatib's claim (da'wa) and possession (yad) in establishing paternity despite the prohibition on intercourse.

Core Idea
A mukatib's child enters his kitabah upon his claim, because possession (yad) suffices for paternity even if intercourse is unlawful.

This passage addresses a nuanced ruling in Hanafi fiqh: whether a child born to a mukatib (a slave who has contracted to purchase his freedom) from his slave girl is included in his kitabah. The matn and commentary establish that if the mukatib claims the child, the child enters the kitabah. This ruling is based on the concept of yad (possession) and is supported by a hadith. The commentary also resolves an apparent contradiction: a mukatib is prohibited from intercourse with his slave girl, so how can a child be born? The answer lies in the sufficiency of yad for paternity upon claim.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the birth of a child to a mukatib from his slave girl. The ruling: if the mukatib claims the child, the child enters his kitabah.
2
Locate the dalil
The hadith 'Her child freed her' (أعتقها ولدها) is cited as evidence for the general principle that a child's birth can affect the mother's status.
3
Understand the role of claim
The child does not automatically enter; the mukatib must claim (da'wa) paternity. The claim is what activates the inclusion.
4
Address the objection
Objection: The mukatib cannot lawfully have intercourse, so how can a child be his? Response: Possession (yad) over the slave girl suffices for paternity upon claim, analogous to cases of shared slave girls and a father's intercourse with his son's slave girl.
5
Note the scope
This ruling applies to the mukatib. The three imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) agree on the prohibition of intercourse but still allow paternity through yad and claim.

Source Text

كتاب المكاتب تصرفاته وعجزه وأحكامه
كولد ولد له من أمته
قال: «أعتقها ولدها» (١)، ولا يثبت أصالة، والقياس ينفيه، (كولد ولد له من أمته، يتعلق بقوله: ويكاتب عليه بالشراء: أي إن ولد ولد من أمته فادعاه دخل في كتابته
[١] قوله: والقياس ينفيه؛ يعني ولا نص فيه ليترك القياس به، بخلاف ما إذا كان معها الولد. كذا في «حاشية الجلبي» (٢)
[٢] قوله: كولد... الخ؛ صورته كما لو ولد له ولد من أمة له دخل في كتابة، فإن قلت: المكاتب لا يملك التسري، ولا وطء أمته وبه قالت الثلاثة، فمن أين ولد له ولد حتى يدخل في كتابته. قلت: نعم إلا أنه له في مكاتبه يداً كالحر؛ وذلك يكفي لثبوت النسب منه عند الدعوى، وإن لم يحل وطؤه، كما في الجارية المشتركة، وجارية الابن إذا وطئها الأب وادعى الولد ثبت النسب، وإن لم يحل لأحد الشريكين وللأب وطء الجارية المشتركة وجارية الابن، هكذا في شرح العيني» (٣).
(٣) قوله: دخل في كتابته؛ لما بينا في المشتري من أنه من أهل أن يكاتب وإن لم يكن من أهل الاعتاق، فيجعل مكاتباً تحقيقاً للصلة بقدر الإمكان، وكان حكمه كحكمه.
فإن قلت: المكاتب لا يملك المشتري ولا وطء أمته وبه قالت الثلاثة فمن أين ولد له ولد حتى يدخل في كتابته.
(1) من حديث ابن عباس الله في سنن ابن ماجه) (٨٤١) ، و((المستدرك» (۲ : ۲۳)، وسنن البيهقي الكبير (١٠: (٣٤٦)، وسنن الدارقطني (٤) : ۱۳۱)، و(مصنف عبد الرزاق(۷: ۲۳۳)، و«الآحاد والمثاني (٥) : (٤٥٠) ، و(مسند ابن الجعد (۱): (٢٦٥)، وغيرها، قال ابن القطان: له إسناد جيد. ورواه ابن حزم بإسناد صحيح ،وصححه، وله شواهد كثيرة. ينظر : «الدراية»(۲) : (۸۷) ، و(الخلاصة (۲) : ٤٦٤)، وغيرهما.
(۲) (ذخيرة العقبى» (ص٥٢٧).
(٣) أي (البناية»(٨ : ٥٧).
Chapter of the Mukatib: His Transactions, Incapacity, and Rulings. Like a child born to him from his slave girl. He said: 'Her child freed her' (1), but it is not established originally, and analogy negates it. (Like a child born to him from his slave girl, it relates to his saying: 'And he writes a kitabah upon him with the purchase': i.e., if a child is born to him from his slave girl and he claims him, he enters his kitabah. [1] His saying: 'And analogy negates it'; meaning there is no text to leave the analogy, unlike when the child is with her. This is in Hashiyat al-Jalabi (2). [2] His saying: 'Like a child...' etc.; its example is if a child is born to him from his slave girl and he enters his kitabah. If you say: The mukatib does not own concubinage, nor intercourse with his slave girl, and the three (imams) said that, so how is a child born to him to enter his kitabah? I say: Yes, except that he has possession (yad) over his mukatab like a free person; that suffices for the establishment of paternity from him upon claim, even if his intercourse is not lawful, as in the case of a shared slave girl, and the slave girl of a son when the father has intercourse and claims the child, paternity is established, even though intercourse with a shared slave girl is not lawful for either partner, nor for the father with the son's slave girl. This is in Sharh al-Ayni (3). (3) His saying: 'He entered his kitabah'; because we explained regarding the purchaser that he is among those eligible to make a kitabah even if he is not eligible to emancipate, so he is made a mukatib to effect the connection as much as possible, and his ruling is like his ruling. If you say: The mukatib does not own the purchased slave nor intercourse with his slave girl, and the three said that, so how is a child born to him to enter his kitabah? (1) From the hadith of Ibn Abbas in Sunan Ibn Majah, etc. (2) Dhakhirat al-Uqba p.527. (3) i.e., al-Binaya 8:57.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling that a child born to a mukatib from his slave girl enters the kitabah if the mukatib claims the child. The matn quotes the hadith 'Her child freed her', indicating that the child's birth brings freedom to the mother. However, the commentary clarifies that this is not established by original rule; analogy would negate it. The ruling is specifically for a mukatib: if a child is born from his slave girl and he claims it, the child enters the kitabah. The commentary addresses an objection: since a mukatib is not allowed to have intercourse with his slave girl (as agreed by the three imams), how can a child be born? The answer is that the mukatib has possession (yad) over his slave girl similar to a free person, which is sufficient for paternity to be established if he claims the child, even if intercourse is not lawful. This is analogous to cases of shared slave girls and a father's intercourse with his son's slave girl, where paternity is established through claim despite the prohibition of intercourse. The hadith of Ibn Abbas is cited as evidence for the general principle.

Key Terms
يد (yad): Possession or control; here it refers to the mukatib's de facto control over his slave girl, which is sufficient for paternity establishment.
دعوى (da'wa): Claim; the mukatib's claim of paternity is required for the child to enter the kitabah.
ثلاثة (thalathah): The three (imams): Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani, who agree that a mukatib cannot lawfully have intercourse with his slave girl.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How does a child born to a mukatib from his slave girl enter the kitabah?
A) Automatically upon birth.
B) Only if the mukatib explicitly buys the child.
C) If the mukatib claims the child as his own.
D) The child never enters the kitabah.

Key Takeaway
A mukatib's child enters his kitabah only if the mukatib claims paternity, because yad (possession) suffices for lineage establishment even when intercourse is unlawful.

Nikah — Marriage
advanced
8 min
Child's Status When a Supposedly Free Woman Marries a Mukatib or Slave
Intra-Hanafi dispute on child's slavery/freedom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions (Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf vs. Muhammad al-Shaybani) on the child's slavery/freedom and explain the competing legal reasoning (qiyas based on parentage vs. gharur/deception).

Core Idea
When a slave father is deceived, Muhammad gives the child freedom with compensation; the majority holds the child remains a slave.

This passage presents a disagreement within the Hanafi school itself. The case involves a mukatib or slave who marries a woman claiming to be free; later she is discovered to be a slave. The child's status—slave or free—turns on whether the father's deception (gharur) can override the principle that a child follows the mother in slavery. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold the child is a slave; Muhammad al-Shaybani says the child is free upon payment of its value.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute and ruling positions
Read the text carefully to see that the child's status is disputed: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf say the child is a slave; Muhammad says it is free upon payment of value (الحر بالقيمة). Note that the father is a mukatib or slave married with permission.
The woman claimed to be free but was later claimed as a slave.
2
Understand the common ground and the contested point
Both sides agree on the facts. The point of ikhtilaf is whether the principle 'the child follows the mother in slavery' applies or whether the father's deception creates an exception.
The text states 'ley the origin is that the child follows the mother in slavery and freedom'.
3
Examine the evidence for the majority position (Abu Hanifa & Abu Yusuf)
The majority rely on qiyas (analogy): since both parents are slaves (the father by being a mukatib/slave, the mother in reality), the child is born between two slaves and thus is a slave. This is the default rule.
The text says: 'لهما : أن القياس أن يكون عبداً لكونه مولوداً بين رقيقين'.
4
Examine the evidence for the minority position (Muhammad al-Shaybani)
Muhammad argues that the father was deceived (gharur) by the woman's claim of freedom; he married with the intention of having free children. Therefore, the child is given freedom in exchange for compensation (qimat al-awlad). This is analogous to the case of a free man deceived.
The text says: 'وعند محمد له حرّ بالقيمة؛ لأنه ولد المغرور'.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and the mufta bihi
The practical consequence: if the child is a slave, it belongs to the mother's master; if free on payment, the father must pay the child's value to the mother's master. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is typically that of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.
The text does not explicitly state the tarjih, but later Hanafi sources usually adopt the majority view.

If a free woman by her claim gives birth from a mukatib or a slave who married her with permission, and then she is claimed as a slave, her child is a slave. That is: The mukatib married a woman with his master's permission, and she said, 'I am free.' She gave birth from him and then was claimed as a slave. Her child is a slave according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. According to Muhammad, the child is free upon payment of value because it is the child of the deceived one. For them (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), the analogy is that the child is a slave because it is born between two slaves. [2] It is not permissible to sell his mother. Thus in al-'Inaya. [1] His saying: 'If a free woman gives birth...' etc. This involves two issues: First: If a mukatib marries a woman with his master's permission, claiming she is free, and she gives birth from him and then is claimed as a slave, her child is a slave, and the mukatib does not take the children by paying value to the claimant; i.e., the child is not free by paying value. The commentator explicitly stated this. Second: If a slave permitted to marry marries a woman, she gives birth, and then is claimed as a slave, his child is likewise a slave. The commentator did not state this explicitly due to its obviousness. This is according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. According to Muhammad, the children in both scenarios are free upon payment of value because he shared with a free person in the cause of establishing this right, which is deception (gharur); for he only desired to marry her to attain freedom of children. So the value of the children and the dower are due immediately due to the master's permission, and the children are free. This is the riwaya of al-Mabsut. In Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir: According to him (Muhammad), the payment of the children's value is deferred until after manumission. The commentator alluded to this with his saying: 'and here there is no power...' etc., as understood from the exposition of al-Akmal. Thus in Hashiyat al-Jalabi. [2] His saying: 'between two slaves' – a child born between them is a slave, and this is because the principle is that the child follows the mother in slavery and freedom.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the status of children born to a woman who claimed to be free but was later discovered to be a slave, married to a mukatib or a slave with permission. The issue is whether the child is free or slave. According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, the child is a slave because both parents were slaves at the time of conception (the father is a slave/mukatib, the mother is a slave in reality). The child is born between two slaves, so the principle 'the child follows the mother in slavery' applies. Hence the child is a slave, and the father does not take the child by paying value. According to Muhammad al-Shaybani, however, the child is free upon payment of its value. The reasoning is that the father (mukatib or slave) was deceived (gharur) by the woman's false claim of freedom; he married her with the intention of having free children. Because of this deception, the child is given freedom in exchange for compensation. There is a difference in riwaya regarding the timing of payment: in al-Mabsut, the value is due immediately, while in Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir, it is deferred until after the father's manumission. The commentator (the sharh) or subsequent commentators have clarified these positions. The passage also notes that this is analogous to the case of a free man marrying a woman who claims to be free but is actually a slave—in that case, the child is free. Here, the mukatib/slave father 'shares with the free person in the cause of establishing this right' because he was also deceived.

Key Terms
الغرور (al-gharur): Deception, specifically where one party is misled by another's false claim (e.g., a woman claiming to be free).
المغرور (al-maghrur): The person who is deceived; in this context, the mukatib or slave who married a woman believing she was free.
قيمة الأولاد (qimat al-awlad): The monetary value of the children, paid to the mother's master to secure the children's freedom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The child is born from a slave father, so it follows the father.
B) The child is born between two slaves, so it follows the mother in slavery.
C) The child is the product of a valid marriage, so it is free.
D) The child is free because the mother claimed to be free.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the child's status in slavery/freedom primarily follows the mother; deception (gharur) may create an exception requiring compensation, but the majority view maintains the default ruling.

Child of Mukatab from a Woman Claiming Freedom: Intra-Hanafi Ruling
Intra-Hanafi dispute on child status from a deceived marriage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the child of a mukatab from a woman who claimed freedom, and distinguish the ruling of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf from that of Imam Muhammad.

Core Idea
Child of a mukatab from a deceived woman: slave according to majority, free upon payment of value according to Muhammad.

This passage examines a nuanced scenario where a mukatab (or an authorized slave) marries a woman who claims to be free, but she is later discovered to be a slave. The child's status is disputed among the Hanafi imams: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold the child is a slave, while Imam Muhammad argues it is free upon payment of its value. The difference stems from whether the free-man-deceived (gharur) exception applies to a mukatab.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The text covers two cases: a mukatab marrying with permission, and an authorized slave marrying. In both, the woman claims freedom but is later claimed as a slave.
2
State the majority view (Abu Hanifa & Abu Yusuf)
According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, the child is a slave. Their qiyas: the child is born between two slaves (the father is a slave, the mother is a slave), so the child follows the mother in slavery.
The principle: the child follows the mother in slavery/freedom.
3
State Muhammad's view
Imam Muhammad holds the child is free upon payment of its value (qimah). He analogizes to the case of a free man deceived by a slave woman (gharur), where the child is free by consensus.
Muhammad argues the mukatab was deceived, just like the free man.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response to Muhammad
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf respond that the free man case is an exception from qiyas based on consensus, due to the free man's ability to pay immediately. The mukatab lacks that ability, so the exception does not extend.
The dispute is about the scope of an exceptional ruling.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference)
Under the majority view, the child remains the property of the mother's owner. Under Muhammad's view, the child is free but the father must pay its value to the owner.
This affects the child's legal status and financial liability.

Commentary
This passage from the commentary of Imam Babarti on Sharh al-Wiqayah deals with the ruling of a child born to a mukatab or a slave from a woman who claimed to be free but later turned out to be a slave. The core issue is whether the child is free or slave. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold the child is a slave based on qiyas: the child is born between two slaves (father is a slave, mother is discovered to be a slave). Imam Muhammad differs, ruling the child is free upon payment of its value, drawing an analogy to the case of a free man who was deceived (gharur) where the child is free by consensus. Muhammad argues that the mukatab too was deceived by the woman's claim, and the reason for freedom in the free man's case is the deception, which also exists here. However, Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf counter that the free man case is an exception from qiyas based on consensus, and the mukatab's case is not analogous because the mukatab lacks the ability to pay the value immediately, unlike the free man. Therefore, the child remains a slave. The commentary also notes two scenarios: one with a mukatab and one with a slave authorized to marry, and within Muhammad's view, there is a difference in narration regarding the timing of the payment (immediate or deferred after manumission).

Key Terms
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has entered into a contract of manumission (kitabah) with his master, agreeing to pay a sum of money in installments, and upon payment becomes free.
غرور (ghurur): Deception; here, the woman's false claim of freedom that induced the marriage.
قيمة (qimah): The market value of the child, which Muhammad holds must be paid to set the child free.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The child is born between two slaves (the father is a slave and the mother is discovered to be a slave).
C) The free man case is distinguished because the free man can pay immediately.
D) The mukatab lacks permission from his master.

Key Takeaway
The status of a child born to a mukatab from a deceived woman: majority Hanafi ruling is slavery, but Imam Muhammad allows freedom upon payment of value.

Istilad of the Mukatabah and the Dispute over Her Options
Intra-Hanafi dispute on mukatabah's options after istilad

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the options of a mukatabah who becomes umm walad and apply the principle of tajazzu' al-‘itq to determine the payment.

Core Idea
A mukatabah who bears a child becomes umm walad, but her freedom upon the master's death depends on whether emancipation is divisible.

This lesson examines a specific intra-Hanafi dispute: what happens when a mukatabah (a slave under a contract of manumission) bears a child to her master and the master dies with no other property? The ruling hinges on the divisibility of emancipation—a key usul point that differentiates Abu Hanifa from his two companions, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. We will trace the reasoning behind each position and identify the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal scenario
The scenario: a mukatabah bears her master's child (istilad) and continues on kitabah (the contract) until the master dies, leaving no other property. She is now an umm walad. Determine what she owes.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa holds that emancipation is divisible (tajazzu'). At the master's death, one-third of the slave becomes free (due to tadbir implied by umm walad status), leaving two-thirds enslaved. The slave must pay two-thirds of either her value (for tadbir) or the kitabah substitute. She has a genuine choice between immediate payment (tadbir) or deferred payment (kitabah).
The key is divisibility: partial freedom is possible.
3
State the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold emancipation is indivisible. At the master's death, the slave becomes fully free immediately. One-third of the kitabah substitute is forgiven. The slave must pay the lesser of two-thirds of the value or two-thirds of the kitabah substitute. There is no choice because only the lesser amount is due.
The key is indivisibility: full freedom at death, debt remains.
4
Identify the illa behind the ikhtilaf
The dispute revolves around the nature of emancipation: Is it divisible (tajazzu') or not? Abu Hanifa says yes, Sahibayn say no. This usul principle directly shapes the outcome.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Abu Hanifa, the slave has a genuine option that may lead to a higher or lower payment. Under Sahibayn, she must pay the lesser amount, giving her no real choice. The practical difference matters when choosing the payment method.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واستيلاد مكاتبته ، ومَضَتْ عليها ، أو عَجِزَتْ وكانت أم ولد له فإن مضى عليها فمات المولى ولا مال له سواه، فهو بالخيار : إما أن يَسْعَى في ثلثي قيمته ، أو ثلثي بدل الكتابة، وعندهما يسعى في الأقل منهما، فإنّ الإعتاق لما كان متجزئاً عند أبي حنيفة بقي الثلثان عبداً، فإن أدى للتدبير ثلثي القيمة في الحال عتق الكل في الحال، وإن أدَّى للكتابةِ ثُلُثي البدل مؤجلاً عتق مؤجلاً ، فيفيدُ التخيير، وقد تلقى جهتا الحرية ببدلين معجل بالتدبير، ومؤجل بالكتابة ، فيتخيرُ بينهما، وعندهما : لَمَّا لم يكن متجزئاً صارَ بموتِ المولى معتق الكل، وقد سقط عنه ثلث المال، وبقي الثلثان، وكلُّ ما هو أقل من ثلثي البدل ، أو ثلثي القيمة يسعى فيه ولا فائدة في التخيير بين الأقل والأكثر .
And the istilad of his mukatabah, and she continues upon it, or she becomes incapacitated while she is umm walad for him, then if she continues upon it and the master dies with no other property, then he (the mukatabah) is at the option: either to pay two-thirds of his value, or two-thirds of the kitabah substitute. And according to them (the two, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), he pays the lesser of the two. For emancipation, since it was divisible according to Abu Hanifa, two-thirds remain slave; then if he pays for tadbir two-thirds of the value immediately, he becomes free entirely immediately, and if he pays for kitabah two-thirds of the substitute deferred, he becomes free deferred. So it yields option. And he has received two directions of freedom with two substitutes: immediate by tadbir, and deferred by kitabah, so he chooses between them. And according to them (the two), since it was not divisible, by the death of the master he becomes entirely free, and one-third of the property is forgiven, and two-thirds remain, and whatever is less than two-thirds of the substitute or two-thirds of the value he pays, and there is no benefit in choosing between the lesser and the greater.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the option of a mukatabah who bears a child to her master and becomes umm walad. The ruling states that if she continues on kitabah and the master dies with no other property, then she has the option: she can either pay two-thirds of her value (as a mudabbar) or two-thirds of the kitabah substitute. According to Abu Hanifa, emancipation is divisible (tajazzu'), so when the master dies, she is partially free: one-third is freed due to the tadbir implied by being umm walad, and two-thirds remain slave. Thus she must pay two-thirds of the value or substitute. She can choose to pay immediately (for tadbir) or defer (for kitabah). According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Al-Shaybani), emancipation is not divisible; at the master's death she is entirely free, and one-third of the substitute is forgiven. She must then pay the lesser of two-thirds of the substitute or value, and there is no real choice since paying the lesser is the only option.

Key Terms
استيلاد (istilad): Bearing a child to the master, which makes the slave woman an umm walad.
أم ولد (umm walad): A slave woman who has borne a child to her master; she is entitled to freedom upon her master's death.
تجزؤ (tajazzu'): The divisibility of emancipation; the view that freedom can be partial, as held by Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The dispute over a mukatabah's options after istilad hinges on whether emancipation is divisible, leading to a genuine choice (Abu Hanifa) or a fixed lesser payment (Sahibayn).

Ruling on the Kitabah of a Mother and Her Children
Commentary on the ruling that mother's kitabah includes children as dependents

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a mother's kitabah can include her children as dependents and explain that payment by one child results in freedom of all.

Core Idea
A mother's kitabah contract validly includes children as dependents; payment by one child frees all.

This lesson examines a nuanced ruling in Hanafi fiqh regarding the kitabah (contract of manumission) of a mother and her children. The commentarial passage from al-Hidayah clarifies that children may be included as dependents (tabi‘) in the mother's kitabah, and that payment by any one of them releases all. Understanding this dependency principle is essential for correctly applying the contract's effects.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the mother entering a kitabah contract that includes her children as dependents. According to the passage, this contract is valid (sahih).
2
Understand the dependency principle
The mother is the principal (asl) and her children are dependents (tabi‘). The contract is even more valid for her own children than for a stranger because they are closer.
This is based on the first issue (al-mas'alah al-ula) of a slave writing kitabah for himself and an absent slave.
3
Analyze the effect of payment
If one child pays the full kitabah amount, all children are freed. The paying child's payment is equivalent to the mother's payment because he is entirely dependent on her.
The commentary explains that the child is 'dependent on her in every respect' (tabi‘ laha min kulli wajh).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لم يرجع وعتقوا) كما في مسألة الأولى.
قوله: وعتقوا; لأنها جعلت نفسها أصلاً في الكتابة، وأولادها تبعاً على ما بينا في المسألة الأولى، وهي أولى بذلك من الأجنبي، يعني أن هذا العقد على هذا الوجه يجوز في حق الأجنبي ففي حق ولدها أولى; لأن ولدها أقرب إليها من الأجنبي. كذا في الهداية وشروحه.
قوله: كما في المسألة الأولى; وهي كتابة العبد الحاضر عن نفسه وعن العبد الغائب.
فإن قلت: إذا أدى أحدهما ينبغي أن لا يعتق الابن الآخر; لأنه لا أصالة بينهما ولا تبعية.
قلت: إن أحدهما إذا أدى كان أداؤه كأداء الأم; لأنه تابع لها من كل وجه، ولو أدت الأم عتقوا، فكذا إذا أدى أحدهما. كذا في شرح العيني.
And they are freed, as in the first issue. He said: 'And they are freed' because she made herself the principal in the kitabah and her children dependents, as we clarified in the first issue. And she is more entitled to that than a stranger, meaning that this contract in this manner is valid regarding a stranger, so regarding her child it is more appropriate because her child is closer to her than a stranger. Thus in al-Hidayah and its commentaries. He said: 'As in the first issue' which is the kitabah of a present slave for himself and for the absent slave. If it is said: If one of them pays, it should be that the other son is not freed because there is no principality nor dependency between them. I say: If one of them pays, his payment is like the mother's payment because he is dependent on her in every respect. If the mother paid, they would be freed, so likewise if one of them pays. Thus in Sharh al-Ayni.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This is a commentary on a matn regarding the kitabah of a mother and her children. The author explains that when a mother includes her children in the kitabah contract, she is the principal (asl) and they are dependents (tabi'). This arrangement is valid even for a stranger, so it is even more valid for her own children. The commentary then addresses an objection: if one child pays the kitabah, why should the other children be freed? The answer is that the paying child is entirely dependent on the mother, so his payment is equivalent to her payment. Thus, all children become free upon payment by one.

Key Terms
كتابة (kitabah): A contract of manumission where a slave agrees to pay the master a sum for freedom.
تبع (tabi'): A dependent who is included in the principal's contract and is freed by the principal's payment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) The payment is invalid and no one is freed.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a mother's kitabah contract validly includes her children as dependents; payment by any one child frees all because the child is completely dependent on the mother.

Ruling on a Mukataba with Children Claimed by Two Partners
Analyzing the case of a joint-ownership mukataba with paternity claims and subsequent incapacity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a joint mukataba's istilad and paternity claims, explain the intra-Hanafi disagreement regarding divisibility, and calculate the financial liabilities arising from the subsequent incapacity.

Core Idea
A joint mukataba's istilad is divisible per Abu Hanifa; after incapacity, the entire slave reverts to being umm walad for the earlier claimant.

This lesson examines a nuanced case from the Kitab al-Mukatab concerning a female slave jointly owned by two partners, each claiming paternity of her children. The ruling, according to Imam Abu Hanifa, hinges on the divisibility of the istilad, contrasting with the view of his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. By studying this case, you will understand how the kitaba contract interacts with umm walad status and the financial consequences when the slave becomes unable to pay.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and sequence of events
Note that a mukataba (female slave under kitaba) is owned jointly by two partners. She gives birth to two children at different times, each claimed by a different partner. Subsequently, she becomes unable to pay her installments ('ajaz).
The order of claims and the timing of 'ajaz are critical to the ruling.
2
Determine the effect of each partner's claim before 'ajaz
According to Abu Hanifa, the first claim makes the claiming partner's share of the mukataba an umm walad. The second claim also validly makes the other partner's share an umm walad. This is because istilad is divisible in a joint mukataba, analogous to a joint mudabbara.
The Sahibayn disagree, holding the kitaba indivisible, so the entire slave would become umm walad for the first claimant immediately.
3
Understand the impact of incapacity ('ajaz)
Upon 'ajaz, the kitaba is retroactively voided. This removes the impediment to full ownership transfer, revealing that the entire slave is umm walad for the first partner because his intercourse was earlier and now the entire slave vests in him.
Under the Sahibayn's view, the outcome might differ because the second claim could be invalidated, but the text explains Abu Hanifa's ruling.
4
Calculate financial liabilities
The first partner owes half the slave's value and half the 'aqr (compensation for intercourse) to his partner, because he acquires his partner's share after 'ajaz. The second partner owes the full 'aqr (since intercourse was with another's umm walad) and the value of his child (which is free but must be compensated).
The second partner is treated as a deceived party (maghrur) regarding the child, so the child is free and valued.
5
Contrast with the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold the kitaba indivisible, so permission for one partner to receive installments is permission for the whole, and the kitaba cannot be split. This would affect the permissibility of the second claim and the distribution after 'ajaz.
The later relied-upon fatwa adopts the Sahibayn's view, but the text explains Abu Hanifa's ruling for completeness.

مكاتبة لرجلين جاءت بولد فادعاه أحدهما ، ثم جاءت بآخر فادعاه الآخر، فعجزت

أنه إذا لم يأذن فله حق الفسخ، فبالإذن لا يبقى ذلك ، وإذنه لشريكه بالقبض

[٢] إذن للعبد بالأداء إليه ، فيكون متبرّعاً" في نصيبه على القابض، فيكون له ، وعندهما: الكتابة غير متجزئة، فالإذن بكتابة نصيبه إذن بكتابة الكل، فالقابض أصيل في البعض، ووكيل في البعض، والمقبوض مشترك بينهما، فبقي كذلك بعد العجز.

حسن الدراية لأواخر شرح الوقاية

فهي أم ولد للأول وضمن نصف قيمتها، ونصف عقرها، وشريكه عقرها، وقيمة الولد وهو ابنه

[١] قوله : فهي أم ولد للأوّل ؛ لأنه لَمَّا ادّعى أحدهما الولد صحت دعوته لقيام الملك له فيها، وصار نصيبه أم ولد له ؛ لأنَّ المكاتبة لا تقبل النقل من ملك إلى ملك، فتقتصر أمومية الولد على نصيبه كما في المديرة المشتركة.

ولو ادعى الثاني ولدها الآخر صحت دعوته ؛ لقيام ملكه ظاهراً، ثم إذا عجزت بعد ذلك جعلت الكتابة كأن لم تكن، وتبين أن الجارية كلّها أم ولد للأول ؛ لأنه زال المانع من الانتقال، ووطؤه سابق. كذا في «الهداية»(۱).

[٢] قوله : نصف قيمتها ؛ لأنه تملك نصيبه لما استكمل الاستيلاد، ونصف عقرها لوطئه جارية مشتركة (٢).

[٣]قوله : وشريكه...الخ ؛ أي ضمن شريكه عقرها ؛ أي كمال عقرها، وقيمة الولد وهو ابنه بمنزلة المغرور ؛ لأنه حين وطئها كان ملكه قائماً ظاهراً، وولد المغرور ثابت بالنسب منه ، حر بالقيمة على ما عرف، لكنّه وطء أم ولد الغير حقيقةً فيلزمُهُ كمال العقر. كذا في «الهداية»(۳). [٤]قوله : فيقتصر على نصيبه ؛ فبقي نصيب الآخر مكاتباً على حاله.
Chapter of the Mukatab: His Transactions, Incapacity, and Rulings. A mukataba belonging to two men gave birth to a child, and one of them claimed it; then she gave birth to another, and the other claimed it; then she became unable (to pay). [Discussion of permission:] That if he did not give permission, he has the right of cancellation; with permission, that right does not remain, and his permission to his partner for receiving (payment) is permission for the slave to pay to him, so he becomes a donor regarding his share to the receiver; thus it belongs to him. According to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): the kitaba is indivisible, so permission for his share is permission for the whole; the receiver is principal in part and agent in part, and what is received is shared between them, and remains so after incapacity. (Commentary: The case of the mukataba with two children.) She is an umm walad for the first, and he is liable for half her value and half her 'aqr, and his partner (is liable for) her 'aqr and the value of the child, which is his son. This is according to Abu Hanifa. Its explanation: The istilad of a joint mukataba is divisible according to Abu Hanifa, so it is confined to his share; because the mukatab does not transfer from ownership to ownership as previously mentioned regarding the mudabbar, and the istilad of a female slave (qinn) is not divisible; so if one of the two partners has istilad of a joint female slave, she becomes entirely an umm walad for him, and he is liable for half her value to the partner. If you know this: then the second's istilad before incapacity occurred. [Commentary on: 'She is umm walad for the first'] because when the first claimed the child, his claim is valid due to his ownership of her, and his share becomes umm walad for him; because the mukataba does not accept transfer from ownership to ownership, so umm walad status is confined to his share as in the joint mudabbara. If the second claimed the other child, his claim is valid due to apparent ownership. Then when she becomes incapable after that, the kitaba is considered as if it never existed, and it becomes clear that the entire slave girl is umm walad for the first, because the impediment to transfer is removed and his intercourse was earlier. [Commentary on half value] because he acquired ownership of his share upon completion of istilad, and half the 'aqr for his intercourse with a joint slave girl. [Commentary on 'and his partner...'] meaning his partner is liable for her 'aqr, i.e., the full 'aqr, and the value of the child, which is his son, as a deceived party (maghrur); because when he had intercourse, his ownership was apparent, and the child of the deceived party is established as his lineage, free for a value as known, but it was actually intercourse with another's umm walad, so the full 'aqr is incumbent upon him. [Commentary on 'confined to his share'] so the other's share remains as a mukatab as it was.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the book of Mukatab. It discusses a case where a female slave under a kitaba contract (mukataba) is owned jointly by two partners. She gives birth to two children, each claimed by one partner. Then she becomes unable to pay the kitaba installments ('ajazat). The ruling is presented according to Imam Abu Hanifa, with an explanation of the underlying principles. The key principle is that the istilad (causing the birth of a child by the master) of a joint mukataba is divisible according to Abu Hanifa, meaning it only affects the share of the partner who does it. In contrast, the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the kitaba is indivisible. This difference leads to different rulings. In this case, the first partner claims the first child, making his share of the slave an umm walad (mother of his child). The second partner claims the second child, also valid, making his share an umm walad as well. However, after the slave becomes incapable, the kitaba is voided retroactively, and it becomes clear that the entire slave is umm walad for the first partner because his intercourse was earlier. Consequently, the first partner must pay half the value of the slave and half the 'aqr (compensation for intercourse) to his partner. The second partner must pay the full 'aqr for his intercourse (since he actually had intercourse with another's umm walad) and the value of his own child (which is free but valued). The text also includes a discussion about permission (idhn) for one partner to receive the kitaba payment, highlighting the difference between Abu Hanifa and the two disciples on the divisibility of the kitaba. The relied-upon position for fatwa is likely the view of the disciples, as it is more cautious, but the text presents Abu Hanifa's view as the basis of the ruling. The commentary from 'Al-Hidayah' and other sources is cited to elaborate the reasoning.

Key Terms
مكاتبة (mukātaba): A female slave who has entered into a kitaba contract (mukataba) to purchase her freedom by paying installments.
استيلاد (istīlād): The act of a master causing his female slave to bear his child, which gives her the status of umm walad.
أم ولد (umm walad): A female slave who has borne a child to her master, thereby becoming free upon the master's death (with restrictions).
عقر (ʿaqr): Compensation paid by a man who has intercourse with a female slave owned by another, equivalent to her value or a fixed amount.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case of a mukataba owned by two partners who each claim a child born to her, what happens after she becomes incapable ('ajaz) according to Abu Hanifa?
A) The entire slave becomes umm walad for the first partner who claimed the first child.
B) The slave remains a mukataba and each partner has a share.
C) The slave is divided equally between the two partners as umm walad.
D) The slave is freed and the children are free.

Key Takeaway
After incapacity of a joint mukataba, the entire slave becomes umm walad for the first claimant due to retroactive voiding of the kitaba.

Guarantee for a Mukataba After Intercourse by a Partner
Explains rulings when a buyer has intercourse with a mukataba and subsequent tadbir by second owner

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding the guarantee amount for a mukataba after intercourse by a partner, and explain why the tadbir by the second owner is nullified.

Core Idea
When a second man has intercourse with a mukataba, the first owner guarantees half her value or the lesser amount; any tadbir by the second is void.

This lesson examines a complex scenario from al-Hidayah involving a mukataba (a slave with a kitaba contract) who is jointly owned. The second owner has intercourse with her, triggering financial and status consequences. We will analyze the two main opinions within the Hanafi school from Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on the guarantee amount, and then explore why any tadbir (declaration of freedom upon death) made by the second owner is invalid if she later becomes unable to pay the kitaba.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their relationship
Determine who are the first and second owners, the status of the slave (mukataba), and the sequence of events (intercourse or tadbir by the second owner, then inability).
The first owner originally owned her as a mukataba and later her share became umm walad.
2
Analyse the financial guarantee
Focus on the two opinions: Abu Yusuf insists on half her value as a mukataba; Muhammad says the lesser of that and half the remaining kitaba payment. Understand the reasoning behind each.
Abu Yusuf sees the partner's right in the person; Muhammad sees uncertainty between person and payment.
3
Examine the nullification of tadbir
Consider the case where the second owner gave tadbir but she becomes unable to pay. Understand why his tadbir is void: it did not encounter ownership because the first owner's rights retroactively take priority.
Abu Hanifa's view that ownership from the time of intercourse becomes clear upon inability.
4
Conclude the final status of the slave
After the guarantee and nullification, the slave is entirely umm walad for the first owner. Identify the practical outcome.
She is fully owned by the first owner and free upon his death.

Then the second man's intercourse occurs in other than his ownership, so upon him is the full compensation (al-'aqr) but not the hadd penalty due to the doubt (shubha), and his child is not free by value (bi'l-qeema), and the first man guarantees to the partner half of her value as a mukataba according to Abu Yusuf, and the lesser of half her value and half of what remains upon her from the kitaba payment according to Muhammad. And if the kitaba is annulled in the partner's share according to both of them before inability, then she is entirely a mukataba for the first man with half the kitaba payment according to Shaykh Abu Mansur al-Makatib? because allowing it would invalidate the kitaba since the buyer would not consent to her remaining a mukataba. So in al-Hidayah. His statement: 'Then the second man's intercourse is in other than his ownership' means when she becomes entirely an umm walad for the first, then the second is having intercourse with another's umm walad, so lineage of the child is not established from him, nor is the child free upon him by value, but the hadd is not obligatory upon him due to shubha, and he is obligated for the full 'aqr because intercourse is never free from one of two penalties: hadd or 'aqr. So in al-Hidayah. His statement: 'And the first man guarantees to the partner half her value as a mukataba according to Abu Yusuf' because she is a mukataba, so he guarantees it whether he is wealthy or poor because it is a guarantee of ownership; he owned the share of his partner, and it is because of the manifestation of effects of ownership in it such as permissibility of intercourse, use, etc. So in al-Kifayah. His statement: 'And the lesser of half her value and half of what remains of the kitaba payment according to Muhammad' because his partner's right is in half the slave's person on the consideration of inability, and in half the payment on the consideration of performance, so due to the fluctuation between the two, the lesser of them is obligatory because the lesser is certain. So in some marginal notes of al-Hidayah. His statement: 'With half the kitaba payment' because the kitaba is annulled in a way that does not harm the mukataba, and she is not harmed by the dropping of half the payment. So in al-Hidayah. 'And if he pays the 'aqr to her, it is valid' i.e. before inability because she is entitled to her benefits and their compensations. Then if the second does not have intercourse and gives tadbir and she becomes unable, his tadbir is nullified, and she is an umm walad for the first. His statement: 'And with the full kitaba payment' meaning according to the general mashayikh: she is entirely a mukataba for the first with the full kitaba payment because the kitaba did not annul except in the right of ownership by necessity, so it does not appear in the right of dropping half the payment, and keeping it in his right is consideration for the master, even if the mukataba is not harmed by its dropping. And the mukataba is the one who gives the 'aqr because she is entitled to the compensations of her benefits. And if she becomes unable and returns to slavery, it is returned to the master because his entitlement becomes apparent as we explained earlier. So in al-Hidayah. His statement: 'Before inability...etc.' if she becomes unable, she returns the 'aqr to the master because his entitlement to her becomes apparent. So in al-Zayla'i. His statement: 'His tadbir is nullified' because it did not encounter ownership. As for according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it is apparent because the mustawlidah (the one who has become umm walad) owned her before inability, so if the kitaba was rescinded before the tadbir, his tadbir is not valid. And as for according to Abu Hanifa, because by inability it becomes clear that he owned his share from the time of intercourse, so it becomes clear that it encountered the ownership of another. And tadbir depends on ownership, unlike lineage because it depends on deception (ghurur) as passed. So in al-Hidayah. His statement: 'And she' meaning the slave-girl whom the second gave tadbir after the first's istilad, then she became unable. His statement: 'She is an umm walad for the first' because he owned his partner's share and the istilad is completed as we explained.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Hidayah deals with a complex case involving a mukataba (a slave woman who has contracted kitaba, i.e., written agreement for manumission upon payment) who is purchased by a second person. The key scenario: the first owner (who originally owned her as a mukataba) has intercourse with her, making her an umm walad (mother of his child) entirely after the second owner's intercourse or tadbir. The passage first addresses the case where the second owner had intercourse with her. Since she becomes umm walad for the first owner retroactively (from the time of intercourse), the second owner's intercourse is considered to have occurred in the property of another. Therefore, he does not receive the hadd penalty due to shubha (doubt), but he must pay the full 'aqr (compensation for loss of virginity or similar). The child from this intercourse is not attributed to him and does not become free by value. Next, the first owner must compensate his partner (the second owner) for his share. According to Abu Yusuf, the first owner pays half her value as a mukataba (i.e., the value at the time she was still a mukataba), regardless of whether the first owner is wealthy or poor, because this is a guarantee for ownership. According to Muhammad, he pays the lesser of half her value and half of the remaining kitaba payment. The reasoning is that the partner's right is uncertain: if she becomes unable to pay, his right is in her person; if she pays, his right is in the payment. The lesser amount is certain and therefore obligatory. After the first owner's payment, the kitaba is annulled in the partner's share, and she becomes entirely a mukataba for the first owner with either half or full kitaba payment depending on the view. The passage then discusses a different scenario: the second owner did not have intercourse but instead gave tadbir (a declaration that she will be free upon his death). If she later becomes unable to pay the kitaba, the tadbir is nullified because it did not properly encounter his ownership. According to Abu Hanifa, it becomes clear that the first owner owned her from the time of intercourse, so the tadbir was on someone else's property. However, lineage (of the child) is different because it depends on deception (ghurur). The final ruling: she is an umm walad for the first owner because he has fully acquired his partner's share, completing the istilad (making her umm walad).

Key Terms
عقر ('aqr): Compensation paid for intercourse with a female slave; here it is the full amount due from the second man because his intercourse was in property of another.
مكاتبة (mukataba): A female slave who has made a kitaba contract with her master to work and pay for her freedom.
تدبير (tadbir): A declaration by a master that a slave will be free upon the master's death; here it is nullified if the slave becomes unable to pay kitaba.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on the tadbir given by the second owner of a mukataba who later becomes unable to pay the kitaba?
A) The tadbir is valid and she becomes free upon his death.
B) The tadbir is nullified because it did not encounter proper ownership.
C) The tadbir is suspended until she pays the kitaba.
D) The tadbir is valid only if the first owner consents.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when a second owner has intercourse with a mukataba, the first owner must guarantee the partner's share (disputed amount), and any subsequent tadbir by the second owner is nullified upon the slave's inability to pay the kitaba.

Validity of Nikah, Talaq, and Itq under Coercion
Stating Hanafi ruling and Shafi'i opposition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on marriage, divorce, and manumission under coercion and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using qiyas to hazl.

Core Idea
Coercion does not invalidate marriage, divorce, or manumission in the Hanafi school.

This lesson examines a key Hanafi position: that certain binding utterances (marriage, divorce, manumission) remain valid even when uttered under coercion. The Hanafi school draws an analogy to the validity of these contracts when made in jest (hazl). We will explore the reasoning, the Shafi'i objection, and the practical consequences of this ikhtilaf.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify clearly: marriage, divorce, and manumission are valid and effective despite coercion. The hukm is sahih (valid) and enforceable.
The coerced person may be sinful if he could have resisted, but the worldly ruling stands.
2
Identify the Hanafi dalil
The proof is qiyas (analogy) to the validity of these contracts when done in jest (hazl). Just as hazl does not nullify them, coercion also does not nullify them.
Hanafi usul holds that the utterance itself is the operative cause (sabab) for these contracts.
3
Present the Shafi'i position and its dalil
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that all transactions of a coerced person are invalid because coercion negates consent (rida), and consent is a condition for validity.
Exception: legitimate coercion (e.g., by a judge) is recognized by Shafi'is.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school distinguishes: in marriage, divorce, and manumission, the utterance itself creates the legal effect; coercion affects the sin (ithm) but not the worldly hukm.
This is based on the principle that these are binding utterances that cannot be retracted.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: under Hanafi, a coerced divorce takes effect; under Shafi'i, it has no effect. The wife is divorced in Hanafi but not in Shafi'i.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(وصح نكاحه وطلاقه وعتقه): أي إعتاقه، فإن هذه العقود تصح عندنا مع وجود الإكراه؛ قياساً على صحتها مع الهزل، وعند الشافعي لا تصح عليه وهو الأثم، بخلاف الإكراه على إتلاف مال الغير؛ لأنه سقط حكمه وهو الأثم، فأضيف إلى غيره. [١] قوله: للشبهة؛ يعني إن القتل الحاصل من المكره بقي مقصوراً عليه من وجه نظراً إلى التأثيم، فإن الإثم عليه، وتفصيله مر آنفا في دليل زفر، وأضيف إلى المكرِه بالكسر من وجه نظراً إلى الحمل، فإنه حمل على القتل، فدخلت الشبهة في كل جانب، والقصاص يدفع بالشبهة. [٢] قوله: والتسبيب عنده كالمباشرة؛ يعني التسبيب في هذا القتل حكم المباشرة، فإن السبب التام ينزل منزلة المباشرة في حق وجوب القصاص عنده. كذا في «التبيين». [٣] قوله: كشهود القصاص؛ فإن الشاهدين لو شهدا على رجل بالقتل العمد، فاقتص المشهود عليه، ثم جاء المشهود به حياً يقتل الشاهدان عنده. كذا في «الكفاية». (٤) قوله: وصح نكاحه... الخ؛ يعني لو أكره على نكاح امرأة أو على طلاق امرأته أو عتق عبده ففعل، صح نكاحه وطلاقه وإعتاقه عندنا، قياساً على صحة هذه العقود مع الهزل. [٠] قوله: وعند الشافعي لا تصح؛ لأن تصرفات المكره كلها باطلة، إلا أن يكون إكراها بحق، وقد مر في «كتاب الطلاق».
(And his marriage, divorce, and manumission are valid): i.e., his freeing of a slave. For these contracts are valid in our school despite the presence of coercion, by analogy to their validity with jest. According to al-Shafi'i, they are not valid, and sin is upon him, unlike coercion to destroy the property of another, because its ruling (sin) falls and is attributed to another. [1] His statement: 'due to ambiguity'; meaning the killing resulting from the coerced remained confined to him from one perspective regarding sin, for sin is upon him, and its detail has passed in Zufar's proof, and it is attributed to the coercer from another perspective regarding compulsion, for he compelled the killing, so ambiguity entered on both sides, and qisas is repelled by ambiguity. [2] His statement: 'and causation with him is like direct action'; meaning causation in this killing has the same ruling as direct action, for a complete cause is treated as direct action regarding the obligation of qisas with him. Thus in al-Tabyin. [3] His statement: 'like the witnesses of qisas'; for if two witnesses testify against a man for intentional killing, and the accused is executed, then the person testified to appears alive, the two witnesses are executed in his school. Thus in al-Kifaya. (4) His statement: 'and his marriage is valid... etc'; meaning if he is coerced to marry a woman, or to divorce his wife, or to free his slave, and he does it, his marriage, divorce, and manumission are valid in our school, by analogy to the validity of these contracts with jest. [0] His statement: 'and according to al-Shafi'i they are not valid'; because all transactions of a coerced person are invalid, unless the coercion is legitimate, and this has passed in 'The Book of Divorce'.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi ruling on the validity of marriage (nikah), divorce (talaq), and manumission (itq) when performed under coercion. The Hanafi position is that these contracts are valid despite coercion, based on qiyas (analogy) to their validity when done in jest (hazl). The underlying principle is that these are binding utterances that cannot be retracted; coercion does not negate their effect in this world, though the coerced person is sinful if he could have endured the coercion. The commentator contrasts this with cases of coercion to destroy another's property, where the sin falls on the coercer. He then notes that al-Shafi'i holds such contracts invalid under coercion because coercion negates consent, which is essential for validity. The Hanafi school relies on the principle that the utterance itself is effective; the presence of coercion does not affect the legal consequence, similar to jest. The passage also includes additional explanations about qisas that seem to be cross-referenced from the previous discussion, but the main focus is the validity of the three contracts.

Key Terms
الهزل (al-hazl): Jest or joking; uttering a statement without serious intent.
العتق (al-itq): Manumission of a slave, setting free.
الإكراه (al-ikrah): Coercion; compelling someone to act against their will through threat of harm.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on a divorce pronounced under coercion?
A) The divorce is invalid
B) The divorce is valid and takes effect
C) The divorce is valid only if the coercion was legitimate
D) The divorce is suspended until the coercion ends

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, marriage, divorce, and manumission are valid even under coercion because the utterance itself is the effective cause, unlike acts requiring consent.

Contracts under Duress: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Ruling that contracts are valid under duress by qiyas to jest

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to STATE the Hanafi ruling on contracts under duress, EXPLAIN the Hanafi qiyas to jest, and DISTINGUISH it from the Shafi'i position, including the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
A contract made under duress is valid but sinful.

This lesson examines a classic inter-madhhab disagreement: what happens to a marriage, divorce, or manumission performed under duress? The Hanafi school rules them valid by analogy to contracts made in jest, while the Shafi'i school invalidates all coerced acts. The dispute hinges on whether the act is purely verbal and whether the sin can be separated from legal effect. By the end, you will be able to articulate each position's proof and the practical consequence.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: marriage, divorce, and manumission are valid (sahih) but the coerced person is sinful (athim).
The text states: 'Wa sahha nikāhuhu wa talāquhu wa ‘itquh' — his marriage, divorce, and manumission are valid.
2
Identify the Hanafi proof: qiyas to jest
The text says 'qiyāsan ‘alā sihhatihā ma‘a al-hazl'. Explain the analogy: just as a contract made in jest (hazl) is valid despite lack of serious intent, so too a contract under duress is valid despite lack of free consent.
The common element (illah) is that both involve a verbal act without true volition.
3
State the Shafi'i position and its proof
Al-Shafi'i holds that all actions of a coerced person are invalid unless duress is with right. The proof is based on the principle that duress vitiates consent entirely.
The text: 'Wa ‘inda al-Shāfi‘ī lā tasihh; li’anna tasarrufāt al-mukrahi kulluhā bātilah'.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response distinguishes contracts (purely verbal) from property destruction. For verbal contracts, the sin (ithm) falls on the coerced person but the act remains legally valid because it is a pure utterance (qawl) that cannot be transferred to the coercer.
Contrast with property destruction: the sin is attributed to the coercer, not the coerced person.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: For Hanafis, the contract takes effect (e.g., the divorce is final, the slave is free). For Shafi'is, the contract is void and produces no legal effects.
This difference has major implications in family law and property.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصح نكاحه وطلاقه وعتقه. أي إعتاقه، فإن هذه العقود تصح عندنا مع وجود الإكراه؛ قياساً على صحتها مع الهزل، وعند الشافعي لا تصح عليه وهو الأثم، بخلاف الإكراه على إتلاف مال الغير؛ لأنه سقط حكمه وهو الأثم، فأضيف إلى غيره. قوله: وصح نكاحه... الخ؛ يعني لو أكره على نكاح امرأة أو على طلاق امرأته أو عتق عبده ففعل، صح نكاحه وطلاقه وإعتاقه عندنا، قياساً على صحة هذه العقود مع الهزل. قوله: وعند الشافعي لا تصح؛ لأن تصرفات المكره كلها باطلة، إلا أن يكون إكراها بحق.
And his marriage, divorce, and manumission are valid. That is, his freeing of a slave. For these contracts are valid according to us with the presence of duress, by analogy to their validity with jest. According to al-Shafi'i, they are not valid, and he is sinful. Unlike duress regarding destroying another's property, because its ruling (sin) has fallen, so it is attributed to another. He said: 'and his marriage... etc.' meaning if he is coerced into marrying a woman, or divorcing his wife, or freeing his slave, and he does so, his marriage, divorce, and manumission are valid according to us, by analogy to the validity of these contracts with jest. He said: according to al-Shafi'i, they are not valid, because the actions of a coerced person are all invalid, unless the duress is with right.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute concerning the validity of marriage, divorce, and manumission when performed under duress. The Hanafi position, based on qiyas to the validity of these contracts when made in jest (hazl), holds that they are valid, although the coerced person is sinful (athim). The Shafi'i position invalidates all actions under duress unless the duress is legitimate (e.g., by a rightful authority). The Hanafi reasoning distinguishes between mere speech (qawl) and actual destruction (itlaf); in contracts like marriage and divorce, the act is purely verbal, which cannot be transferred to the coercer, so it remains attributed to the coerced person despite his sin. The commentary (sharh) expands on this by providing the example of coercion into marriage, divorce, or manumission.

Key Terms
إكراه (ikrāh): Duress; compelling someone to act against their will by threat of harm.
الهزل (al-hazl): Jest or joking; a contract made without serious intent.
آثم (āthim): Sinful; one who commits a sin by performing a prohibited act under duress.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi ruling on the validity of a marriage contract performed under duress?
A) It is completely invalid because duress voids consent.
B) It is valid but the person is sinful.
C) It is valid only if the duress is from a rightful authority.
D) It is valid and the person is not sinful.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school validates contracts under duress by analogy to jest, upholding legal effect while holding the coerced person sinful — a key difference from the Shafi'i position.
