Coerced Ila', Fay', and Islam: Validity and Consequences
Specific rulings on ila', fay', and Islam under coercion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on coerced ila', fay', and Islam, and explain why apostasy after coerced Islam does not warrant execution.

Core Idea
Coerced vows and conversions are valid, but doubt (shubha) in coerced Islam prevents execution for apostasy.

This passage from al-'Inayah addresses the validity of three actions performed under coercion: ila' (vow to abstain), fay' (return), and conversion to Islam. The Hanafi school holds these actions valid despite duress. However, if a coerced Muslim later apostatizes, he is not executed because of the doubt surrounding the sincerity of his initial acceptance. The principle of shubha (doubt) averts hadd punishments, here the death penalty for apostasy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three acts under coercion
The passage rules on ila', fay', and Islam. Ila' is a sworn vow to abstain from marital relations; fay' is the husband's return to normal relations; Islam is the declaration of faith. All occur under coercion.
These are distinct acts with different legal consequences.
2
Understand why coercion does not invalidate
Ila' is both an oath (immediate) and a potential divorce (future). Coercion does not invalidate oaths or divorces. Fay' is a verbal act ('I have returned'), and speech under coercion is valid. Islam is a verbal declaration; the hadith about fighting until people say 'La ilaha illa Allah' implies acceptance of coerced utterances.
The Hanafi view prioritizes the external act over internal coercion for these matters.
3
Apply financial consequences
If the husband does not perform fay' within four months, the wife may seek divorce. If she leaves without consummation, half the mahr is due. The husband cannot recover this from the coercer because he had the opportunity to approach but didn't. If he did approach and expiate, he also cannot recover because he acted contrary to the coercion.
The inability to recover emphasizes personal responsibility despite coercion.
4
Analyze the apostasy exception
A coerced convert who later apostatizes is not executed because of shubha (doubt). The second could be that the initial Islam was invalid, so his original disbelief remained. Doubt averts hadd punishments, including execution for apostasy.
This is a mercy from shubha, not a license to apostatize.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإيلاؤه، وفيؤهُ فيه، وإسلامه بلا قتل لو رجع

[١] قوله : وإيلاؤه وفيؤه فيه ; لأنَّ الإيلاء يمين في الحال، وطلاق في المآل، كما صرح به في بابه، والإكراه لا يمنع كلّ واحد منهما، والفيء الرجوع. والمراد هاهنا أن يقول المولى رجعتُ فيه : أي في زمان الإيلاء، ولأن ترك الفيء إليها أربعة أشهر حتى بانت ولم يكن دخل بها وجب نصف المهر، ولا يرجع به على المكره ; ; لأنه كان متمكناً من القربان في المدة، فإذا لم يفعل كان ذلك رضاً منه بما لزمه من الصداق، وإن قربها وكفَّر لم يرجع على المكره بشيء; لأنه أتى بضد ما أكرهه عليه. كذا في «العناية».

[٢] قوله : لتمكن الشبهة في إسلامه; أي لاحتمال عدم الإسلام من الابتداء، فيكون كفره أصيلاً ; لعدم صحة إسلامه، والشبهة دارئة للقتل.
And his ila', and his fay' therein, and his Islam, without killing if he returns... [1] His statement: 'and his ila' and his fay' therein' because ila' is an oath immediately and divorce eventually, as clarified in its chapter, and coercion does not prevent either of them. Fay' means return. Here it means the husband says 'I have returned' i.e., to the time of ila'. And because if he leaves fay' for four months until she becomes separated and he had not consummated, half the mahr becomes obligatory, and he does not recover it from the coercer because he was able to approach her during the period; if he did not do so, that was consent from him to what became binding of the dowry. And if he approached her and made expiation, he does not recover anything from the coercer because he did the opposite of what he was coerced to do. Thus in al-'Inayah. [2] His statement: 'due to the presence of doubt in his Islam' meaning due to the possibility that he was not Muslim from the beginning, so his disbelief is original, because his Islam was not valid, and doubt prevents killing.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage covers three specific rulings under coercion. First, ila' (a vow to abstain from marital relations) and fay' (return to marital relations) are valid under coercion because ila' combines aspects of an oath and divorce, both of which are valid under coercion. If the husband does not return within four months, divorce occurs, and if he had not consummated, half the mahr is due; he cannot recover from the coercer because he had the option to approach. If he does approach and expiate, he also cannot recover because he acted contrary to what he was coerced to do. Second, Islam is valid under coercion, as proven by the hadith 'I have been commanded to fight people until they say La ilaha illa Allah.' However, if a coerced Muslim later apostatizes, he is not killed because of the doubt (shubha) that his initial Islam was invalid, so his apostasy is considered original disbelief, and doubt averts the hadd punishment.

Key Terms
الإيلاء (al-eela'): A sworn vow by a husband to abstain from sexual relations with his wife for a period, with legal consequences after four months.
الفيء (al-fay'): The husband's return to normal marital relations within the ila' period, cancelling the effects of ila'.
الشبهة (al-shubha): Doubt or uncertainty that prevents the application of a hadd punishment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on a man who was coerced into ila'?
A) The ila' is invalid and no consequences follow.
B) The ila' is valid, and if he does not return within four months, half the mahr is due.
C) He can recover the mahr from the coercer if divorce occurs.
D) The ila' is valid only if he did not consummate.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, coerced ila', fay', and Islam are valid, but the doubt in coerced Islam prevents execution for apostasy.

The Ma'dhun's Authority: Marriage Requires Explicit Permission
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the scope of ma'dhun authority

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether a ma'dhun (authorized slave) may marry or marry off his slaves without explicit permission, and explain the positions of Abu Hanifa, Muhammad, and Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
Trade authorization does not include marriage — the ma'dhun needs explicit permission to marry off slaves.

In this lesson, we examine a specific limit on the ma'dhun's authority. A ma'dhun is a slave permitted by his master to engage in trade. The question arises: does this general permission extend to arranging marriages, either for himself or for his slaves? The Hanafi school clarifies that marriage is not a trade activity, so without explicit authorization, the ma'dhun cannot validly conduct marriages. We will explore the majority view and an important intra-Hanafi difference raised by Abu Yusuf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruled Act
The passage states two prohibitions: the ma'dhun does not marry (himself) and does not marry off his slaves (both male and female). Focus on the second act: marrying off a slave.
The term 'yuzawwij' here means to give in marriage, i.e., to contract a marriage on behalf of someone else.
2
Understand the Basis of the Ruling
The reasoning given is that marriage is not from trade (laysa min al-tijarah). Since the ma'dhun's authority is limited to trade, marriage falls outside that scope.
This is a talil (reason) that distinguishes trade from marriage.
3
Note the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Abu Yusuf holds a different view: he permits the ma'dhun to marry off a female slave because doing so can be a means of acquiring wealth (dowry). Contrast this with the majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) who reject this analogy.
The text explicitly presents the positions of the two (lahuma) and Abu Yusuf.
4
Determine the Relied-Upon Position
The passage indicates that the relied-upon position (al-muhtār) is that a specific, separate authorization is required. This is the basis for fatwa.
Al-Hidayah, a key Hanafi reference, supports the majority view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يتزوج ولا يزوج رقيقه. وعند أبي يوسف له أن يزوج الأمة؛ لأنه تحصيل المال، لهما: إنه ليس من التجارة لا يحجر به، ويحصل به المقصود وهو الربح ويملك. كذا في «الهداية». قوله: ولا يزوج رقيقه؛ أي لا يكفي في تزويجه الإذن المفهوم من الإذن العام للتجارة، بل يجب أن يأذن له قصداً على حدة.
And he does not marry, nor does he marry off his slaves. And according to Abu Yusuf, he may marry off the female slave, because it is acquisition of wealth; according to the two [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad], it is not from trade, he is not prevented by it, and the objective—profit—is attained and he owns. Likewise in al-Hidayah. The author says: 'Nor does he marry off his slaves'; i.e., the permission understood from the general permission for trade is not sufficient for marrying them off; rather, he must explicitly authorize him separately.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding whether the ma'dhun (authorized slave) may marry off his slaves. The base ruling (matn) states that the ma'dhun does not marry and does not marry off his slaves. Abu Yusuf holds that he may marry off the female slave because it leads to acquisition of wealth (through the dowry), thus falling under trade. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (Sahibayn) argue that marrying off slaves is not trade per se; it does not obstruct trade and can yield profit and ownership, but it is not a customary trade activity. The relied-upon position (al-mukhtar) is that marriage is not included in the general authorization for trade; the master must give explicit permission for it. The commentary emphasizes that the ma'dhun's capacity to act is limited to what is necessary for trade; marriage is not a trade act, so it requires a specific, separate authorization.

Key Terms
يزوج (yuzawwij): To give in marriage; here, the ma'dhun marrying off his slave (either male or female).
رقيق (raqiq): Slaves (plural of raqiq).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority of Hanafi imams (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), why may the ma'dhun not marry off his female slave without explicit permission?
A) Because it is a form of worship, not trade.
B) Because marriage is not a trade activity and therefore not covered by implied authority.
C) Because the female slave must consent.
D) Because the ma'dhun does not own the slave.

Key Takeaway
The ma'dhun's trade authorization does not automatically include marriage; explicit permission from the master is required for the ma'dhun to marry or marry off his slaves.

The Ma'dhun's Intercourse with His Female Slave: Pure Zina Despite Permission
Ruling that the ma'dhun's intercourse with his female slave is adultery

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a ma'dhun having intercourse with his own female slave and explain why it remains zina despite the master's permission.

Core Idea
Even explicit permission from the master cannot make the ma'dhun's intercourse with his female slave permissible; it remains pure fornication.

This passage addresses a critical boundary in Hanafi fiqh regarding the authorized slave (ma'dhun). The ma'dhun is given permission only for trade, not for marriage or concubinage. Here, the scholars emphasize that even if the master explicitly gifts his female slave and permits intercourse, the act remains absolutely unlawful (haram) and is considered zina. The only concession is that the hadd punishment is dropped due to legal ambiguity (shubha), but the sin remains.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is the ma'dhun having intercourse with his own female slave. The ruling is that it is pure zina and haram.
Focus on the absolute prohibition; the ma'dhun's permission is strictly limited to trade.
2
Locate the proof
The proof is the limitation of the ma'dhun's authorization: he is only permitted for trade, not for sexual relations. This is a rational argument (dalil aqli) based on the scope of the permission.
No specific Quran or hadith is cited; the reasoning is that his capacity (wilaya) does not extend to marriage or concubinage.
3
Understand the exception: hadd dropped
The passage states that the only difference from intercourse with a completely unrelated woman is that the hadd punishment is dropped due to shubha (ambiguity) arising from the master's gift. This does not change the prohibition.
Shubha prevents punishment but does not make the act halal.
4
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Some schools might argue that if the master transfers full ownership, the ma'dhun gains the right to intercourse. The Hanafi position is that his status as ma'dhun permanently blocks this right.
The mufta bihi is the Hanafi position: the act is always zina.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يجب التنبيه أنه لا يجوز للمأذون أن يتخذ أمته للمجامعة، وإن صرّح المولى بتجويزه قصداً على حدة، حتى أنّ المولى لـو سَلّم إلى مأذونه أمته المملوكة فقال : أعطيتكها ووهبتكها فتمتع بها كتمتع الرجال من النساء، فقبضها فوطء، يكون زناً محضاً، وحراماً صرفاً، ولا فرق بينها وبين الأجنبيات إلا بسقوط الحد فيه للشبهة. كذا في «حاشية الجلبي» ناقلا عن «البيانية» و«التحفة».
And from what must be noted: it is not permissible for the authorized slave to take his female slave for intercourse, even if the master explicitly permits it as a separate intent, such that if the master handed over to his authorized slave his owned female slave and said: 'I give her to you and I gift her to you, so enjoy her as men enjoy women,' and he takes possession and has intercourse, it is pure fornication and absolutely unlawful. There is no difference between her and foreign women except the dropping of the hadd punishment due to the ambiguity. Thus in 'Hashiyat al-Jalabī' quoting from 'al-Bayāniyya' and 'al-Tuhfa'.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with rational proof, clarifying that the ma'dhun is never permitted to have sexual intercourse with his female slave, even if his master explicitly gives him permission. The reasoning is that the ma'dhun's permission is solely for trade; sexual relations are entirely outside that scope. The act is considered pure fornication (zina) and absolutely unlawful (haram). The only difference from intercourse with a completely unrelated woman is that the hadd punishment is not applied due to a legal ambiguity (shubha) arising from the master's gift, but the sin remains. This ruling is cited from the commentaries of al-Jalabī, al-Bayāniyya, and al-Tuhfa.

Key Terms
أمة (amah): Female slave owned by the ma'dhun, but he does not have ownership rights over her body for intercourse.
شبهة (shubha): Legal ambiguity that prevents the application of hadd punishment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the status of intercourse between a ma'dhun (authorized slave) and his female slave if the master explicitly gives permission?
A) Halal and permissible
B) Makruh but not sinful
C) Pure fornication (zina) and haram
D) Valid if he owns her

Key Takeaway
The ma'dhun's intercourse with his female slave is zina and haram, even with the master's permission; only the hadd is dropped due to shubha.

The Three Categories of a Minor's Transactions
Opening statement and two commentary notes on classification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a minor's transaction as purely beneficial, purely harmful, or mixed, and state the Hanafi ruling for each category.

Core Idea
A minor's transaction is valid if purely beneficial, invalid if purely harmful, and suspended on guardian's permission if mixed.

This lesson introduces the Hanafi framework for evaluating a minor's legal actions. Understanding this trichotomy is essential for determining which transactions a minor can perform independently and which require guardian oversight. The classification is based on the nature of the transaction—whether it brings benefit, causes harm, or is ambiguous.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories
Read the opening sentence of the passage. It lists three types: purely beneficial (nafi' mahd), purely harmful (dar mahd), and mixed (ma nafa'a wa darra).
Examples given: Islam/gift = beneficial; divorce/manumission = harmful; sale/purchase = mixed.
2
State the ruling for each
Purely beneficial → valid without permission; purely harmful → invalid even with permission; mixed → suspended on guardian's permission.
The ruling for mixed transactions is further detailed in the next lesson.
3
Understand the rationale
The text explains: the minor has partial capacity (ahliyyah qasirah) for benefit, needs full capacity for harm, and mixed transactions require guardian oversight to prevent harm.
This rationale is key to distinguishing Hanafi view from Shafi'i.
4
Apply the categories to examples
Given a list of minor's actions, determine the category and ruling. E.g., accepting a gift = beneficial → valid; giving divorce = harmful → invalid; buying a book = mixed → needs guardian permission.

فصل في حكم تصرف الصبي
(وتصرف الصبي إن نفعَ كالإسلام والاتهاب، صح بلا إذن، وإن ضر كالطلاق والعتاق لا وإن أذنَ به ، وما نفع وضر كالبيع والشراء عُلقَ بإذن وليه
فصل في حكم تصرف الصبي
وتصرف الصبي إن نفع كالإسلام والاتهاب، صح بلا إذن، وإن ضر كالطلاق والعتاق لا وإن أذنَ به ، وما نفع وضر" كالبيع والشراء عُلقَ بإذن وليه) ؛ اكتفاء بالأهلية القاصرة في النافع ، واشتراطاً للكاملة في الضار، ودفعاً للضرر بانضمام رأي الولي في المتردّد بينهما ، وعند الشافعي ) لا يصح تصرفه بإجازة الولى، وكذا لا يصح إسلامه.
[ 1 ] قوله : وتصرف الصبي..الخ ؛ هذا شروع لبيان أحكام إذن الصبي بعد الفراغ من أحكام إذن العبد إلا أنه قدم الأول لكثرة وقوعه، ولكونه مجمعاً عليه في الجواز، لا يقال : قد سبق حالُ الصبي في كتاب الحجر» ؛ لأنا نقول : إنما ذكر هناك لبيان حجره ، وهنا لبيان كونه مأذوناً بإذن وليه ، فلا اعتبار. كذا في حاشية الجلبي)" [٢]قوله : وما نفع وضر...الخ ؛ فإن قيل : إذا باع شيئاً بأضعاف قيمته كان نافعاً
محضاً، كقبول الهبة، فيجب نفوذه بلا تعليق وتوقف. أجيب : بأن المعتبر في ذلك هو الوضع لا الجزئيات الواقعة اتفاقاً، والبيع في أصل الوضع متردد بينهما. كذا في «حاشية الجلبي» [٣] قوله : لا يصح تصرفه ...الخ ؛ لأنّ حجره لصباه نفسه، وهو باق بعد الإذن، وبقاء العلة يستلزم بقاء المعلول لا محالة، بخلافِ حجر الرق، فإنه ليس للرق نفسه بل لحق المولى، وهو يسقط بإذنه، لكونه راضياً بتصرّفه حينئذ ؛ ولأنه مولى عليه حتى يملك المولى التصرف والحجر عليه، والمولى عليه لا يكون وليّاً للمنافاة ؛ لأنّ الأول : سمة العجز، والثاني : سمة القدرة، وهما متضادان فلا يجتمعان.
وشرطه : أن يعقل البيع سالباً للملك والشراء جالباً له. ووليه : أبوه، ثم وصيه
ثم جده ثم وصيه وشرطه : أن يعقل البيع سالباً للملكِ والشراء جالباً له.
ولنا : إنّ التصرف المشروع صدرَ من أهله في محله عن ولاية شرعية، فوجب تنفيذه على ما عرف تقريره في الخلافيات، والصبا سبب الحجر ؛ لعدم الهداية لا لذاته، وقد ثبتت نظراً إلى إذن الولي وبقاء ولايته لنظر الصبي ؛ لاستيفاء المصلحة بطريقين، واحتمال تبدل الحال، بخلاف الطلاق والعتاق ؛ لأنه ضار محض، فلم يؤهل له. والنافع المحض، كقبول الهبة والصدقة يؤهل له قبل الإذن، والبيع والشراء دائر بين النفع والضرر، فيجعل أهلاً له بعد الإذن لا ،قبله، لكن قبل الإذن يكون موقوفاً منه على إجازة الولي؛ لاحتمال وقوعه نظراً للصبي، وصحة التصرف في نفسه، كذا في «الهداية»(۱). [1] قوله : وشرطه ؛ أي شرط كونه مأذوناً يعني أن الشرط من كون الصبي عاقلاً أن يعرف مضمون البيع والشراء ، لا أن يعرف مجرّد العبارة، هذا عين عبارة «البيان»، كذا في «حاشية الجلبي». [٢] قوله : ووليه أبوه ؛ أي الصبي ثم وصي الأب بعد موته، ثم وصي وصيه ، ثمّ بعدهـم جـده الصحيح وإن عــلا ، ثـم بـعـد مــوت الجـد وصـيه ، ثم وصــي وصيه.
Chapter on the ruling of a minor's transaction. (The transaction of a minor: if it is beneficial, like accepting Islam or accepting a gift, it is valid without permission. If it is harmful, like divorce or freeing a slave, it is not valid even if he permits it. And what is both beneficial and harmful, like sale and purchase, is suspended on the permission of his guardian.) — Sufficing with limited capacity in beneficial, requiring full capacity in harmful, and preventing harm by including the guardian's opinion in what is ambiguous between them. According to al-Shafi'i, his transaction is not valid with the guardian's permission, nor is his acceptance of Islam valid. [1] His words: 'The transaction of a minor...' This is the beginning of explaining the rulings of a minor's permission after finishing the rulings of a slave's permission, but he presented the former first due to its frequent occurrence and because it is agreed upon as permissible. It cannot be said: The case of the minor preceded in the Book of Hijr; because we say: He mentioned it there to explain his interdiction, and here to explain that he is permitted by his guardian's permission, so there is no contradiction. Thus in Hashiyat al-Jalabi. [2] His words: 'and what is both beneficial and harmful...' For if it is said: If he sells something at many times its value, it was purely beneficial, like accepting a gift, so it should be effective without suspension. The answer: What is considered is the nature of the transaction, not the accidental particulars. Sale in its original nature is ambiguous between them. Thus in Hashiyat al-Jalabi. [3] His words: 'his transaction is not valid...' Because his interdiction is due to his minority itself, and it remains after permission, and the persistence of the cause necessarily requires the persistence of the effect. Unlike the interdiction of slavery, for it is not due to slavery itself but due to the right of the master, and it drops with his permission because he is then pleased with his transaction. And because he is under guardianship, and the guardian has authority over him and his interdiction. The one under guardianship cannot be a guardian due to contradiction, because the first is a sign of incapacity and the second of capability, and they are opposites, so they cannot combine. And its condition: that he understand sale as removing ownership and purchase as bringing ownership. And his guardian: his father, then his father's executor, then his grandfather, then his grandfather's executor. And its condition: that he understand sale as removing ownership and purchase as bringing ownership. And our proof: The legislated transaction issued from one qualified for it in its place with a legal authority, so it must be executed as known in the disputations. Minority is a cause of interdiction due to lack of guidance, not due to its essence. And it has been established considering the guardian's permission and the continuation of his guardianship for the minor's benefit, to achieve benefit by two ways and the possibility of change of situation. Unlike divorce and manumission, because they are purely harmful, so he is not qualified for them. And purely beneficial like accepting a gift or charity qualifies him before permission. Sale and purchase fluctuate between benefit and harm, so he becomes qualified for them after permission but not before. But before permission, they were suspended from him on the guardian's approval, due to the possibility of it occurring for the minor's interest and the validity of the transaction in itself. Thus in al-Hidayah. [1] His words: 'And its condition' means the condition of being permitted, that the condition of the minor being rational is that he knows the content of sale and purchase, not just the words. This is exactly the wording of al-Bayan, thus in Hashiyat al-Jalabi. [2] His words: 'And his guardian is his father' meaning the father of the minor, then the father's executor after his death, then the executor's executor, then after them his true grandfather even if higher, then after the grandfather's death his executor, then his executor's executor.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof and an inter-madhhab dispute. The author begins by stating the general rule concerning a minor's transactions, classifying them into three types: purely beneficial, purely harmful, and mixed. Purely beneficial acts (like accepting Islam or a gift) are valid without any permission because the minor possesses limited legal capacity (ahliyyah qasirah) sufficient for benefit. Purely harmful acts (like divorce or freeing a slave) are invalid even if the minor gives permission himself, because they cause loss and require full capacity. Mixed transactions (like sale and purchase) are suspended on the permission of the guardian (wali), because they are neither purely beneficial nor purely harmful; combining the minor's capacity with the guardian's oversight protects against harm. The commentator then explains that this chapter follows the chapter on a slave's permission; the minor's case is presented first because it is more common and agreed upon. He addresses an objection: if a minor sells something at a huge profit, it becomes purely beneficial like accepting a gift, so why isn't it valid without permission? The reply is that the ruling is based on the generic nature of sale, which is inherently ambiguous; individual windfall cases do not change the general rule. The Shafi'i position is then refuted: the Shafi'is hold that a minor's transaction is never valid even with the guardian's permission, and even his acceptance of Islam is invalid. The Hanafi response explains that the cause of interdiction (hajr) for a minor is his own minority, which remains even after permission, so the interdiction continues — unlike slavery, where the cause is the master's right, which drops with permission. Moreover, a minor under guardianship cannot also be a guardian for himself because guardianship implies capability and the minor's state implies incapability, which are contradictory. The Hanafi proof: a legally sanctioned transaction has been done by a qualified person in its proper place with legitimate authority, so it must be executed. Minority is a cause of interdiction only due to lack of guidance, not in itself; with the guardian's permission and continued oversight, guidance is achieved. The passage then gives the condition for the minor to be considered permitted: he must understand that sale removes ownership and purchase brings ownership — mere verbal repetition is insufficient. Finally, the hierarchy of guardians is given: the father, then his executor (wasi), then the grandfather (the father's father), then his executor. This order is derived from the principle that the closest male agnate with authority over the minor's property has priority.

Key Terms
صبي (sabiy): A minor child who has not reached puberty, subject to interdiction (hajr) due to minority.
ولاية (wilayah): Authority over the minor's property and affairs, held by the father, then his executor, then grandfather, etc.
إذن (idhn): Permission given by the guardian allowing the minor to engage in mixed transactions like sale and purchase.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling if a minor accepts a gift (hibah) without his guardian's permission?
A) The gift is invalid because the minor has no capacity.
B) The gift is valid because it is a purely beneficial act.
C) The gift is suspended until the guardian permits it.
D) The gift is valid only if the minor understands the concept of ownership.

Key Takeaway
A minor's transaction is classified by its nature: purely beneficial acts are valid without permission, purely harmful acts are invalid, and mixed acts require guardian permission.

The Hanafi Proof for a Minor's Mixed Transactions and the Shafi'i Counterargument
Commentary on the Shafi'i dispute and Hanafi proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi reasoning for allowing a minor's mixed transactions with guardian permission and evaluate the Shafi'i objection that the minor's interdiction persists after permission.

Core Idea
Minor's interdiction is due to lack of guidance, not minority itself; with guardian's permission, guidance is restored, making mixed transactions valid.

This lesson delves into the Hanafi proof for the validity of a minor's mixed transactions when the guardian permits, and addresses the Shafi'i counterargument. It also clarifies the condition that the minor must understand the transaction, and outlines the hierarchy of guardians. Understanding this proof is essential for appreciating the Hanafi emphasis on maslahah (benefit) and the role of the guardian.

Guided Path
1
State the Shafi'i objection
Read the commentary note [3] and the opening objection. The Shafi'i argue that the minor's interdiction is due to minority itself, which persists after permission, so transactions remain invalid.
This objection also applies to acceptance of Islam according to Shafi'i.
2
Explain the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response distinguishes the cause of interdiction: for a minor, it is lack of guidance (adam al-hidaya), not minority per se. With the guardian's permission, guidance is achieved, removing the cause.
This is contrasted with slavery, where the cause is the master's right, which drops with permission.
3
Understand the proof (lana)
Read the passage starting 'ولنا : إن التصرف المشروع...' It states that the transaction is issued by a qualified person (the minor with limited capacity) in its proper place with legal authority (guardian's permission), so it must be executed.
The proof also explains why purely harmful acts like divorce remain invalid: they are entirely harmful and the minor is never qualified.
4
Identify the condition for the minor
The condition for the minor to be permitted (ma'dhun) is that he understands the nature of the transaction: that sale removes ownership and purchase brings ownership. Mere verbal repetition is insufficient.
This condition ensures the minor has the necessary comprehension.
5
Learn the guardian hierarchy
The passage lists the guardians in order: father, then his executor, then grandfather (even if higher), then grandfather's executor.
This hierarchy is based on closeness and authority over the minor's property.

And it has been established considering the guardian's permission and the continuation of his guardianship for the minor's benefit, to achieve benefit by two ways and the possibility of change of situation. Unlike divorce and manumission, because they are purely harmful, so he is not qualified for them. And purely beneficial like accepting a gift or charity qualifies him before permission. Sale and purchase fluctuate between benefit and harm, so he becomes qualified for them after permission but not before. But before permission, they were suspended from him on the guardian's approval, due to the possibility of it occurring for the minor's interest and the validity of the transaction in itself. Thus in al-Hidayah. [1] His words: 'And its condition' means the condition of being permitted, that the condition of the minor being rational is that he knows the content of sale and purchase, not just the words. This is exactly the wording of al-Bayan, thus in Hashiyat al-Jalabi. [2] His words: 'And his guardian is his father' meaning the father of the minor, then the father's executor after his death, then the executor's executor, then after them his true grandfather even if higher, then after the grandfather's death his executor, then his executor's executor.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof and an inter-madhhab dispute. The author begins by stating the general rule concerning a minor's transactions, classifying them into three types: purely beneficial, purely harmful, and mixed. Purely beneficial acts (like accepting Islam or a gift) are valid without any permission because the minor possesses limited legal capacity (ahliyyah qasirah) sufficient for benefit. Purely harmful acts (like divorce or freeing a slave) are invalid even if the minor gives permission himself, because they cause loss and require full capacity. Mixed transactions (like sale and purchase) are suspended on the permission of the guardian (wali), because they are neither purely beneficial nor purely harmful; combining the minor's capacity with the guardian's oversight protects against harm. The commentator then explains that this chapter follows the chapter on a slave's permission; the minor's case is presented first because it is more common and agreed upon. He addresses an objection: if a minor sells something at a huge profit, it becomes purely beneficial like accepting a gift, so why isn't it valid without permission? The reply is that the ruling is based on the generic nature of sale, which is inherently ambiguous; individual windfall cases do not change the general rule. The Shafi'i position is then refuted: the Shafi'is hold that a minor's transaction is never valid even with the guardian's permission, and even his acceptance of Islam is invalid. The Hanafi response explains that the cause of interdiction (hajr) for a minor is his own minority, which remains even after permission, so the interdiction continues — unlike slavery, where the cause is the master's right, which drops with permission. Moreover, a minor under guardianship cannot also be a guardian for himself because guardianship implies capability and the minor's state implies incapability, which are contradictory. The Hanafi proof: a legally sanctioned transaction has been done by a qualified person in its proper place with legitimate authority, so it must be executed. Minority is a cause of interdiction only due to lack of guidance, not in itself; with the guardian's permission and continued oversight, guidance is achieved. The passage then gives the condition for the minor to be considered permitted: he must understand that sale removes ownership and purchase brings ownership — mere verbal repetition is insufficient. Finally, the hierarchy of guardians is given: the father, then his executor (wasi), then the grandfather (the father's father), then his executor. This order is derived from the principle that the closest male agnate with authority over the minor's property has priority.

Key Terms
صبي (sabiy): A minor child who has not reached puberty, subject to interdiction (hajr) due to minority.
ولاية (wilayah): Authority over the minor's property and affairs, held by the father, then his executor, then grandfather, etc.
إذن (idhn): Permission given by the guardian allowing the minor to engage in mixed transactions like sale and purchase.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following is a condition for a minor's mixed transaction to be valid with guardian permission?
A) The minor must be at least 15 years old.
B) The minor must understand the transaction (e.g., that sale removes ownership).
C) The guardian must be the father, not the grandfather.
D) The transaction must be witnessed by two males.

Key Takeaway
A minor's mixed transaction becomes valid with guardian permission and the minor's understanding; the Shafi'i objection that minority itself prevents validity is refuted by the Hanafi distinction between cause (lack of guidance) and essence (minority).

Guardians Authorized to Permit Minor's Trade
Explaining who can authorize a minor's trade: father, his executor, grandfather, judge, his executor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the hierarchy of guardians who can authorize a minor's trade and explain the distinction between an executor (wasi) and a deputy (wakil) in Hanafi law.

Core Idea
Father, his executor, grandfather, his executor, judge, or judge's executor can authorize a minor's trade; no others.

This lesson explains a key ruling from the Book of al-Ma'dhun. The Hanafi school specifies a clear hierarchy of guardians who have the authority to permit a minor to engage in trade. Understanding the order and the distinction between an executor and a deputy is crucial for correctly applying the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act in question is authorizing a minor to engage in trade. The ruling is that only specific guardians can give valid permission.
2
List the guardians in order
The hierarchy is: father, then his executor after his death, then grandfather, then his executor, then the judge, then the judge's executor.
The matn mentions 'judge or his executor' but the commentator clarifies the order.
3
Distinguish between wasi and wakil
A wasi (executor) is appointed after death to manage property; a wakil (deputy) is appointed during life for specific transactions. The father's administrator during his life is a wakil, not a wasi.
The same distinction applies to the grandfather's appointee.
4
Explain why the judge's executor is called wasi
The judge's executor acts during the judge's lifetime, but he is still called wasi because the judge's authority substitutes for the father's authority. The judge's action is like the father's.
This is a special usage unique to the judge's appointee.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب المأذون أحكامه
القاضي أو وصيه ، ولو أقر بما معه من كسبه أو إرثه صح
ثم القاضي أو وصيه : إنما قال : ثم وصيه في الأولين ، وقال : أو وصيه في الأخير؛ لأنّ وصي الأبِ مَن استخلفَهُ بعد موتِهِ في التصرف في مال ولده، وأما الذي أذن له في التّصرُّفِ حالَ حياتِهِ فوكيل لا وصي، وكذا في الجد، وأما وصي القاضي فهو الذي أمره بالتّصرف في مال اليتيم، فهو يتصرف في حال حياة القاضي، وإنما سمي وصيَّاً مع أنَّ الإيصاء هو الاستخلاف بعد الموت ؛ لأنَّ هنا يصير خليفة للاب، كان الأب جعله وصيّاً، فإن فعل القاضي يصير كفعله ، فمعنى الكلام أن وليه أبوه ، ثم وصيه بعد موته ، ثم الجد إن لم يكن الأب، ولا وصيه ثم وصيه بعد موته ، ثمَّ القاضي أو وصيه، أيهما تصرف صح.
Book of the Authorized Minor: Its Rulings. The judge or his executor, and if he acknowledges what is with him from his earnings or inheritance, it is valid. Then the judge or his executor: He only said 'then his executor' in the first two, and said 'or his executor' in the last; because the executor of the father is one whom he appointed after his death to dispose of his child's property. As for one who authorized him to dispose during his lifetime, he is a deputy, not an executor. Likewise for the grandfather. As for the judge's executor, he is the one whom he ordered to dispose of the orphan's property, so he disposes during the judge's lifetime. He is called an executor even though 'bequest' (isā') is appointment after death, because here he becomes a successor to the father, as if the father made him an executor, so the judge's action becomes like his action. Thus the meaning of the statement is: his guardian is his father, then his executor after his death, then the grandfather if there is no father or his executor, then his executor after his death, then the judge or his executor; whichever of them transacts, it is valid.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This is a ruling statement from the book of al-Ma'dhun (the authorized minor). The text explains the hierarchy of guardians who have the authority to grant permission (idhn) for a minor to engage in trade. The base text (matn) lists: the judge or his executor, but the commentator clarifies the order. The father is the primary guardian. After his death, his executor (wasi) takes over. If there is no father or executor, the grandfather has authority, then his executor. Finally, the judge (qadi) or his executor can give permission. The commentator distinguishes between an executor (wasi) appointed after death and a deputy (wakil) appointed during life. The judge's executor is called an executor even though he acts during the judge's lifetime, because the judge's authority stems from being a substitute for the father. The mother and her executor cannot give such permission, as clarified later in the hashiya.

Key Terms
وصي الأب (wasi al-ab): The executor appointed by the father in his will to manage the child's property after the father's death.
وصي القاضي (wasi al-qadi): The person appointed by the judge to manage the orphan's property; he acts during the judge's lifetime.
وكيل (wakil): An agent appointed by the father during his lifetime to transact on behalf of the child; not an executor.
وصي (wasi): Executor appointed after death to manage property and make decisions for minors.
وكالة (wakala): Agency; a contract by which a person appoints another to act on their behalf during their lifetime.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Who among the following can give permission (idhn) for a minor to engage in trade according to the Hanafi school?
A) The mother
B) The executor of the mother
C) The judge's executor
D) An older brother

Key Takeaway
Only the father, his executor, grandfather, his executor, judge, or judge's executor can authorize a minor's trade; understanding the hierarchy prevents invalid transactions.

The Authorized Minor's Acknowledgment: Earnings vs. Inheritance
Ruling on a permitted minor's acknowledgment with intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on an authorized minor's acknowledgment and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding inheritance.

Core Idea
Authorization lifts incapacity; the minor's acknowledgment is valid for trade consequences, but inheritance remains disputed.

In Hanafi fiqh, when a guardian authorizes a minor for trade (ma'dhun), the minor gains capacity in financial matters. But can he make a binding acknowledgment (iqrar) about property he holds, especially if it came from inheritance? The school has two positions: the mainstream view allows it, while a narration from Abu Hanifa himself restricts it to earnings only. Let us examine the reasoning and the practical impact.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Ruling
The act is the minor's acknowledgment (iqrar) regarding property in his possession – either from earnings (kasb) or inheritance (irth). The base matn states it is valid (sahih).
2
Locate the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
In the zahir al-riwaya (the authoritative transmission of Imam Muhammad), the acknowledgment is valid for both earnings and inheritance. However, a narration from Hasan ibn Ziyad reports that Abu Hanifa restricts validity to earnings only.
This is a dispute within the Hanafi school itself, not between madhahib.
3
Analyze the Reasoning for Each Position
The mainstream argument: authorization lifts incapacity (hajr), making the minor akin to an adult. Since both earnings and inheritance are his property (maliyya), acknowledgment should be valid. The restrictive argument: validity for earnings is a necessity of trade (people would not deal with him otherwise), but inheritance is not a direct consequence of trade, so the necessity is absent.
The reasoning is based on ta'lil (effective cause) – what is the underlying reason for validity?
4
Understand the Response to the Objection
An objection arises: if the guardian cannot himself acknowledge on behalf of the minor, how can his authorization enable the minor to acknowledge? The answer: the guardian can authorize trade and its necessary consequences, and acknowledgment is such a consequence.
This shows that the authorization directly empowers the minor for trade-related acts.
5
Determine the Mufta Bihi (Relyed-upon Position)
The text indicates that the zahir al-riwaya is the apparent position (wajh al-zahir), and the narration from Hasan is exceptional. Later authorities like al-Kifaya support the validity for both. Thus, the mufta bihi is the validity for both earnings and inheritance.
The authority_weight is zahir_riwayah, the highest level.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو أقر بما معه من كسبه أو إرثه صح ، فإنَّ المولى إذا أذنَ الصَّبي بالتجارة صح إقراره بكسه ؛ لأنَّه من تمام التجارة ، إذ لو لم يصح إقرارُهُ لا يعاملُهُ النَّاسُ مع أن إقرار الولي لا يصح " ؛ لأنه إقرار على الغير، وإقرار الصبي إقرار على نفسه، والحجر ارتفعَ بالإذنِ فصار كالبالغ فصح إقراره بالإرث أيضاً في ظاهر الرواية، وعن أبي حنيفة الله : أنه لا يصح في الإرث ؛ لأَنَّهُ إِنَّما يصح في الكسب ؛ لِمَا ذُكِرَ أنه من توابع التجارة، ولا كذلك في الإرث.
فإن الولي إذا أذن الصبي ... الخ، من أنّ الولاية المتعدية فرع الولاية الأصلية القائمة ، والولي لا يملك الإقرار على الصبي، فكيف يفيد ذلك بإذنه.
والجواب : إنه أفاده من حيث كونه من توابع التجارة، والولي يملك الإذن والتجارة وتوابعها، وقد عبر الشارح الله له عن هذا الجواب بقوله ؛ لأنه من تمام التجارة، إذ لو لم يصح إقراره لا يعامله الناس كذا في حاشية الجلبي
[١]قوله : في ظاهر الرواية ؛ قيد بظاهر الرواية ؛ لأنه روى الحسن له عن الأعظم ه أنه لا يصح إقراره فيما ورثه عن أبيه ؛ لأنّ صحة إقراره في كسبه لحاجته إلى ذلك في التجارات، حتى لا يمتنع الناس عن المبايعة معه . خوفاً عن تَوَى أموالهم ؛ ولهذا ملكه ، وإن لم يملك الولي الإقرار عليه.
ووجه الظاهر : إنّه بانضمام رأي الولي التحق بالبالغ، وكل واحد من المالين ملكه فارغ عن حاجة الغير، فيصح إقراره فيهما. كذا في «الكفاية»(۳) ؛ ولهذا جوز أبو حنيفة له بعد الإذن تصرفه بالغبن الفاحش كالبالغين ، فكان الإرث والكسب سواء في صحة الإقرار لكونهما مالية.
And if he acknowledges what is with him from his earnings or his inheritance, it is valid. For when the guardian authorizes the minor for trade, his acknowledgment of his earnings is valid because it is a completion of trade. If his acknowledgment were not valid, people would not deal with him. While the acknowledgment of the guardian is not valid because it is an acknowledgment against others. And the minor's acknowledgment is an acknowledgment against himself. The incapacity (hajr) is lifted by the authorization, so he becomes like an adult. Thus his acknowledgment of inheritance is also valid in the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya). From Abu Hanifa: It is not valid regarding inheritance, for it is only valid regarding earnings because that is a consequence of trade, not so for inheritance. For if the guardian authorizes the minor... etc., that the derived guardianship is a branch of the original existing guardianship, and the guardian does not possess the right to acknowledge against the minor, so how does that benefit by his authorization? The answer: It benefits insofar as it is a consequence of trade, and the guardian possesses the authority to authorize trade and its consequences. The commentator expressed this answer by saying 'because it is completion of trade; if his acknowledgment were not valid, people would not deal with him' as in Hashiyat al-Jalabi. [1] His statement: 'in the apparent narration' — he qualified it with 'apparent narration' because al-Hasan narrated from al-A'zam (Abu Hanifa) that his acknowledgment of what he inherited from his father is not valid, because the validity of his acknowledgment of his earnings is due to his need for that in trade, so that people do not refrain from transacting with him for fear of losing their money. For this reason he is allowed that, even though the guardian does not possess the right of acknowledgment over him. And the apparent position: He, by joining the opinion of the guardian, becomes attached to the adult, and each of the two properties is his property free from the need of others, so his acknowledgment in both is valid. As in al-Kifaya. For this reason, Abu Hanifa allowed him after authorization to transact with gross injustice like adults. Thus inheritance and earnings are equal in the validity of acknowledgment because both are property.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage addresses the issue of an authorized minor's (ma'dhun) acknowledgment (iqrar) regarding property in his possession, whether from earnings (kasb) or inheritance (irth). The base ruling from the matn states that such acknowledgment is valid. The commentary explains the reasoning: authorization lifts legal incapacity (hajr), making the minor like an adult in financial matters. The acknowledgment is necessary for trade; without it, people would not deal with him. Note that a guardian's acknowledgment on behalf of the minor is invalid because it is against another. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: In the zahir al-riwaya (the well-known transmission from the six books of Imam Muhammad), the minor's acknowledgment is valid for both earnings and inheritance. However, a narration from Hasan ibn Ziyad reports that Abu Hanifa restricted validity to earnings only, because earnings are a direct consequence of trade, while inheritance is not. The commentator then addresses a potential objection: if the guardian cannot acknowledge, how can his authorization enable the minor to acknowledge? The response is that the guardian can authorize trade and its necessary consequences, and acknowledgment is one such consequence. The passage concludes by stating that Abu Hanifa's ruling permitting the minor to transact even with gross injustice (ghabn fahish) like adults supports the equality of earnings and inheritance in this context, as both are property (maliyya).

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrar): A declaration or admission by the minor regarding ownership of property in his possession; it has legal effect after authorization.
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwaya): The well-known transmitted rulings from Imam Muhammad's six books (al-Mabsut, etc.), considered the primary authority in the Hanafi school.
كسب (kasb): Earnings acquired through the minor's trade or labor after being authorized.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on an authorized minor's acknowledgment of inheritance according to the zahir al-riwaya?
A) It is invalid because inheritance is not related to trade
B) It is valid because authorization lifts incapacity
C) It is valid only if the guardian also acknowledges
D) It is invalid because the minor cannot own inheritance

Key Takeaway
An authorized minor's acknowledgment is valid for both earnings and inheritance according to the relied-upon Hanafi position, as authorization grants full capacity over property.

Shuf'ah in Mixed Dower: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on shuf'ah in mixed dower

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi disagreement on shuf'ah when a dower includes a cash component from the wife, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Shuf'ah attaches to any portion exchanged for consideration, even within a marriage.

This passage examines a specific case where a marriage dower involves a house and a cash payment from the wife. The Hanafi school is divided: Abu Hanifa denies shuf'ah in the house entirely, while his two disciples grant shuf'ah proportional to the cash. Understanding this dispute illuminates how the school resolves internal differences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Transaction
Recognize that the dower is a house but the wife pays 1,000 dirhams to the husband. This creates a mixed consideration.
2
State Abu Hanifa's Position
Abu Hanifa says no shuf'ah in the entire house because the primary contract is marriage, not a pure sale.
Marriage is the dominant element.
3
State the Sahibayn's Position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say shuf'ah is obligatory in the portion equal to the cash, because that part is effectively a sale.
The cash creates valid consideration.
4
Distinguish from Gift (Hibah)
Contrast with a gift that does not condition compensation in the contract — even if compensation is paid later, it remains a gift and not a sale.
The condition in the contract is key.
5
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf
Under Abu Hanifa, a co-owner cannot claim shuf'ah; under Sahibayn, he can claim the portion corresponding to the cash.
The mufta bihi is Sahibayn's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا قوبل ببعضها مال كما إذا تزوجها على دار على أن ترد عليه ألفاً، فلا شفعة في جميع الدار عند أبي حنيفة ، وقالا : تجب في حصة الألف إذ فيها فيوجب الشفعة فيها، بخلاف الهبة التي لا يكون مشروطاً بشرط الـعـوض في صلب العقد، وإن دفع العوض في المقابلة لا يكون بيعاً انتهاء.
And if [the dower] is compensated with some wealth, such as when a man marries a woman on [the condition of giving] a house provided she returns one thousand [dirhams] to him, then there is no shuf'ah in the entire house according to Abu Hanifa, and the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: Shuf'ah is obligatory in the share of the thousand because there is consideration, so it necessitates shuf'ah in it, unlike the gift that is not conditioned with compensation in the contract, and even if compensation is paid in exchange it is not a sale. End.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific case: a man marries a woman and gives a house as dower, but with the condition that the woman gives him one thousand dirhams. In other words, the dower consists of a house and cash consideration from the wife. The question is whether a co-owner (shafi') can claim shuf'ah in the house portion. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, no shuf'ah exists in the entire house because the transaction is not a pure sale; the house is being exchanged for something that includes both the marriage contract and cash, which is not the standard sale (bay'). According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn), shuf'ah does apply to the portion that corresponds to the cash amount (the one thousand dirhams), because that part is effectively a sale. They distinguish this from a gift (hibah) without a condition of compensation in the contract, which remains a gift and not a sale, even if compensation is later paid. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) on this issue is the view of the Sahibayn, as is often the case when they differ from Abu Hanifa regarding partial shuf'ah.

Key Terms
أبو حنيفة (Abu Hanifa): The eponym of the Hanafi school; his view is that the transaction is primarily a marriage, not a sale.
صاحبين (Sahibayn): Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, Abu Hanifa's two main disciples.
الشفعة (al-shuf'ah): The right of a co-owner to purchase the share sold to an outsider, often translated as pre-emption.
مهر (mahr): Dower; the payment from husband to wife upon marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Shuf'ah in the entire house.
B) No shuf'ah in any part.
C) Shuf'ah in the portion corresponding to the cash.
D) Shuf'ah in the house only if the cash is more than the house.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when a dower includes a cash payment from the wife, the mufta bihi is that shuf'ah applies proportionally to the cash portion.

Attending a Wedding Feast with Forbidden Entertainment
Ruling on invited person finding play/singing at wedding

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for a muqtadi and an ordinary person when invited to a wedding feast with forbidden entertainment, and apply the Hanafi principle of avoiding endorsing sin.

Core Idea
A muqtadi must leave a wedding with haram entertainment; an ordinary person may eat but should avoid if forewarned.

Wedding feasts (walima) are occasions of joy, but what if they include forbidden play or singing? This lesson from al-Hidayah distinguishes between the obligations of a religious leader (muqtadi) and a regular invitee. The ruling rests on the principle that one's presence can imply approval of sin.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of invitees
The passage distinguishes between a muqtadi (a religious leader followed by others) and an ordinary person (ghair). The ruling differs for each.
2
State the ruling for each
For the muqtadi: if he finds forbidden entertainment and cannot stop it, he must leave entirely (yakhruj al-batta). For others: they may sit and eat, but they should not attend if they knew beforehand.
The muqtadi's presence implies approval, so he is obligated to leave.
3
Understand the evidence from Abu Hanifa
Abu Hanifa once endured such a situation before he became a leader. His endurance shows that even a muqtadi may stay if he cannot prevent it, but the preferred ruling (mufta bihi) is to leave.
The qawl of Abu Hanifa indicates the prohibition of all such entertainment.
4
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Consider a muqtadi invited to a walima where there is singing with musical instruments. He cannot stop it. He must leave. If an ordinary person knows beforehand, he should not attend; if he goes unknowingly, he may eat but should be cautious.
This ruling is from the chapter of Karahiyyah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومقتدي دُعِي إلى وليمةٍ فَوَجَدَ ثَمة لعباً أو غناءً لا يَقْدِرُ على منعِهِ يخرج البتة، وغيره إن قعد وأكل جاز، ولا يحضر إن عَلِمَ من قبل، وقال أبو حنيفة : ابتليت بهذا مرة فصبرت، وذا قبل أن يقتدى به، ودلّ قوله على حرمة كل الملاهي؛ لأن الابتلاء بالمحرم يكون. قوله : لأن الابتلاء بالمحرّم يكون...الخ؛ يعني إنّ الابتلاء لا يكون إلا بالشر بشهادة تقديم الظرف. قاله الجلبي.
And a muqtadi (follower) who is invited to a wedding feast and finds there play or singing and is unable to prevent it, he must leave entirely. As for others, if they sit and eat, it is permissible. And he should not attend if he knew beforehand. Abu Hanifa said: I was tested with this once and I endured, and that was before he became a leader. And his statement indicates the prohibition of all forms of entertainment, because being tested with the forbidden occurs. His statement: 'because being tested with the forbidden occurs... etc'; meaning that being tested only occurs with evil, as evidenced by the precedence of the adverb. Said by al-Jalabi.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling of a person invited to a wedding feast (walima) where there is play (la'b) or singing (ghina') that is forbidden. It distinguishes between two types of invitees: a muqtadi (a religious leader or one followed) and an ordinary person. The muqtadi, if he finds such entertainment and cannot stop it, must leave immediately (yakhruj al-batta). This is because his presence may be seen as approval, and he is expected to enjoin good. An ordinary person may sit and eat, but it is better not to attend if he knew beforehand. If he attends and eats, it is permissible. The text then quotes Abu Hanifa, who said he once endured such a situation before he became a leader, indicating that even a muqtadi may endure if he cannot prevent it, but the preferred ruling is to leave. The statement of Abu Hanifa is taken as evidence that all forms of musical entertainment are prohibited (haram), because being tested with them implies they are sinful. The commentator al-Jalabi explains that 'the test' here means being faced with evil, referencing the grammatical structure. This ruling is from the book of Karahiyyah and reflects the Hanafi position that attending gatherings with haram entertainment is impermissible for leaders, and caution is advised for others.

Key Terms
مقتدي (muqtadi): A person who is followed as a leader in religion, such as a scholar or imam.
الملاهي (al-malahi): Forms of entertainment, specifically musical instruments and idle play that are forbidden.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He may sit and eat, but should be silent.
B) He must leave immediately.
C) He may stay and try to stop it gently.
D) He can only leave if others also leave.

Key Takeaway
A muqtadi must leave a wedding with haram entertainment; an ordinary person should avoid if foreknowledge, otherwise may eat.

Ruling on Attending a Wedding Feast with Impermissible Entertainment
Ruling on wedding feast attendance with prohibited entertainment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a muqtada bih and a non-muqtada bih regarding attending a walimah with prohibited entertainment, and apply the distinction between wajib and mubah in this context.

Core Idea
A muqtada bih must leave a feast with singing; a non-muqtada may stay because the sunnah of accepting invitations outweighs the innovation.

In this passage, we examine the delicate balance between fulfilling the sunnah of attending a wedding feast and avoiding participation in prohibited activities. The Hanafi school provides a nuanced ruling that distinguishes between a public figure (muqtada bih) and an ordinary person, based on the principle of avoiding setting a bad example. We will explore the evidence and the reasoning behind the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
The text divides invitees into muqtada bih (one followed by others) and non-muqtada bih. Recognize each category's legal consequences.
Muqtada bih includes religious leaders, scholars, and those with public influence.
2
Determine the ruling based on prior knowledge and ability to prevent
If the invitee knows beforehand about the entertainment, attendance is prohibited. If he discovers it after arrival, he must prevent it if able. If unable, his status decides: muqtada bih must leave; non-muqtada may sit and eat.
This is a conditional ruling: multiple factors affect the outcome.
3
Understand the dalil: the hadith on accepting invitations
The hadith from Muslim commands answering the wedding invitation, with a threat of disobedience. This establishes the invitation as a sunnah with the strength of wajib.
The Hanafi principle 'al-amr yufidu al-wujub' is applied here.
4
Analyze the dispute: sunnah vs. bid'ah
Some scholars argue that the sunnah should not be upheld if it involves enduring a bid'ah. The Hanafi response is that the sunnah here is in the strength of wajib due to the threat, so it is not abandoned.
This is a key example of weightage in Hanafi usul.
5
Derive the broader principle: all musical instruments are haram
From Abu Hanifa's personal experience and the use of the term 'play and singing', the text deduces that all musical instruments (malahi) are prohibited.
This is a strong stance typical of the Hanafi school.

A person who is followed is invited to a wedding feast and finds there play or singing that he is not able to prevent, he leaves absolutely. Another person, if he sits and eats, it is permissible. His saying: because answering the invitation is a sunnah; as Muslim narrated in al-Nikah from Abu Hurayrah that the Prophet said: 'The worst food is the food of the wedding feast; he who does not accept the invitation has disobeyed Allah and His Messenger.' His saying: 'I was tested' indicates prohibition... the issue indicates that all musical instruments are prohibited, because Muhammad (the narrator?) used the term play and singing in his statement 'he found there play and singing', and play is amusement, and it is forbidden.

Commentary
This passage covers the ruling for a person invited to a wedding feast where there is play or singing (lahw or ghina). If the person is a muqtada bih (one who is followed by others in religious practice), he must leave immediately to avoid being an example of condoning sin. If he is not a muqtada bih, he may sit and eat if he did not know beforehand and cannot prevent it. However, if he knew in advance, he should not attend. The commentary elaborates that if he discovers the entertainment after arrival and can prevent it, he must do so. If unable, the muqtada bih must leave. For others, sitting and eating is permissible because the invitation is a sunnah and should not be abandoned due to a mere innovation. This is compared to attending a funeral prayer even if there is wailing (niyahah). The text then quotes a hadith emphasizing the obligation to accept wedding invitations. It also discusses the analogy between sunnah and fard, concluding that the sunnah here is in the strength of wajib due to the threat of punishment. Finally, Abu Hanifa's personal experience is cited to deduce that all musical instruments (malahi) are haram.

Key Terms
مقتدى (muqtada bih): A person who is followed or taken as an example by others in religious matters.
وليمة (walimah): A wedding feast or banquet given by the groom.
لهو (lahw): Play, amusement, or entertainment that distracts from religious obligations; often synonymous with la'ib.
الملاهي (al-malahi): Musical instruments or objects of amusement; anything that distracts from Allah.

Permissibility of Looking at One's Wife and Halal Female Slave
Permits looking at wife and halal slave's entire body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on looking at one's wife and halal female slave, citing the primary proof and identifying the exceptions.

Core Idea
Intimacy permitted implies looking is even more so, except for those prohibited.

This lesson explores the Hanafi position on a man's gaze towards his wife and his halal female slave. While the general rule requires lowering the gaze from non-mahram women, the text establishes an exception based on the permissibility of marital intimacy. We will examine the proof from the hadith and the logical reasoning, as well as the important condition that 'halal' excludes women with whom intercourse is forbidden.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its scope
The passage states that a man may look at his wife and halal female slave up to their private parts. This is an absolute permission covering all body parts, with or without desire.
2
Locate the primary proof
The author cites a saying attributed to Allah (likely a hadith): 'Lower your gaze except from your female slave and your wife.' Analyze how this exception proves the permissibility.
The exact wording may not be a Quranic verse but conveys the meaning.
3
Understand the logical reasoning
The text argues that since touching and intercourse are permissible, looking is even more permissible (awla bil-jawaz). Grasp this argument of precedence.
The reasoning is based on qiyas meaning: what is less intimate (looking) is allowed if the more intimate act is allowed.
4
Note the exception for etiquettes
Despite permissibility, the text advises modesty based on a hadith: couples should not be completely naked. Recognize the distinction between legal permissibility and recommended behavior.
This does not change the ruling; it is adab.
5
Apply the restriction of 'halal'
Jilabi notes that 'halal' excludes women with whom intercourse is prohibited (e.g., mahrams, polytheist women, married female slaves). Understand how the ruling changes for these categories.
The permissibility of looking is directly linked to the permissibility of intercourse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ومن عرسه وأمته الحلال.... الخ ؛ أي ينظر الرجل من عرسه وأمته الحلال إلى فرجهما، وهذا إطلاق في النظر إلى سائر بدنها عن شهوة وغير شهوة. والأصل فيه قوله الله : « غض بصرك إلا عن أمتك وامرأتك»(۱) ؛ ولأن ما فوق ذلك من المسيس والغشيان مباح ، فالنظر أولى بالجواز إلا أن الأدب أن لا ينظر كل واحد منهما إلى عورة صاحبه، لما روى الطبراني في «معجمه»: عن أبي أمامة ه قال : قال رسول الله : «إذا أتى أحدكم أهله فليستر ما استطاع، ولا يجردان كجرد العيرين» (٢). (4) قال جلبي : احترز بقيد الحلّ عن المجوسية أو المشركة أو أمه أو أخته من الرضاع، أو أم امرأته أو بنتها ؛ لأن حكمها حكم أمة الغير في النظر إليها ؛ لأن إباحة النظر إلى جميع البدنِ مبنيّة على حلّ الوطء، فينتفى بانتفائه، وقد عرفت بما ذكرنا أن الأمة التي أنحكت للغير حكمُها في باب النظرِ حكم أمة الغير؛ لوجود حرمة الوطء فيها ما دامت منكوحة له، كما لا يخفى.
His statement: 'And from his wife and his halal female slave... etc.' means that a man looks at his wife and his halal female slave up to their private parts. This is an absolute permission to look at all of her body, with desire or without. The basis for this is the saying of Allah: 'Lower your gaze except from your female slave and your wife.' And because what is above that, i.e., touching and intercourse, is permissible, so looking is even more permissible, except that etiquettes dictate that neither should look at the other's private parts, as narrated by al-Tabarani in his Mu'jam from Abu Umamah: The Messenger of Allah said: 'When one of you goes to his wife, let him cover as much as possible, and they should not be completely naked like two donkeys.' (4) Jilabi said: He restricted with the condition of 'halal' to exclude a Magian or polytheist woman, or his mother or sister from breastfeeding, or the mother of his wife or her daughter; because their ruling is like that of another's female slave in looking at them, because the permissibility of looking at the entire body is based on the permissibility of intercourse, so it ceases with its prohibition. And you have known from what we mentioned that the female slave who is married to another has the same ruling as another's female slave in the chapter of looking, due to the prohibition of intercourse with her as long as she is married to him, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling with proof concerning a man looking at his wife and his halal female slave. The matn states that a man may look at his wife and halal female slave up to their private parts. The commentator explains this as an absolute permission to look at all parts of the body, whether with desire or not, unlike the general rule for foreign women. The primary proof is the Quranic command to lower the gaze except from one's wife and female slave. The reasoning is that since intimate relations are permitted, looking is even more permissible. However, there is an etiquetical recommendation not to look directly at the private parts, based on a hadith from Abu Umamah. The commentator Jilabi adds an important nuance: the condition 'halal' excludes those women with whom intercourse is not permissible, such as a Magian or polytheist woman, or certain mahram relationships like a foster mother or sister, or the wife's mother or daughter; these are treated like a stranger's female slave. Similarly, a female slave who is married to someone else is also treated like a stranger's female slave because intercourse is prohibited with her.

Key Terms
عرس ('urs): Wife; the woman a man is married to.
أمة حلال (amat halal): A female slave with whom intercourse is permissible (i.e., who is not married to another and is halal for the master).
فرج (farj): Private parts (genitals).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Looking at Wife and Halal Slave: Ruling and Proof
Ruling on looking at spouse's and slave's private parts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on looking at a spouse's or halal slave's private parts and explain the proof and the recommended conduct.

Core Idea
Looking at spouse's private parts is permissible, but the Prophet advised covering.

This passage from al-Wiqayah addresses a common question: what may a man look at of his wife or female slave? The Hanafi school rules that it is absolutely permissible to look at the entire body, including the private parts, because the Qur'anic command to lower the gaze makes an exception for them. However, the Prophet's guidance encourages modesty even between spouses. This lesson examines the ruling, its proof, and the nuanced distinction between permissible and recommended conduct.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
The act is looking at the private parts of one's wife or halal slave. The ruling is mubah (permissible) based on explicit texts.
2
Examine the Primary Proof
The proof is a hadith: 'Guard your 'awrah except from your wife and your female slave.' The exclusion indicates permissibility.
The hadith is sahih and transmitted via multiple routes.
3
Consider the Secondary Rationale (Qiyas)
Since intercourse is halal, looking, which is less severe, is also halal by priority (al-wajh al-awla).
4
Note the Recommended Conduct (Adab)
Despite permissibility, it is adab (recommended) to avoid looking at each other's private parts, based on the hadith advising covering during intimacy.
5
Identify the Condition for Halal
The permissibility is conditional on the woman being 'halal'—i.e., a wife or one's own female slave. It excludes those with temporary prohibition (e.g., foster relatives, polytheists).
Jalabi explains that the ruling is built on the permissibility of intercourse.

This passage states the ruling that a man may look at his wife and his halal female slave's entire body, including private parts, without restriction of desire. The passage also includes a discussion on the ruling of the thigh and knee as 'awrah, and the degrees of punishment for uncovering them.

أدب (adab): Recommended conduct; not obligatory but encouraged.

Looking at Mahram Relatives: Permissible Body Parts
Ruling on looking at mahram relatives and which body parts are permissible

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the body parts of a mahram woman that a man may look at, and identify the condition that makes it prohibited.

Core Idea
Looking at a mahram’s head, face, chest, shin, and upper arm is permissible if safe from desire; otherwise forbidden.

This passage from the Hanafi tradition specifies which parts of a mahram relative’s body may be viewed. The ruling is derived from Quran 24:31, which permits the display of adornment to close relatives. The Hanafi school extends this permission to areas typically covered by work clothes, provided there is no risk of desire. Understanding this ruling helps clarify the boundaries of modesty within the family.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is looking at a mahram relative. The ruling is mubah (permissible) under the condition of safety from desire. If desire is present, it becomes haram.
The passage states: 'إن أمن شهوته وإلا فلا'
2
Locate the dalil
The Quranic verse 24:31 is cited: 'وَلَا يُبْدِينَ زِينَتَهُنَّ إِلَّا لِبُعُولَتِهِنَّ...' (And let them not display their adornment except to their husbands...). The permissible parts are the places where adornment is worn.
The commentator mentions 'والأصل فيه قوله تعالى'
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The verse permits display of adornment to mahram. Hanafi jurists interpret 'adornment' as the places where jewelry or ornament is typically worn: head, face, chest, shin, upper arm, and by extension forearm and ear.
These are the parts commonly visible in work clothes.
4
Note the condition and exceptions
The condition is safety from desire. If one fears desire, looking is forbidden. The back, belly, and thigh are explicitly excluded because they are not places of adornment and their coverage is necessary to avoid fitnah.
The text says 'ولا إلى الظهر والبطن والفخذ'
5
Compare with the ruling for a slave of another
The same permissible parts apply to a female slave owned by another (amah ghayrihi) due to the necessity of seeing her in work clothes.
The passage: 'فإن حكم أمة الغير حكم المحرم'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن محرمه إلى الرأس ، والوجه والصدرِ والسَّاقِ والعضد إن أمن شهوته وإلا فلا ، ولا إلى الظهر والبطن والفخذ كامة غيره) فَإِنَّ حكم أمة الغيرِ حكمُ المَحْرِمِ لضرورة رؤيتها في ثياب المهنة. (وما حل نظراً منهما ، حل مساً ، وله مس ذلك إن أراد شراءها" وإن
خاف شهوته، وأمةٌ بلغت لا تعرض في إزار واحد، ومن الأجنبية إلى وجهها [١] قوله : ومن محرمه إلى الرأس.... الخ ; والأصل فيه قوله لا : وَلَا يُبْدِينَ زِينَتَهُنَّ إِلَّا ) الخ ، والمراد والله أعلم مواضع الزينة ، وهي ما ذكره المصنّف ويدخل في ذلك الساعدُ والإذن من مواضع الزينة.
بخلاف الظهر والبطن والفخذ ; لأنها ليست من مواضع الزينة ; ولأن بعض المحارم يدخل على البعض من غير استئذان والمرأة في بيتها في ثياب الخدمة عادة، فلو حرم النظر إلى هذه المواضع أدى إلى الحرج.
And from his mahram: to the head, face, chest, shin, and upper arm if he is safe from desire, otherwise not; and not to the back, belly, and thigh, like a slave of another. For the ruling of a slave of another is like the ruling of a mahram due to the necessity of seeing her in work clothes. And whatever is permissible to look at from them, it is permissible to touch. And he may touch that if he intends to buy her, even if he fears desire. And a female slave who has reached puberty should not be displayed in a single garment. And from the non-mahram woman: to her face. ... The basis for this is His saying: 'And let them not display their adornment except...' (Quran 24:31). The meaning, Allah knows best, is the places of adornment, which are what the author mentioned, and that includes the forearm and the ear. Unlike the back, belly, and thigh because they are not places of adornment, and because some mahram enter upon each other without permission, and the woman is in her house in service clothes usually; so if looking at these parts were forbidden, it would lead to hardship.
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage details the ruling on looking at mahram relatives (those permanently forbidden in marriage). The author lists permissible body parts: head, face, chest, shin, and upper arm. The condition is that one is safe from desire; if desire is present, it is not permissible. The back, belly, and thigh are not permissible, similar to looking at a slave of another. The proof is the Quranic verse (24:31) which commands women not to display their adornment except to specified relatives; the permissible parts are those where adornment is typically worn. The commentator explains that the forearm and ear are included. The rationale for allowing these parts is necessity because mahram relatives enter without permission and women wear work clothes at home; forbidding would cause hardship. This lesson also introduces the ruling on touching: what is permissible to look at is permissible to touch, which will be elaborated in the next lesson.

Key Terms
محرم (mahram): A relative with whom marriage is permanently forbidden due to blood, suckling, or marriage ties.
شهوة (shahwah): Desire; if one fears arousal, looking is forbidden.
أمة غيره (amah ghayrihi): A female slave owned by someone else; the ruling for looking at her is similar to mahram in terms of permissible parts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which body parts of a mahram woman is a man allowed to look at if safe from desire?
A) Only face and hands
B) Head, face, chest, shin, upper arm
C) Entire body except private parts
D) Only face

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits looking at a mahram's head, face, chest, shin, and upper arm, provided one is safe from desire; the back, belly, and thigh are forbidden.
