Ruling on Looking at Non-Mahram Woman's Face and Hands
Explains permissibility of looking at a non-mahram woman's face and hands, with exception for need.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on looking at a non-mahram woman's face, hands, and feet, and identify the exceptions based on necessity.

Core Idea
Looking at a non-mahram is haram except the face and hands when needed; the foot is always forbidden in the relied-upon view.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah clarifies the Hanafi position on looking at a non-mahram woman's body parts. It distinguishes between the general prohibition and exceptional cases where necessity permits looking at the face and hands. The foot remains a point of internal debate within the school, with the relied-upon view prohibiting it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its general ruling
Determine what the passage rules on: looking at a non-mahram woman's face, hands, and feet. Note that the default ruling is haram without necessity.
The passage begins with 'وكفيها فقط' indicating the face and hands are the subject.
2
Locate the dalil and its type
The passage argues using istihsan (juristic preference). It contrasts the prayer ruling (foot not 'awra) with ordinary looking, citing necessity as the distinguishing factor.
The dalil is implicit: necessity (in prayer) does not transfer to non-mahram looking.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Explain how the istihsan applies: in prayer, uncovering the foot is excused due to need; for a non-mahram, there is no such need to look at the foot, so the analogy is overturned.
The phrase 'قلنا : في الصلاة ضرورة' highlights the key distinction.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the foot
Abu Hanifa held that looking at the foot is permissible because it is not 'awra. However, the relied-upon (Zahir al-Riwayah) view prohibits it.
This ikhtilaf is recorded in the passage: 'وعن أبي حنيفة له : أَنه يحل النظر إلى قدمها'.
5
Identify the exceptions for the face and hands
Even without desire, looking at the face is permissible only for specific needs: judge, witness, marriage, purchase of a slave woman, medical treatment. With desire, these needs still permit it.
The passage lists: 'كقاض يحكم، وشاهد يشهد عليها، ومن يريد نكاح امرأة أو شراء أُمة، ورجل يداويها'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكفيها فقط، هذا في ظاهر الرواية، وعن أبي حنيفة له : أَنه يحل النظر إلى قدمها، وقد مر في «كتابِ الصَّلاة»: أنّ القدم ليست بعــورة، قلنا : في الصلاة ضرورة، وليس في نظرِ الأجنبي إلى القدم ضرورة بخلاف الوجه والكف، (وكذا السيدة، فإنَّها في النظر إلى قدميها كالأجنبية. (فإن خاف) : أي الشهوة، لا ينظرُ إلى وجهها إلا لحاجة كقاض يحكم، وشاهد يشهد عليها ، ومن يريد نكاح امرأة أو شراء أُمة ، ورجل يداويها)، فإنَّ لهؤلاء يحل لهم النظر مع خوفِ الشُّهوة للحاجة ، فينظر إلى موضع مرضها بقدرِ الضرورة.
And only her hands [and face]. This is in the apparent narration (Zahir al-Riwayah). And from Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him): that it is permissible to look at her foot. And it has passed in the Book of Prayer that the foot is not 'awra. We said: in prayer there is necessity, and for a non-mahram looking at the foot there is no necessity, unlike the face and hand. (Likewise the female master (sayyidah), she is like a non-mahram in looking at her feet. (If he fears desire): meaning desire, he does not look at her face except for a need, such as a judge passing a ruling, a witness testifying against her, and one who wants to marry a woman or buy a slave woman, and a man who treats her medically. For these, it is permissible to look despite fear of desire because of need. So he looks at the place of her illness to the extent of necessity.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof, expanding on the permissibility of looking at a non-mahram woman. It states the Zahir al-Riwayah position: only the face and hands are permissible to look at (to avoid fitna). However, regarding the foot, there is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: Abu Hanifa permitted looking at the foot, but the relied-upon view (Zahir al-Riwayah) forbids it because there is no necessity, unlike in prayer where covering the foot is not required due to necessity. The passage then addresses the case of the 'sayyidah' (a female owner of a male slave): she is like a non-mahram woman regarding her feet, so looking is prohibited. Then it discusses exceptions when one fears desire: if there is a legitimate need (judgment, testimony, marriage, purchase, or medical treatment), it is permissible to look at the face to the extent required. The ruling is based on istihsan, balancing necessity against the general prohibition.

Key Terms
الأجنبية (al-ajnabiyyah): A woman who is not a mahram (unmarriageable kin) to the man.
السيدة (al-sayyidah): A female owner of a male slave; in this context, she is treated like a non-mahram in the ruling of looking at her feet.
الكف (al-kaf): The hand including the palm and up to the wrist; along with the face, it is excluded from the 'awra for women but looking at it by a non-mahram is still restricted.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is it permissible for a man to look at a non-mahram woman's face even if he fears desire?
A) Always, because the face is not 'awra.
B) Only if there is a legitimate need such as giving testimony or medical treatment.
C) Never, because fear of desire always prohibits looking.
D) Only for a judge but not for a witness.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, looking at a non-mahram woman's face and hands is haram without necessity, but becomes permissible for legitimate needs; looking at the foot is prohibited according to the relied-upon view.

Ruling on Women Looking at Women and Men
States the ruling for women looking at women and conditions for looking at men.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on women looking at other women, identify the permissible extent, and explain the condition for women looking at men.

Core Idea
A woman may look at another woman like a man looks at a man; she may look at a man only if safe from desire.

The Hanafi school establishes that women may look at other women to the same extent that men may look at men—this includes the face, hands, and feet for necessity. The commentary further clarifies that women may also look at men, but only when there is no fear of fitna (temptation). This lesson unpacks these rulings based on the text of 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage states that a woman may look at another woman like a man looks at a man. This means the ruling is mubah (permissible) within the same limits: the 'awra of a woman in front of another woman is between the navel and knee, so looking at face, hands, and feet is allowed.
The analogy is to the permissibility of men looking at men.
2
Understand the condition for looking at men
Women may look at non-mahram men only if they are safe from desire (amn al-shahwa). If desire is feared, looking becomes haram.
This condition is derived from the phrase 'in aminat shahwataha'.
3
Consider the practical application in bathhouses
The commentary uses the example of women's bathhouses (hamam) to show that necessity justifies uncovering, and custom supports this. Women need baths for adornment, unlike men who can wash in rivers.
The ruling is not absolute; it is conditioned on necessity and absence of fitna.
4
Contrast with common misconceptions
Some people mistakenly believe women should be prevented from entering bathhouses, but the Hanafi school permits it based on custom and greater need.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتنظر المرأة من المرأة كالرِّجلِ من الرّجل، وكذا من الرجل إن أمنت شهوتها ، والخصي والمجبوب والمخنث في النظر إلى الأجنبية كالفحل. [١]قوله : كالرجل....الخ ؛ يعني ما جاز للرجل أن ينظر إليه من الرجل، جاز للمرأة أن تنظر إليه من المرأة بوجوه من جملتها تحقق الضرورة إلى الانكشاف فيما بينهن. قال في «النهاية»: أي في الحمام وهذا دليل على أنهن لا يمنعن عن الدخول في الحمام، خلافاً لما يقوله بعض الناس ؛ لأنَّ الـعـرف الظاهر في جميع البلدان بـنـاءُ الحمامات للنساء وتمكينهن من دخول الحمامات دليل على صحة ما قلنا، وحاجة النساء إلى دخول الحمامات فوق حاجة الرجل إليه ؛ لأن المقصود تحصيل الزينة، والمرأة إلى هذا أحوج، ويتمكن الرجلُ من الاغتسال في الأنهار والحياض، والمرأة لا تتمكن من ذلك [٢] قوله : والمخنث...الخ ؛ أي الذي يفعل الرديء، يعني يمكن من نفسه فحلاً ليجامعه، احتراز عن المخنث الذي في أعضائه لين وتكسر بأصل الخلقة، ولا يشتهي النساء، فإنّه رخّص بعض مشايخنا في تركِ مثله مع ا النساء. قاله الجلبي
And a woman looks at a woman like a man looks at a man. Likewise from a man if she is safe from her desire. And the eunuch (khasii), the castrated (majbub), and the effeminate (mukhanath) in looking at a non-mahram woman are like a normal man (fahl). [1] His saying (qawluhu): 'like a man...' etc.: meaning what is permissible for a man to look at from a man is permissible for a woman to look at from a woman, among the aspects of which is the necessity of uncovering among themselves. He said in al-Nihaya: i.e., in the bathhouse, and this is evidence that they are not prevented from entering the bathhouse, contrary to what some people say; because the apparent custom in all countries of constructing baths for women and allowing them to enter baths is evidence of the correctness of what we say, and women's need to enter baths is greater than men's need for it, because the purpose is to obtain adornment, and women are more in need of that, while men can wash in rivers and tanks, but women cannot do that. [2] His saying: 'and the effeminate...' etc.: i.e., the one who does bad deeds, meaning he makes himself available as a male to be copulated with, in contrast to the effeminate who has softness and bending in his limbs due to his original creation and does not desire women; some of our scholars permitted leaving such a one with women. Al-Jalabi said it.
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with commentary, addressing three distinct rulings. First, the ruling that a woman may look at another woman to the same extent that a man may look at a man (i.e., places of necessity like face, hands, and feet are permissible, but not the 'awra). The commentary explains that this includes situations like in a bathhouse, where women have a legitimate need to uncover; custom supports this. Second, a woman may look at a man if she is safe from desire (i.e., no fear of fitna); otherwise it is prohibited. Third, the ruling for a eunuch (khasii), a castrated man (majbub), and an effeminate man (mukhanath) who submits to being used as a male: they are treated like a normal man regarding looking at non-mahram women, meaning it is prohibited unless there is necessity. An exception is made for an effeminate man who is naturally soft and does not desire women—some scholars permit him to be with women freely. This is all based on established Hanafi rulings without explicit textual proof here.

Key Terms
الخصي (al-khasii): A eunuch, a male whose testicles have been removed or destroyed, making him incapable of reproduction.
المجبوب (al-majbub): A male whose penis has been cut off; such a person is also treated as a normal man in rulings of looking.
المخنث (al-mukhanath): An effeminate man; in this context, one who actively submits to being used as a male for intercourse. A natural effeminate (with inherent softness and no desire for women) is an exception.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, when is a woman allowed to look at a non-mahram man?
A) Always, because she is not responsible for her gaze.
B) Only if she is safe from her own desire.
C) Only if he is a mahram.
D) Never, it is always haram.

Key Takeaway
A woman may look at another woman like a man looks at a man, and may look at a man only when safe from desire.

Ruling on Eunuchs, Castrated Men, and Effeminate Men Looking at Non-Mahram Women
States the ruling for eunuchs, castrated, and effeminate men looking at non-mahram women, with exception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that eunuchs, castrated men, and effeminate men who act immorally are treated as normal men regarding looking at non-mahram women, and identify the exception for the natural effeminate.

Core Idea
Eunuchs, castrated men, and immoral effeminates are like normal men: looking at non-mahram women is prohibited; only the natural effeminate without desire is an exception.

This lesson examines the Hanafi position on three types of men with altered physical conditions: eunuchs (khasii), castrated men (majbub), and effeminate men (mukhanath). The general ruling is that they are treated as normal men (fahl) in the prohibition of looking at non-mahram women. However, a distinct exception is made for an effeminate man who is naturally soft and does not desire women—some scholars permit him to be with women freely.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types and the general ruling
The text lists eunuch (khasii), castrated (majbub), and effeminate (mukhanath). The general ruling is that they are like a normal man (fahl) in looking at non-mahram women, meaning it is prohibited unless necessity.
The ruling is based on the assumption that desire may still exist.
2
Understand the definition of the effeminate (mukhanath) in this context
The commentary specifies that 'mukhanath' here refers to one who does bad deeds, meaning he makes himself available as a male for copulation. This is the immoral type.
This is contrasted with the natural effeminate.
3
Recognize the exception for the natural effeminate
An effeminate who has softness and bending in his limbs due to original creation and does not desire women is exempted. Some Hanafi scholars permit him to be with women without restriction.
This exception is a rukhsa (concession) based on his lack of desire.
4
Apply the ruling to practical scenarios
For example, a eunuch working in a women's gathering: he must lower his gaze and avoid non-mahram women. If a natural effeminate is present, the stricter scholars still consider him as a man, but some allow mixing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Ruling on Coitus Interruptus ('Azl) with Slave and Free Wife
A ruling with proof (dalil naqli) from hadith and rational argument distinguishing 'azl with slave and free women.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on 'azl with a slave woman and a free wife, and explain the dalil and reasoning for the difference.

Core Idea
The slave woman has no right to offspring; the free wife does — thus 'azl requires her permission.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya addresses a practical question in marital relations: when is coitus interruptus permissible? The answer depends on the woman's status and her rights. You will learn the ruling, the prophetic evidence, and the underlying legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its differential ruling
Understand what 'azl is and how the ruling differs based on the woman being a slave or free. Note that with a slave woman, it is permissible without permission; with a free wife, it requires her permission.
'Azl is withdrawal before ejaculation to prevent pregnancy.
2
Locate the primary dalil
Find the hadith cited: the Prophet said to a master of a slave woman, 'Practice 'azl with her if you wish.' This is the main textual evidence.
The hadith is from Sahih Muslim and other sources.
3
Analyse the wajh al-dalalah
Consider how the permission in the hadith indicates permissibility for the slave woman. The prohibition for the free wife is implied by contrast and by the principle that intercourse is her right.
The hadith explicitly permits for the slave; silence regarding the free wife combined with her rights yields the prohibition.
4
Understand the rationale (ta'lil)
The commentary explains that a free wife has the right to sexual satisfaction and offspring, whereas a slave woman has no such right. This difference justifies the distinction.
The rights-based approach is key in Hanafi juridical reasoning.
5
Note the scope and related rulings
A footnote adds a ruling on abortion: permissible before 120 days (before the soul is breathed). While not the main focus, it shows how the same principle of rights applies elsewhere.
This ruling is from al-Nahr and is a separate but related issue.

[1] His saying: 'and he may practice 'azl...' etc.: because he was prohibited from 'azl with a free woman except with her permission, and he said to the master of a slave woman: 'Practice 'azl with her if you wish' (3); and because intercourse is the right of the free woman for satisfying desire and obtaining offspring, and the slave woman has no right to intercourse. Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on coitus interruptus ('azl) with proof. The ruling differentiates between a slave woman and a free wife. With a slave woman, 'azl is permissible without her permission because she has no right to offspring or sexual satisfaction—her owner may use her as he wishes. With a free wife, 'azl is not permissible without her permission, as the Prophet (peace be upon him) prohibited it except with her consent. The commentary explains the reasoning: the free wife has a right to both sexual gratification and offspring, whereas the slave woman does not. The footnote adds a related ruling on abortion from al-Nahr, stating it is impermissible after 120 days (when the soul is breathed). The hadith from Sahih Muslim supports the permissibility with a slave woman.

Key Terms
العزل (al-'azl): Coitus interruptus: withdrawal before ejaculation during intercourse to prevent pregnancy.
الأمة (al-ummah): A female slave. In this context, the ruling concerns her owner's right to practice 'azl without her consent.
العرس (al-'urs): A free wife (literally 'bride'). She has full rights to intercourse and offspring.

Ruling on Coitus Interruptus ('Azl) with a Slave-Girl and Free Wife
States the permissibility of 'azl with slave-girl without permission and with wife with permission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on 'azl with a slave-girl and a free wife, identify the evidence, and explain the juristic reasoning for the distinction.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school permits 'azl with a slave-girl without permission but requires a free wife's consent.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains the Hanafi ruling on coitus interruptus. The ruling differs based on whether the woman is a free wife or a slave-girl, and the evidence comes from prophetic hadith and juristic reasoning. Let's examine the ruling, its proof, and the underlying rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the two categories
First, note that 'azl is defined as withdrawing before ejaculation to prevent pregnancy. The passage addresses two categories of women: free wife (ʿurs) and slave-girl (amah).
2
State the ruling for each category
For the slave-girl, 'azl is permissible without her permission. For the free wife, it is permissible only with her permission.
The text: 'يعزل عن أمته بلا إذنها، وعن عرسه به' – 'he performs 'azl with his slave-girl without her permission, and with his wife with her permission.'
3
Examine the dalil (evidence)
The commentator cites a hadith: the Prophet (ﷺ) prohibited 'azl with a free woman except with her permission, and told the owner of a slave-girl: 'اعزل عنها إن شئت' (Practice 'azl with her if you wish).
The hadith is from Sahih Muslim and other sources.
4
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
The explicit permission for the slave-girl indicates no consent needed, while the prohibition for the free woman (except with permission) shows consent is required. The distinction is drawn from the specific wording and context.
5
Understand the ta'lil (juristic reasoning)
The commentator explains: intercourse is a right of the free wife for fulfilling desire and obtaining offspring, so her consent is needed if 'azl denies that right. A slave-girl has no established right to intercourse from her master.
This reasoning (ta'lil) is based on the difference in marital rights between free wives and slave concubines.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويعزل عن أمته بلا إذنها، وعن عرسه به، العزل: أن يطأ فإذا قَرُبَ إلى الإنزال أخرج ذكره، ولا يُنزلُ في الفرج. [٣] قوله: ويعزل...الخ ; لأنه نهى عن العزل عن الحرّة إلا بإذنها، وقال لمولى أمة: «اعزل عنها إن شئت (۳) ; ولأن الوطء حقٌّ الحرّة قضاء للشهوة، وتحصيلاً للولد، ولا حق للأمة في الوطء ).
And he performs 'azl with his slave-girl without her permission, and with his wife with her permission. 'Azl is that he has intercourse and when he approaches ejaculation, he removes his penis and does not ejaculate inside the vagina. [3] His statement: 'and he performs 'azl...' etc.; because he (the Prophet) prohibited 'azl with a free woman except with her permission, and said to the owner of a slave-girl: 'Practice 'azl with her if you wish'; and because intercourse is the right of the free woman for fulfilling desire and obtaining offspring, while the slave-girl has no right to intercourse.
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage provides a Hanafi ruling on 'azl (coitus interruptus). The base ruling: a man may practice 'azl with his slave-girl (umm walad or concubine) without her permission, but with his free wife only with her consent. The commentator explains the reason: the Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade 'azl with a free woman unless she permits, but allowed it with a slave-girl, as narrated in Sahih Muslim. The juristic reasoning (ta'lil) is that intercourse is a right of the free wife for both gratification and obtaining children, so her consent is required if that right is to be partially negated. A slave-girl, however, has no established right to intercourse from her master, thus 'azl is permissible without permission. This distinction is based on the difference in marital rights between free wives and slave concubines in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
العزل (al-'azl): Coitus interruptus; the act of withdrawing the penis before ejaculation to prevent pregnancy.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Neither

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, 'azl requires the free wife's consent due to her right to intercourse and offspring, but not the slave-girl's, based on prophetic hadith and juristic reasoning.

Ruling on Abortion Before Ensoulment in Hanafi Fiqh
Refinement of Hanafi ruling on abortion before 120 days

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on abortion before 120 days, identify the intra-Hanafi dispute, and apply the conditions for permissibility.

Core Idea
Abortion before 120 days is permissible with excuse in the Hanafi school, but potential life is considered.

This passage from the hashiya tradition refines the Hanafi ruling on early abortion. It presents a dispute within the school between those who permit unconditionally before ensoulment and those who restrict it due to qiyas on potential life. By analyzing the sources, you will understand the conditions that make abortion permissible and how later scholars reconciled differing views.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling from al-Nahr
Find the explicit statement that abortion is permissible before any 'creation' is evident, interpreted as before 120 days (ensoulment). Note that this does not require husband's permission.
This is the starting point of the dispute.
2
Locate the opposing view from al-Khaniyya
Al-Khaniyya states, 'I do not say it is permissible,' using qiyas (analogy) to breaking a game bird's egg in the sanctuary, where potential life gives sanctity.
The basis is that the fluid in the womb has potential life.
3
Examine the exceptions and excuses
Ibn Wahban and al-Dhakhira introduce excuses such as the mother's milk drying up, which would permit abortion. Some scholars call it makruh without excuse.
This qualifies the absolute prohibition from al-Khaniyya.
4
Trace the analogical reasoning (qiyas)
The qiyas compares the fertilized fluid to a game bird's egg: both have potential life, so destruction incurs sin. The Hanafi response argues the analogy is weak before ensoulment.
This is a key point of contention in usul.
5
Apply Ibn Abidin's tarjih (preference)
Ibn Abidin reconciles by permitting abortion before 120 days when there is a valid excuse, due to 'corruption of times'. This is the mufta bihi (operative ruling).
The ruling is conditional: permissible with excuse, disliked without.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حكم إسقاط الحمل : قال في «النهر»: يباح إسقاط الحمل ما لم يتخلق منه شيء ولن يكون ذلك إلا بعد مئة وعشرين يوما، وهذا يقتضي أنهم أرادوا بالتخليق نفخ الروح وإلا فهو غلط لأن التخليق يتحقق بالمشاهدة قبل هذه المدة. وإطلاقهم يفيد عدم توقف جواز إسقاطها قبل المدة المذكورة على إذن الزوج. وفي (كراهة) (الخانية»: ولا أقول بالحل إذ المحرم لو كسر بيض الصيد ضمنه ; لأنه أصل الصيد فلما كان يؤاخذ بالجزاء فلا أقل من أن يلحقها إثم هنا إذا سقط بغير عذرها. قال ابن وهبان : ومن الأعذار أن ينقطع لبنها بعد ظهور الحمل وليس لأب الصبي ما يستأجر به الظئر ويخاف هلاكه. ونقل عن «الذخيرة : لو أرادت الإلقاء قبل مضي زمن ينفخ فيه الروح هل يباح لها ذلك أم لا ؟ اختلفوا فيه : وكان الفقيه علي بن موسى يقول : إنه يكره ، فإن الماء بعدما وقع في الرحم مآله الحياة فيكون له حكم الحياة كما في بيضة صيد الحرم ونحوه في «الظهيرية»، قال ابن وهبان فإباحة الإسقاط محمولة على حالة العذر، أو أنها لا تأثم إثم القتل. وبما في «الذخيرة» تبين أنهم ما أرادوا بالتحقيق إلا نفخ الروح وأن قاضي خان مسبوق بما مر من التفقه والله تعالى الموفق انتهى كلام «النهر». أخذ في «النهر من هذا ومما قدمه الشارح عن «الخانية والكمال أنه يجوز لها سد فم رحمها كما تفعله النساء مخالفاً لما بحثه في «البحر» من أنه ينبغي أن يكون حراماً بغير إذن الزوج قياساً على عزله بغير إذنها . قلت : لكن في البزازية أن له منع امرأته عن العزل .اهـ. قال ابن عابدين في رد المحتار (۳) : (١٧٦ : نعم النظر إلى فساد الزمان يفيد الجواز من الجانبين فما في «البحر» مبني على ما هو أصل المذهب ، وما في «النهر» على ما قاله المشايخ والله الموفق. لا
Ruling on aborting pregnancy: He said in al-Nahr: It is permissible to abort the pregnancy as long as nothing has been created from it, and that will not be except after one hundred and twenty days. This indicates that by 'creation' they meant ensoulment, otherwise it would be a mistake because creation is observed before this period. Their absolute expression indicates that the permissibility of aborting before the mentioned period does not depend on the husband's permission. And in al-Khaniyya: I do not say it is permissible, since if a pilgrim broke the egg of game, he would guarantee it because it is the origin of the game; if he is taken to task for compensation, then at least she will incur sin here if it falls without excuse. Ibn Wahban said: Among the excuses is that her milk dries up after pregnancy appears and the child's father cannot afford to hire a wet nurse and fears for the child's death. It is transmitted from al-Dhakhira: If she wants to abort before the time when the spirit is breathed, is that permissible for her or not? They differed: al-Faqih Ali ibn Musa said it is disliked, because after the water falls into the womb it is destined for life, so it has the ruling of life, like the egg of a game bird in the sanctuary and similar. Ibn Wahban said: The permissibility of abortion is conditioned upon a state of excuse, or that she does not incur the sin of killing. And by what is in al-Dhakhira it becomes clear that by 'creation' they meant only ensoulment, and that Qadikhan was preceded by the fiqh mentioned. And Allah is the giver of success. End of the words of al-Nahr. He adopted in al-Nahr from this and from what the commentator presented from al-Khaniyya and al-Kamal that it is permissible for her to block the mouth of her womb as women do, contrary to what was discussed in al-Bahr that it should be forbidden without the husband's permission by analogy to 'azl without her permission. I said: But in al-Bazzaziyya, he has the right to prevent his wife from 'azl. End. Ibn Abidin said in Radd al-Muhtar: Yes, looking at the corruption of the times indicates permissibility from both sides. What is in al-Bahr is based on the origin of the madhhab, and what is in al-Nahr is based on what the scholars said. And Allah is the giver of success. No.
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hashiya) discussing the Hanafi ruling on abortion (isqat al-haml). The core issue is whether aborting a pregnancy before ensoulment (120 days) is permissible. The author quotes from al-Nahr: abortion is permissible before creation (takhalluq) is evident, which is usually taken to mean before 120 days when the soul is breathed. The definition of 'creation' (takhalluq) is debated: some say it refers to ensoulment at 120 days, others note that physical formation begins earlier. The text then presents an ikhtilaf: al-Khaniyya states it is not permissible, using an analogy (qiyas) to breaking the egg of game in the sanctuary: the egg has potential life, so destroying it incurs sin. Ibn Wahban provides excuses that might permit abortion, such as the mother's milk drying up and inability to feed the infant. Some scholars like Ali ibn Musa say it is makruh (disliked) because the fluid in the womb has potential life. Ultimately, the relied-upon view is that abortion before ensoulment is permissible with a valid excuse, but not without. The passage also discusses the question of blocking the uterus after conception (سد فم رحمها) which is analogized to 'azl. The final opinion from Ibn Abidin notes that due to the corruption of times, permissibility may be granted from both sides. The overall lesson shows the tension within Hanafi fiqh between the general permissibility before ensoulment and the conservative view considering potential life.

Key Terms
إسقاط الحمل (isqat al-haml): Abortion; terminating a pregnancy before delivery.
نفخ الروح (nafkh al-ruh): Ensoulment; the moment when the soul is breathed into the fetus, occurring at around 120 days (four months).

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Permissible unconditionally.
C) Permissible with a valid excuse (e.g., mother's health).
D) Only permissible if the husband consents.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, abortion before 120 days is permissible with a valid excuse, though some scholars restrict it due to potential life.

Istibra' for a Purchased Slave-Girl: When Is It Required?
Expansion on the ruling of istibra' for acquired slave-girls, focusing on the case of a slave seller.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the general rule of istibra' for a purchased slave-girl and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the case when the seller is the buyer's own indebted slave.

Core Idea
Istibra' is wajib for every purchased slave-girl, except when the seller's status eliminates the need for ascertaining non-pregnancy.

This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah clarifies the ruling of istibra' (waiting period of one menstrual cycle) for a slave-girl acquired by purchase. The commentator expands on the base text by examining an edge case: what if the seller is a slave who is indebted? Here, Abu Hanifa and his two disciples differ. Understanding this dispute reveals how the Hanafi masters apply qiyas and attend to the underlying rationale (illa) of istibra', which is to ensure the slave-girl is not pregnant from a prior owner who had access to her.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Istibra' is the waiting period of one menstrual cycle for a slave-girl acquired by purchase or similar means. It is wajib (obligatory) according to the Hanafi school to ascertain that she is not pregnant from the previous owner.
Wajib, not fard, because the evidence is based on ijtihad (qiyas) and not a definitive text.
2
State the general rule from the base text
Whoever owns a slave-girl by purchase, bequest, inheritance, or similar must perform istibra'. This applies even if the seller is a woman, a slave, or the slave-girl's mahram (except a close blood relative).
The rule covers all acquisitions, not just purchase.
3
Examine the specific case of a slave seller
The passage focuses on when the seller is a slave. Two subcases: if the slave is another person's slave, istibra' is required by agreement. If the slave is the buyer's own slave and is indebted (madiyunan mustaghriq), there is disagreement.
The dispute is intra-Hanafi: between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad).
4
Follow the intra-Hanafi reasoning
Abu Hanifa: istibra' is required because the master owns the indebted slave's earnings, so the slave's possession does not guarantee lack of access. Sahibayn: if the slave does not own his earnings and can dispose of his own property, his possession is like his own (i.e., he is effectively a free owner); if she menstruated during his ownership, no further istibra' is needed.
The key factor is whether the slave's master owns his earnings (kasb).
5
Understand the underlying rationale
The dispute hinges on the illa (effective cause) of istibra': the possibility of recent sexual intercourse. If the slave is considered to have complete authority over the slave-girl (like a free owner), then her menstruation at his place suffices to prove non-pregnancy. If not, the buyer must wait a new cycle.
This exemplifies qiyas: comparing the slave's position to a free owner's.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
افصل في الاستبراء وغيرها (ومن ملك أمةً بشراء أو نحوه ) : كالوصية والإرث ونحوهما ، ولو بكراً ومشرية من امرأة، أو عبد أو محرمها ) : أي محرم الأمة، لكن غير ذي رحم محرم [١] قوله : أو عبد ... الخ ; أمّا من عبدِ غيره فبالاتفاق، وأما من عبد المشتري إذا كان مديوناً مستغرقاً ، فكذا عند أبي حنيفة ; لأنه يملك المولى حينئذ كسبه. وأما عندهما : إن حاضت عند العبد لا يلزم الاستبراء للمولى ; لأنه لا يملك كسبه، ويتصرَّفُ في ماله، فقبضه ،كقبضه، وإنّما عدّ العبد من هذا العداد ; لأنه ممن لا يملك شيئاً أصلاً، فضلاً عن الجارية ووطئها. قاله الجلبي
Chapter on istibra' and other matters: (And whoever owns a slave-girl by purchase or similar): such as bequest, inheritance, and the like, even if a virgin and purchased from a woman, or from a slave, or from her mahram): i.e., the mahram of the slave-girl, but not a close blood relative mahram. [1] His statement: 'or from a slave...' etc.; as for from another's slave, by agreement (istibra' is required). As for from the buyer's own slave, if he is indebted and his debt covers his entire earning, then similarly (istibra' is required) according to Abu Hanifa, because the master then owns his earnings. But according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): if she menstruated while with the slave, istibra' is not required for the master, because the slave does not own his earnings and disposes of his own property, so his possession is like his own possession. The slave is counted among these (categories) because he is someone who owns nothing at all, let alone a slave-girl and sex with her. Al-Jalabi said.
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on istibra' (waiting period) for a purchased slave-girl. The base text says that whoever acquires a slave-girl by purchase, bequest, etc., must perform istibra' (i.e., wait for one menstrual cycle to ensure she is not pregnant). This applies even if the seller is a woman, a slave, or the slave-girl's own mahram (but not a close blood relative mahram). The commentator then details the case when the seller is a slave. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa says if the slave who sells is the buyer's own slave who is insolvent (madiyunan mustaghriq), istibra' is still required because the master owns the slave's earnings. However, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say that if the slave does not own his earnings and can dispose of his own property, then his possession (as seller) is valid without istibra' because the slave essentially owns himself? Actually, the text says: if she menstruated while with the slave, istibra' is not required for the master because the slave does not own his earnings and disposes of his property, so his possession is like his own possession. This means the slave's sale is treated as valid and the buyer does not need to wait for a new istibra' because the slave-girl already had opportunity to menstruate with the slave owner. The ruling highlights the Hanafi principle that istibra' is to ascertain non-pregnancy, and the need depends on the likelihood of recent intercourse. The passage illustrates the application of qiyas and the positions of the early Hanafi masters.

Key Terms
الاستبراء (al-istibra'): The waiting period of one menstrual cycle for a slave-girl after acquisition to ensure she is not pregnant.
مديوناً مستغرقاً (madiyunan mustaghriq): Indebted to the extent that his entire earnings are covered by debt.
كسبه (kasbuhu): The slave's earnings; ownership of which determines the slave's financial independence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when is istibra' required for a purchased slave-girl?
A) Only if she was previously married.
B) In all cases of purchase, regardless of seller.
C) Only if she is a virgin.
D) Only if purchased from a non-Muslim.

Key Takeaway
Istibra' is wajib for every acquired slave-girl unless the seller's status removes the likelihood of recent intercourse, as debated among the Hanafi imams.

Istibra': Obligation and Rational Proof
Ruling on istibra' with rational proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on istibra' upon acquiring a slave-girl, explain the rational proof based on protecting lineage, and analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute about buying from an insolvent slave.

Core Idea
Istibra' is wajib to protect lineage, and the ruling is tied to the visible cause of new ownership, not actual knowledge.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah presents the ruling on istibra' (waiting period) when a person acquires a slave-girl. The ruling is wajib, and the proof is rational (hikmah): to prevent mixing of lineages. The text also addresses an objection and an intra-Hanafi disagreement. By working through this passage, you will learn how Hanafi jurists derive obligations from hidden wisdoms and how they handle exceptions within the school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage states that upon owning a slave-girl by purchase, inheritance, etc., intercourse is forbidden until istibra' is completed. Determine the legal category (wajib) and the condition (new ownership).
The ruling applies even to virgins and purchases from mahrams, showing the obligation is broad.
2
Locate the proof (dalil)
The proof is the wisdom of protecting respectable fluid (ma' muh'taram) from mixing. This is a rational proof (dalil aqli) based on preserving lineage. Note that the ruling is attached to the manifest cause: acquisition of ownership (istihdath al-milk).
The proof is not a direct text but a general principle of hikmah.
3
Analyze the objection and response
An objection is raised: if the master's non-intercourse is known, how can there be suspicion? The response: the seed could be from another man, and even a virgin can have womb occupation without loss of virginity. Understand that the law considers the category, not every individual case.
This shows how Hanafi usul handles hidden matters by attaching rulings to outward signs.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute
A sub-case is discussed: buying a slave-girl from one's own slave who is insolvent. Abu Hanifa requires istibra' because the master owns the slave's earnings; the Sahibayn do not because the slave does not own the girl. Identify the respective proofs and the thamarah (practical difference).
The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's view, as the master's ownership of the slave's earnings is affirmed.

Detailed discussion on istibra'. Whoever owns a slave-girl by purchase or similar (like will, inheritance, etc.), even if she is a virgin or bought from a woman, a slave, or her mahram, it is unlawful to have intercourse with her and its preliminaries until he performs istibra': one menstrual cycle for those who menstruate, one month for those with a monthly period, and until delivery for a pregnant one. The wisdom of istibra' is to know the emptiness of the womb to protect the respectable fluid from mixing. According to Abu Hanifa, it is due to actual occupation with prohibited fluid or suspicion of occupation. Since it is a hidden matter, the ruling is attached to a manifest thing, which is the acquisition of ownership. The objection that if the master's non-intercourse is known, how can the womb be suspected, is answered: the occupation need not be from the master; it could be from another. Also, the suspicion is present even in a virgin because occupation can occur without loss of virginity.
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah, a commentary on Al-Wiqayah, discusses the ruling of istibra' (waiting period to ensure the womb is clear) when a person acquires a new slave-girl. The ruling is a ruling_with_proof, as it provides the legal position and then justifies it with rational wisdom (hikmah). The passage begins by stating that upon ownership of a slave-girl by purchase, inheritance, gift, etc., intercourse is forbidden until istibra' is completed. This applies even if the girl is a virgin (which might suggest no previous intercourse) and even if bought from a woman, a slave, or the girl's own mahram (who cannot marry her). The commentary then notes an intra-Hanafi dispute: if the slave-girl is bought from a slave who is insolvent, Abu Hanifa holds that istibra' is required because the master owns the slave's earnings, while the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say it is not required because the slave does not own the girl. The main body then explains the wisdom behind istibra': it is to ensure the womb is free from another man's seed (respectable fluid) to prevent mixing of lineages. Since the actual state of the womb is hidden, the law attaches the obligation to a visible cause: the acquisition of a new slave-girl. If the master is known not to have had intercourse with her, the wisdom is not observed in each individual but in the general category. An objection is raised: if the master's non-intercourse is certain, how can there be suspicion? The answer is that the seed could be from someone else, and even in a virgin, occupation of the womb is possible without breaking the hymen. The passage also includes a lengthy digression on the permissibility of abortion and preventing pregnancy, discussing various scholarly opinions, but the core legal unit is the istibra' ruling.

Key Terms
استبراء (istibra'): A waiting period required upon acquiring a new slave-girl to ensure her womb is free from another man's pregnancy, observed by one menstrual cycle, one month, or until delivery.
ماء محترم (ma' muh'taram): Respectable fluid, i.e., the semen from a valid marriage or lawful intercourse, which should not be mixed with another.
استحداث الملك (istihdath al-milk): The acquisition of new ownership, which is the visible cause triggering the obligation of istibra'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the duration of istibra' for a pregnant slave-girl?
A) One menstrual cycle
B) One month
C) Until she delivers
D) No istibra' needed

Key Takeaway
Istibra' is wajib upon acquiring a slave-girl to protect lineage, and the obligation is tied to the visible cause of new ownership, even if the girl is a virgin or bought from a mahram.

Istibra' and the Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Does a Prior Menstruation Suffice?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the sufficiency of prior menstruation for istibra'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on istibra' for acquired slave women and explain why Abu Hanifa and Muhammad require a fresh istibra' even if the slave had a menstruation under previous ownership.

Core Idea
The cause of istibra' is new acquisition; the ruling cannot precede its cause.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya addresses a key practical question: when you acquire a female slave, is a menstruation she had before the transfer sufficient for istibra'? The Hanafi school has an internal disagreement on this very point. Let's examine the reasoning of the two sides and understand which position is relied upon for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule and its basis
The Prophet ﷺ gave a general ruling requiring istibra' for female captives, and by qiyas it extends to all means of ownership. Note that the ruling is general and not restricted by specific wisdoms.
This is based on the principle that preventing harm (here, mixing lineage) requires a general rule.
2
Understand the effective cause (ʿilla) of istibra'
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, the cause is the acquisition of ownership and possession (istihdath al-milk wa al-yad). The ruling is a consequence of this new legal relationship.
This cause is absent in cases like return of an absconding slave, so no istibra' is needed.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Yusuf disagrees: he says that if the slave had a menstruation under the previous owner, we are certain her womb is empty, so a new istibra' is not required. He analogizes to a divorced woman before consummation, who does not observe ʿiddah.
The key point is whether the ʿilla is new ownership or mere certainty of emptiness.
4
Examine the Hanafi response to Abu Yusuf
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad argue that the analogy is flawed. In divorce, the cause of ʿiddah (marriage and possible consummation) is removed; here, the cause (new ownership) is present and necessitates a fresh waiting period.
The ruling cannot precede its cause (al-hukm la yasbiq al-sabab).
5
Determine the mufta bihi position
The relied-upon position is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: a menstruation under previous ownership does not suffice; a new istibra' must be observed. This is the view adopted by later Hanafi masters.
Thus, in practice, one must always wait for a new cycle after acquisition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(ولم تكف حيضة ملكها فيها، ولا التي قبل القبض، ولا ولادة كذلك، وتجب في شراء أمةٍ إلا شقصاً هو له لا عند عود الآبقة، وردّ المغصوبة، والمستأجرة، وفك المرهونة). هذا عند الأعظم ومحمد ا خلافاً لأبي يوسف له، فإن عنده : تكفي تلك الحيضة للتيقن بفراغ الرحم، كما لو طلقها قبل الدخول لا يجب العدة كذلك. ولهما : إن السبب استحداث الملك واليد والحكم لا يسبق السبب. (لا عند عود الآبقة...الخ) لانعدام السبب، وهو استحداث الملك واليد.
For female captives [sabaya] are not free from including a virgin, a woman who has been captured, and the like. Despite this, the Prophet ﷺ gave a general ruling, not restricted by the wisdom. Just as He [Allah] explained the wisdom in the prohibition of wine by His saying: 'Satan only wants to cause...' the verse, so no one can say 'I drink it such that no enmity occurs and it does not prevent me from prayer.' When the harm is predominant in its prohibition, the Law prohibits it generally, because in specification there is obvious confusion and people's audacity such that the wisdom is lost. So when the ruling is established for captives generally, it is established for other causes of ownership likewise by analogy, for the cause is known. Then it is supported by consensus. (And a menstruation that occurred in her ownership does not suffice, nor the one before possession, nor a childbirth likewise. And it is obligatory in purchasing a slave woman except for a share that is his. Not upon the return of an absconding slave, return of a usurped one, a leased one, or redemption of a pawned one.) This is according to the Greatest Imam and Muhammad, contrary to Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on him. For in his view that menstruation suffices due to certainty of the womb's emptiness, just as if he divorced her before consummation, 'iddah is not obligatory likewise. And for them (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad): the cause is acquisition of ownership and possession, and the ruling does not precede the cause. (Not upon the return of an absconding slave... etc.) because the cause is absent, which is acquisition of ownership and possession.
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya discusses the ruling of istibra' (waiting for one menstrual cycle) when acquiring a female slave. The author first argues that the general ruling made by the Prophet ﷺ requiring istibra' for captives applies universally, not limited by specific wisdoms, because specification would lead to chaos. This general ruling is extended by qiyas to all other means of acquiring ownership (like purchase) because the effective cause (the possibility of pregnancy) is common. Then the passage mentions the rule: a menstruation that occurred while the slave was under the previous owner's ownership does not suffice for istibra', nor does a menstruation before the new owner takes possession, nor does a childbirth. Istibra' is required upon purchase except when one already owns a share (shiqs) and buys the rest; then no new istibra' is needed. Istibra' is not required upon the return of an absconding slave (about from Dar al-Islam), return of a usurped slave, return of a leased slave, or redemption of a pawned slave, because no new acquisition of ownership occurs. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa (al-A'zam) and Muhammad hold that a menstruation that occurred in the previous owner's ownership does not suffice, because the cause of istibra' is the acquisition of ownership and possession, and the ruling cannot precede its cause. Abu Yusuf argues that it does suffice because we are certain the womb is empty, analogous to a divorced woman before consummation who does not observe 'iddah. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.

Key Terms
الاستبراء (al-istibra'): The waiting period (usually one menstrual cycle) required before intercourse with a newly acquired female slave to ensure she is not pregnant from another.
السبايا (female captives taken in war): Female prisoners of war who become slaves, for whom the Prophet ﷺ established the ruling of istibra'.
استحداث الملك واليد (istihdath al-milk wa al-yad): The acquisition of ownership and physical possession, which is the effective cause ('illa) for istibra' according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the effective cause (ʿilla) for istibra' according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad?
A) The possibility of pregnancy from a previous man.
B) The acquisition of ownership and possession (istihdath al-milk wa al-yad).
C) The certainty of emptiness of the womb.
D) The command of the Prophet ﷺ.

Key Takeaway
The ruling of istibra' is tied to the legal cause of new ownership; thus, a menstruation occurring before acquisition does not satisfy it, and a fresh istibra' must be observed.

Stratagem to Avoid Istibra' When Purchasing a Married Woman
Explaining a legal stratagem to avoid istibra' by marrying before purchase

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi legal stratagem (hila) to avoid istibra' when purchasing a married woman and identify the conditions under which it is valid.

Core Idea
A properly executed marriage before purchase voids the obligation of istibra'.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains a legal stratagem (hila) recognized in later Hanafi fatwa to avoid the obligation of istibra' (waiting period) when a man purchases a female slave who is his wife. The stratagem involves marrying her to a third party before purchase, then divorcing after purchase. The text also discusses intra-Hanafi disagreements on the timing of istibra' obligation. Mastering this hila will help you navigate cases where the default rule leads to unnecessary hardship.

Guided Path
1
Understand the default rule of istibra'
Istibra' is required when purchasing a female slave who had conjugal relations, to rule out pregnancy. This is the baseline ruling.
Hanafi: wajib upon purchase; other schools differ.
2
Identify the problem
If the buyer is purchasing his own wife, requiring istibra' is unnecessary because she was already permissible to him. A stratagem is sought to avoid hardship.
The purchase changes ownership but does not change permissibility of intercourse if she was already his wife.
3
Learn the primary hila
The seller marries the woman to a trustworthy third party before the buyer's purchase. Then the buyer purchases her, and the third party divorces her. The buyer never owned her while she was unmarried and not in 'iddah, so no istibra'.
If the third party is not trustworthy, the stratagem may fail.
4
Recognize the alternative hila
The buyer marries her before taking possession. Her permissibility continues through marriage, not ownership. Even if he later takes possession, the obligation of istibra' does not arise because it was not triggered at the moment of ownership.
This is based on the principle that istibra' is linked to the possibility of intercourse at the time of ownership.
5
Analyze the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf and one narration of Muhammad hold that istibra' becomes obligatory upon the contract itself, confirmed by possession. However, if she menstruates at the buyer's before divorce, istibra' is not required even in their view. The text's preferred view (mufta bihi) is that the hila works if divorce occurs before possession.
The later fatwa collections rely on the hila as valid despite these objections.

وإن كانت أن يُنكحها البائع قبل الشراء أو المشتري قبل قبضِهِ مَن يوثق به ثم يشتري ويقبض، فيطلق الزوج) : أي إن كانت تحتَهُ حرَّة ، فالحيلة أن يُنكحها البائع قبل شراء المشتري رجلاً عليه اعتماد أن يطلقها، ثم يشتري المشتري ، ثمَّ يطلق الزوج ، فإنَّه لا يجب الاستبراء ؛ لأنه اشترى منكوحة الغير، ولا يحل وطؤها، فلا استبراء ، فإذا طلقها الزوج قبل الدخول حل على المشتري، وحينئذ لم يوجد حدوث الملك فلا استبراء، أو يُنكحها المشتري قبل القبض ذلك ثم الحل له لم يتجدد بملك الرقبة ؛ لأنها كانت حلالاً له قبل ذلك بالنكاح، وهذا معنى قول الشارح : ثم إذا اشترى زوجته لا يجب أيضاً. قاله الجلبي. قوله : رجلاً عليه اعتماد أن يطلقها ؛ لأنه إذا لم يكن ثقة يجوز أن لا يطلقها، فكان احتيالاً عليه لا له، والحيلة في تمشية هذه الحيلة أن يزوجها المشتري على أن يكون أمرها بيده ، يطلقها متى شاء. قاله الجلبي. قوله : أو ينكحها المشتري قبل القبض ... الخ ؛ ولو قبضها يبيعها من آخر، ثم يفعل ما مرّ فيسقط. كذا في التسهيل، قال قاضي خان : إلا أن في هذا نوع شبهة، فإنّ عند أبي يوسف هو إحدى الروايتين عند محمد كما اشتراها يجب الاستبراء إلا الوجوب، يتأكد بالقبض، والتزويج بعد الشراء لا يسقط، والاستبراء وجب بنفس العقد إلا أن تحيض عند المشتري بحيضة قبل الطلاق. فحينئذ لا يجب الاستبراء في قولهم وقيل في إسقاطه لا بد أن يكاتبها المولى ثم يأمرها بالعجز على سبيل الرفق واللطف فإذا عجزت نفسها صارت قنة، ولا استبراء ؛ لأنه سقط بالكتابة أوّلاً ، والساقط لا يعود وهذا الوجه هو الأليق بالأمراء والسلاطين. قاله الجلبي. [١] قوله : ثم يقبضها ثم يطلقها الزوج ... الخ ؛ وإنما اعتبر تقديم القبض على الطلاق ؛ لأنه لو طلقها قبله كان على المشتري الاستبراء إذا قبضها في أصح الروايتين عن محمد ؛ لأنه إذا طلقها قبل القبض، فإذا قبضها والقبض بحكم العقد ؛ أي بمنزلة العقد، صار كأنه اشتراها في هذه الحالة، وليست في نكاح ولا عدة، فيلزمه الاستبراء. [٢] قوله : ولا يحل الوطء....الخ ؛ فلا يجب الاستبراء ؛ لأن القبض إذ ذلك ليس بممكن من الوطء، وجزء العلة هو الممكن منه، ألا ترى أن تزويج المشتري وإن كان قبضاً حكماً لم يعتبر لكونه مزيلاً للتمكن. قاله الجلبي.
And if it is that the seller marries her before the purchase, or the buyer before receiving possession marries her to a trustworthy person, then he buys and takes possession, and the husband divorces: i.e., if a free woman is under him, the stratagem is that the seller before the buyer's purchase marries her to a man upon whom reliance can be placed to divorce her, then the buyer buys, then the husband divorces, then istibra' is not obligatory; because he bought a woman who is another's wife, and it is not permissible to have intercourse with her, so no istibra'. If the husband divorces her before consummation, she becomes permissible to the buyer, and then ownership did not arise, so no istibra'. Or the buyer marries her before taking possession, then the permissibility for him is not renewed by ownership of the slave-body; because she was permissible to him before by marriage. This is the meaning of the commentator's statement: 'Then if he buys his wife, it is also not obligatory.' Al-Jalabi said. His statement: 'A man upon whom reliance can be placed to divorce her': because if he is not trustworthy, it is possible that he does not divorce her, so it would be a trick against him, not for him. The stratagem to make this stratagem work is that the buyer marries her with the condition that her affair is in his hand, he divorces her whenever he wishes. Al-Jalabi said. His statement: 'Or he marries her before possession...' etc.: And if he takes possession, he sells her to another, then does what passed, so it falls away. Thus in al-Tashil. Qadi Khan said: However, there is a kind of doubt in this. According to Abu Yusuf, it is one of two narrations from Muhammad: as he bought her, istibra' is obligatory, but the obligation is confirmed by possession, and marriage after purchase does not remove it, and istibra' becomes obligatory by the contract itself unless she menstruates at the buyer's with one menstruation before divorce. Then at that time istibra' is not obligatory in their view. And it is said in its removal: it is necessary that the master makes a kitabah with her, then orders her to become incapable in a gentle and kind manner. If she makes herself incapable, she becomes a slave-girl, and no istibra'; because it fell away by kitabah initially, and what has fallen does not return. This aspect is most fitting for princes and sultans. Al-Jalabi said. [1] His statement: 'Then he takes possession of her, then the husband divorces...' etc.: The precedence of possession over divorce is considered because if he divorced before it, istibra' would be incumbent upon the buyer when he takes possession, in the more authentic of the two narrations from Muhammad; because if he divorces before possession, and possession is by virtue of the contract—i.e., like the contract—it becomes as if he bought her in this state, and she is not in marriage nor 'iddah, so istibra' is necessary. [2] His statement: 'And intercourse is not permissible...' etc.: So istibra' is not obligatory; because possession at that time is not possible for intercourse, and part of the cause is the possibility of it. Do you not see that the buyer's marriage, even if it is a constructive possession, is not considered because it removes the possibility. Al-Jalabi said.
Source pages p. 308

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a legal stratagem (hila) to avoid the obligation of istibra' (waiting period for pregnancy) when a man purchases a free woman who is his wife. The text explains that if the seller marries the woman to a trusted third party before the buyer's purchase, then the buyer purchases her, and the third party divorces her, istibra' is not required because the buyer never owned her while she was unmarried. Alternatively, if the buyer marries her before taking possession, her permissibility continues. The commentary discusses variations and objections. Qadi Khan notes a doubt: according to Abu Yusuf and one narration of Muhammad, istibra' becomes obligatory upon purchase, though the obligation is confirmed by possession. However, if she menstruates at the buyer's before divorce, istibra' is not required in their view. Another stratagem involves kitabah (contract of manumission). The relied-upon position is that the first stratagem is valid if properly executed, and istibra' is not required.

Key Terms
الاستبراء (al-istibra'): A waiting period to ensure a female slave is not pregnant, required when ownership changes.
الحيلة (al-hila): A legal stratagem to achieve a permissible result while adhering to the letter of the law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
After the buyer purchases the woman and the third husband divorces her, why is istibra' not required?
A) Because she was married to the buyer before purchase.
B) Because the buyer never owned her while she was unmarried and not in 'iddah.
C) Because she menstruated after purchase.
D) Because the buyer took possession before marriage.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits a hila to avoid istibra' when purchasing one's own wife, provided the marriage to a third party occurs before purchase and divorce after possession.

Prohibition of Intercourse After Acts of Desire on Two Slave Women
Defining prohibition of intercourse with two slave women after desirous acts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the prohibition of intercourse with two slave women after acts of desire, identify the condition of desire (shahwa), and explain the dalil for equating acts of desire with intercourse.

Core Idea
Acts of desire with shahwa are like intercourse in prohibiting union of two slave women.

This passage from the Kitab al-Karahiyya of al-Hidayah clarifies a specific prohibition: a master who performs desirous acts on both his slave women cannot have intercourse with either until he makes one unlawful. The ruling hinges on the presence of desire (shahwa). We will examine the ruling, the conditions, and the legal proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is performing incitements to intercourse (dawa'i al-wat') such as kissing, touching, and looking at private parts with desire on two slave women. The ruling is that intercourse with both is haram until one is made unlawful.
2
Locate the dalil
The text cites the principle that incitements to intercourse have the ruling of intercourse, based on an absolute text (nass mutlaq) or the qiyas that the inciter is like the act itself.
The exact source of the nass is not specified here; it refers to general textual principles.
3
Examine the condition of desire
The ruling applies only when the acts are done with shahwa (desire). Without desire, the acts are not legally significant.
The commentary explicitly restricts the ruling to acts with desire.
4
Apply the three scenarios
There are three cases: (1) no acts on either → free choice; (2) acts on one only → only that one is permissible; (3) acts on both with desire → both are prohibited until one is made unlawful.
The resolution is to make one slave unlawful by sale, gift, or marriage.
5
Recognize the relied-upon position
This is the mufta bihi in the Hanafi school, as stated in the matn and commentary.
See Fath Bab al-`Inayah for further details.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن فعل بشهوة إحدى دواعي الوطء بأمتيه، لا تجتمعان نكاحاً، حرم عليه وطؤهما بدواعيه حتى يحرم أحديهما. دواعي الوطء هي القبلة، والمس بشهوة، والنظر إلى فرجها بشهوة، فإن لدواعي الوطء حكم الوطء، وتحريم أحديهما يكون بإزالة الملك كلاً أو بعضاً أو بإنكاحها. [٣] قوله : بأمتيه لا تجتمعان...الخ ؛ هذه على ثلاثة أوجه : قبلهما، أو لم يقبل واحدة منهما، أو قبل إحداهما ، فإن لم يقبلهما أصلاً كان له أن يقبل وطء أيهما شاء، سواء كان اشتراهما معاً أو على التعاقب، وإن كان قبل إحداهما كان له أن يطأ المقبلة دون الأخرى. وأما إذا قبلهما بشهوة فالحكم ما ذكره في «المتن»، وإنما قيدناه بشهوة ؛ لأنه إذا لم يكن قبلهما بها لا يكون معتبراً، وهذا هو مذهب عليه ؛ لإطلاق قوله : ( لأن النص مطلق فيتناولها أو لأن الداعي إلى الوطء بمنزلته في التحريم. ينظر : فتح باب العناية (٣: ٢٠).
And whoever does with desire one of the incitements to intercourse to his two slave women who do not both come together in marriage, intercourse with both of them is prohibited due to those incitements until he makes one of them unlawful. The incitements to intercourse are kissing, touching with desire, and looking at her private part with desire. For incitements to intercourse have the ruling of intercourse. And prohibition of one of them is by removing ownership entirely or partially, or by marrying her off. [3] His statement: 'his two slave women who do not both come together...' etc.; this has three cases: he kissed both, or did not kiss any, or kissed one. If he did not kiss any at all, he may have intercourse with whichever he wishes, whether he bought them together or sequentially. If he kissed one, he may have intercourse with the kissed one and not the other. As for if he kissed both with desire, the ruling is what the matn mentioned. We have restricted it to desire because if he did not kiss them with it, it is not considered. This is the relied-upon school; due to the absolute nature of the text: because the text is absolute so it includes them, or because the inciter to intercourse is like intercourse in prohibition. See: Fath Bab al-`Inayah (3:20).
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage from the chapter on disliked matters (Kitab al-Karahiyya) addresses a specific prohibition: if a man performs an act of desire (such as kissing, touching, or looking with desire) on two slave women who are his property but cannot both be married simultaneously (since they are his slaves, marriage is not applicable), he is prohibited from having intercourse with both until he makes one of them unlawful. The prohibition is because acts of desire are treated like actual intercourse in legal effect. The commentary elaborates three scenarios: if he has not performed any act of desire on either, he may have intercourse with either; if he performed a desirous act on one, he may have intercourse with that one only; if he performed desirous acts on both with desire, he cannot have intercourse with either until he makes one prohibited through sale, gift, or marriage to another. The restriction to desire (shahwa) is crucial; without desire, the act does not trigger the prohibition. This is the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
دواعي الوطء (dawa'i al-wat'): Acts that incite desire, such as kissing, touching, and looking at private parts with desire.
الشهوة (al-shahwa): Sexual desire; the criterion that makes acts of touching, kissing, etc. legally significant.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man kisses one of his two slave women without desire, then later kisses the other without desire, what is the ruling?
A) He may have intercourse with both.
B) He may only have intercourse with the first.
C) He may not have intercourse with either until he makes one unlawful.
D) He may have intercourse with the second only.

Key Takeaway
Acts of desire (kissing, touching, looking) performed with shahwa on both slave women make intercourse with both haram until one is made unlawful.

Combining Acts of Desire with Two Slave Women: Prohibition and Conditions
Ruling on prohibition of intercourse after acts of desire with two slave women

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a man commits acts of desire with both his slave women and apply the condition of desire to determine whether the prohibition is triggered.

Core Idea
Acts of desire with two slave women prohibit intercourse with both until one is made lawful.

This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah explores the prohibition on combining two slave women for intercourse after the owner has engaged in acts of desire with both. The key principle is that the means of intercourse (dawa'i al-wat') are treated like actual intercourse in creating prohibitions. We will unpack the ruling, its conditions, and the related issue of istibra' when the slave woman is sold and later divorced.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is committing 'dawa'i al-wat'' (kissing, touching, looking with desire) with both slave women. The ruling: intercourse with both becomes haram until one is made unlawful.
This ruling is from the matn and commentary.
2
Understand the legal reasoning
The means of intercourse (dawa'i) have the same ruling as intercourse itself. This is a Hanafi principle based on the absolute nature of the text. The commentary states: 'Indeed, the means of intercourse have the ruling of intercourse.'
This principle is key to understanding the prohibition.
3
Note the condition of desire (shahwa)
The prohibition only applies when the act is done with desire. Without desire, the act is not considered a means of intercourse and does not trigger the ruling.
The commentary explicitly restricts the ruling to acts done 'with desire'.
4
Distinguish the three scenarios
The commentary outlines three possibilities: he kissed both, he kissed one, or he kissed neither. Only if he kissed both with desire does the prohibition on combining apply. If he kissed one, he may only have intercourse with that one. If he kissed neither, he may have intercourse with either.
This distinction avoids overgeneralization.
5
Address the sub-issue of istibra' and possession
When a slave woman is sold, taken possession, then married and divorced, istibra' (waiting period) becomes obligatory after possession if the divorce occurred before possession. This is the soundest narration from Imam Muhammad, because possession after divorce is like a new acquisition.
This sub-issue illustrates how ownership and marriage affect the rulings.

And whoever, with desire, commits one of the means of intercourse with his two slave women, they are not combined in marriage, so it becomes forbidden for him to have intercourse with both of them due to those means until he makes one of them unlawful [to him], the man, then he takes possession of her, then the husband divorces her. Indeed, the waiting period (istibra') becomes obligatory after possession, and at that time intercourse is not permissible. And when it becomes permissible after the husband's divorce, no acquisition of ownership has occurred. (And whoever, with desire, commits one of the means of intercourse with his two slave women, they are not combined in marriage, so it becomes forbidden for him to have intercourse with both of them due to those means until he makes one of them unlawful). The means of intercourse are: the kiss, touching with desire, and looking at her private part with desire. Indeed, the means of intercourse have the ruling of intercourse. And making one of them unlawful is by removing ownership entirely or partially, or by marrying her off. [1] His statement: "Then he takes possession of her, then the husband divorces her..." etc. The condition of possession before divorce is considered because if he divorced her before possession, the buyer would have to perform istibra' when he takes possession according to the soundest of the two narrations from Muhammad, because if he divorces her before possession, then when he takes possession—and possession is by virtue of the contract, i.e., it is like the contract—it becomes as if he purchased her in that state, and she is not in marriage or waiting period, so istibra' becomes binding. (2) His statement: "And intercourse is not permissible..." etc. So istibra' is not obligatory because possession at that time does not enable intercourse, and part of the cause is what enables it. Do you not see that the marriage of the buyer, although it is a legal possession, is not considered because it removes the ability [to own]. Said by al-Jalabi. [3] His statement: "With his two slave women, they are not combined..." etc. This has three aspects: he kissed both, or did not kiss either, or kissed one. If he did not kiss either at all, he may kiss and have intercourse with whichever he wishes, whether he bought them together or consecutively. If he kissed one, he may have intercourse with the one he kissed but not the other. As for if he kissed both with desire, the ruling is what was mentioned in the text. And we restricted it to desire because if he did not kiss them with desire, it is not considered. And this is the relied-upon school [madhhab] due to the generality of his statement [the text] because the text is absolute and includes them, or because the means to intercourse is like intercourse in prohibition. Refer to Fath Bab al-'Inayah (3:20). (2) In Dhakhirat al-'Uqba (p. 582). (3) In Dhakhirat al-'Uqba (p. 582).
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage from the commentary expands on the matn ruling concerning a man who owns two slave women and commits acts of desire (kissing, touching, looking with desire) with both. The ruling states that such acts render both women prohibited for intercourse until one is made lawful through transfer of ownership (sale, gift, emancipation) or marriage. The commentary explains the legal reasoning: the means of intercourse (dawa'i al-wat') are treated like actual intercourse in terms of prohibition. The text then discusses a scenario involving sale and divorce: if a man buys a slave woman, takes possession, then marries her to someone else, and the husband divorces her, the buyer must observe istibra' after possession if the divorce occurred before possession (according to the preferred narration from Muhammad). This is because possession after divorce is like a new purchase. Another point: if the buyer marries her, that marriage is a legal possession but does not count for istibra' because it does not enable intercourse (since she is in marriage). The commentary also details three cases of kissing: if he kisses neither, he may have intercourse with either; if he kisses only one, he may have intercourse with that one; if he kisses both with desire, he is prohibited from both until one is made lawful. The condition of desire (shahwa) is emphasized because without it, the act is not legally equivalent to intercourse. The authoritative position (madhhab 'alayhi) is based on the absolute textual proof that the means to intercourse have the same prohibition as the act itself.

Key Terms
دواعي الوطء (dawa'i al-wat'): Acts that lead to or are means of intercourse: kissing, touching with desire, looking at private parts with desire.
أمتين (amateen): Two female slaves owned by the same master.
استبراء (istibra'): A waiting period of one menstrual cycle after acquiring a slave woman to ensure she is not pregnant from a previous owner.
تمليك البعض (tamlik al-ba'd): Transferring partial ownership of a slave, e.g., selling a share or giving a share as a gift.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on a man who kisses both of his slave women with desire?
A) He may have intercourse with both freely.
B) He is prohibited from intercourse with both until he makes one unlawful.
C) He must perform istibra' for both.
D) He may have intercourse with the one he kissed first.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, acts of desire (dawa'i al-wat') with two slave women are legally equivalent to actual intercourse, thus prohibiting intercourse with both until one is made lawful.
