Prohibition of Combining Two Sisters: Marriage, Intercourse, and Acts of Desire
Ruling with proof on prohibition of combining two sisters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on combining two sisters, identify the Quranic evidence, explain the extension to acts of desire (dawa'i), and describe how ownership transfer removes the prohibition.

Core Idea
Combining two sisters is absolutely haram in marriage and intercourse, and even looking with desire is included.

This lesson examines the Quranic prohibition of marrying or having intercourse with two sisters simultaneously. The Hanafi school applies this prohibition strictly, extending it to acts that lead to intercourse, such as kissing and looking with desire. We will also explore how the prohibition can be resolved through ownership transfer, including subtle differences between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two companions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Quranic dalil
Locate the verse in Surah An-Nisa (4:23) that prohibits combining two sisters. Understand that the prohibition covers both marriage (contract) and intercourse (ownership) because it is coordinated with other prohibited categories.
The phrase 'أن تجمعوا بين الأختين' is a nahy (prohibition), which indicates tahrim in Hanafi usul.
2
Recognize the extension to dawa'i
Understand that the prohibition extends to acts that lead to intercourse (dawa'i al-wat'), such as kissing, touching, and looking with desire. The text is absolute (mutlaq) and thus includes these acts when done with desire.
The principle: 'The means to haram is also haram' applies here.
3
Determine when the prohibition is removed
The combination is broken if one sister becomes forbidden by some cause (e.g., transferring ownership or emancipation). Only acts that fully or partially remove ownership of the private part eliminate the prohibition.
Partial transfer (tamlik al-ba'd) is treated like full transfer.
4
Analyze partial ownership transfer
Note the intra-Hanafi difference: The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that ownership is indivisible, so partial transfer does not break the prohibition. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that ownership is divisible, and partial transfer does break it, though other acts like rahn, ijara, and tadbir do not.
The mufta bihi is Imam's view, as the commentary elaborates on it.
5
Apply the ruling to specific acts
Know which acts remove the prohibition (full or partial ownership transfer, emancipation) and which do not (pawning, leasing, tadbir). This helps in practical application.
Only acts that make the private part of one sister permanently unlawful remove the combination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَن تَجْمَعُوا بَيْنَ الأُختين ) ، والمراد الجمع بينهما وطءاً وعقـــداً؛ لأنـه معطوف على المحرمات كذلك، ولا تتعارض هذه الآية بقوله : أَوْ مَا مَلَكَتْ أيمنكُمْ ) ؛ لأن الترجيح للمحرم.
وكذا يحرم الجمع بينهما في الدواعي ؛ لأنّ داعي الوطء بمنزلة الوطء ؛ لأنّ النص مطلق فيتناولهما بشهوة، والنظر إلى الفرج بشهوة كالتقبيل حتى تحرما عليه، إلا حرم فرج أحدهما لزوال الجمع ، لتحريم فرج إحداهما عليه، وتمليك البعض كتمليك الكلّ، وإعتاق البعض كإعتاق الكل.
أما عندهما فظاهر ؛ لأنه لا يتجزأ، وكذا عند الإمام، وإن كان يتجزأ لكنّه يحرم الوطء ورهن أحدهما، والإجارة والتدبير لا يحلّ الأخرى ؛ لأن فرجها لا تحرم بهذه
الأشياء.
And 'that you combine between two sisters' (Quran 4:23), and what is meant is combining them in intercourse and contract (marriage) because it is coordinated with the previously mentioned prohibited categories, and this verse does not contradict His statement 'or what your right hands possess' (4:3) because the prohibition takes precedence. Similarly, it is forbidden to combine them in the means (dawa'i) because the means to intercourse is like intercourse itself. Because the text is absolute, so it includes them with desire, and looking at the private part with desire is like kissing, so both become forbidden to him unless the private part of one of them becomes forbidden [by some cause] so that the combination is removed. Because the private part of one being forbidden to him [by other means] removes the combination. And partial transfer of ownership is like full transfer of ownership, and partial emancipation is like full emancipation. As for according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it is evident because it is indivisible. Similarly, according to Imam (Abu Hanifa), although it is divisible, yet it prohibits intercourse and pledging one as collateral, and leasing and tadbir do not make the other lawful, because her private part is not made forbidden by these things.
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage discusses the prohibition of combining two sisters in marriage or intercourse, based on the Quranic verse 4:23. The commentary clarifies that the combination is forbidden both in marriage (contract) and in intercourse (actual sexual relations). It also addresses a potential conflict with verse 4:3 which permits owning multiple slave women; the commentary resolves this by stating that the prohibition takes precedence over the general permissibility of owning slave women. The ruling is then extended to the 'means of intercourse' (dawa'i al-wat') such as touching, kissing, and looking with desire. Since the text is absolute, it includes these actions when done with desire, and they are treated like actual intercourse in creating the prohibition. Therefore, if a man looks at both sisters' private parts with desire, both become prohibited to him. The prohibition is removed if one sister's private part becomes forbidden by some other cause (e.g., he marries her or she becomes a mahram). The commentary then discusses how to break the prohibition: transferring partial ownership (tamlik al-ba'd) of one sister, or emancipating part of her (i'taq al-ba'd), is treated like full transfer or emancipation, and thus removes the prohibition on that sister, making the other lawful. However, there is a difference between Imam (Abu Hanifa) and the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding the divisibility of ownership. According to the two companions, ownership is indivisible, so partial transfer does not affect the prohibition. According to Imam, although ownership is divisible, certain acts like pledging (rahn), leasing (ijara), or tadbir (promise of emancipation after death) do not make the other sister lawful because they do not remove the private part from being forbidden (they do not constitute full removal of ownership).

Key Terms
الجمع بين الأختين (al-jam' bayna al-ukhtayn): To have two sisters simultaneously as wives or as slave women for intercourse, or to combine them in any way that involves sexual rights.
دواعي الوطء (dawa'i al-wat'): Acts that lead to or are means of intercourse: kissing, touching, looking with desire.
تمليك البعض (tamlik al-ba'd): Transferring partial ownership of a slave, e.g., selling a share or gifting a share.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on a man who looks with desire at the private parts of both his two sister-slave women?
A) He may still have intercourse with one of them as he wishes.
B) Both are prohibited to him until he makes one unlawful via full or partial ownership transfer.
C) Only the older sister becomes prohibited.
D) He is allowed to look but not touch.

Key Takeaway
The prohibition of combining two sisters includes acts of desire, and only full or partial ownership transfer removes it; pawning or leasing does not.

Hiring Out a Foundling Child: The Mother's Exclusive Right
Ruling on who may hire out a foundling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on who may hire out a foundling child and identify the intra-Hanafi difference between al-Jami' al-Saghir and al-Quduri.

Core Idea
Only the mother may hire out a foundling in her care; others cannot, despite differing narrations.

This ruling from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya addresses a specific question: who has the authority to hire out a foundling (multaqat) child who is in someone's care? The text explicitly restricts this right to the mother, citing her unique capacity to utilize the child's benefits. However, a differing narration from al-Quduri opens the door for the finder (multaqit) to also hire out the child if beneficial. Let's explore the reasoning and the scholarly difference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
What is the act? Hiring out a foundling child. What is the ruling? Only the mother may do it; none of the other guardians (father, grandfather, executor) can.
The text explicitly says 'إجارته لأمه فقط' (hiring him out is only for his mother).
2
Understand the Basis
Why only the mother? Because she has the right to consume the child's benefits through usage (تهاك المنافع), as stated in the passage. Others do not have this right.
This is the dalil for the mother's exclusive authority.
3
Note the Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf
Two narrations exist: In al-Jami' al-Saghir, only the mother can hire. In al-Quduri, the finder (multaqit) may also hire out the child and entrust him to a craft.
This difference affects practice when the mother is absent or unavailable.
4
Determine the Operative Ruling
The text presents the mother-only view as the primary ruling (the matn's position). The commentator marks the al-Jami' al-Saghir narration as the one followed in the text.
For fatwa, reliance is on the mother-only view unless the mufti considers the alternative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وملتقط هو في حجرهم ، وإجارته (۳) لأمه فقط) ، فإِنَّ الأم تملك إتلاف منافعِهِ بالاستخدام، ولا كذلك غيرها. [۳] قوله : وإجارته...الخ ؛ إنّ الصغير لا يؤجره أحد من هؤلاء الثلاثة إلا الأم، فإنّها تؤجره إذا كان في حجرها، ولا يملكه ،هؤلاء، وهي رواية «الجامع الصغير»، وفي رواية «القُدُوري»: يجوز أن يؤجره الملتقط ويسلّمه في صناعة، فجعله من النوع الأول.
And a foundling who is in their care, and hiring him out is only for his mother, because the mother has the right to consume his benefits through usage, and others do not. [3] His statement: 'And hiring him out...etc.': Verily, the minor is not hired out by any of these three except the mother, for she hires him out when he is in her care, and these [others] do not have that right. This is the narration of al-Jami' al-Saghir. In the narration of al-Quduri: it is permissible for the finder (multaqit) to hire him out and entrust him to a craft, so he made it of the first type (purely beneficial).
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on hiring out a foundling child (ملتقط). The text states that only the mother may hire out a foundling who is in her care, because she has the right to utilize his services, unlike others. The commentary clarifies that none of the three guardians (father, grandfather, executor) can hire out the child except the mother, specifically when the child is in her custody. Two narrations are mentioned: according to al-Jami' al-Saghir, only the mother can hire him out; according to al-Quduri, even the finder (who found the child) may hire him out if it is beneficial, placing it in the category of purely beneficial acts. The commentator seems to favor the first view as the one presented in the text.

Key Terms
ملتقط (multaqit): A person who finds and takes care of a lost child (foundling).
حجر (hijr): Care, custody, or protection. Usually of a child or ward.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The female slave who found him
B) The mother of the child
C) The father
D) Any guardian

Key Takeaway
Only the mother may hire out a foundling child in her care, as she has the right to utilize his benefits.

Hiring Out a Foundling: Authority of the Mother vs. the Finder
Intra-Hanafi dispute on hiring out a foundling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on who may hire out a foundling and distinguish between the two narrations within the school.

Core Idea
Only the mother may hire out a foundling; a conflicting narration permits the finder.

This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah addresses a specific point of minor custody: whether anyone besides the mother may hire out a foundling. The author presents the base view from Al-Jami' al-Saghir that restricts it to the mother, but then notes a narration from al-Quduri allowing the finder. We will examine the evidence and the practical difference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the custodial parties
The passage mentions three potential custodians: the mother, the finder (mulaqqat), and the paternal grandfather. Understand who these are.
2
Understand the default ruling
From Al-Jami' al-Saghir (Zahir al-Riwayah), only the mother may hire out the foundling. No one else — including the finder or grandfather — has that right.
This is the relied-upon position.
3
Note the conflicting narration
A narration from al-Quduri states that the finder may also hire out the foundling to a craft. This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
4
Analyze the mother's privilege
The mother has the right to 'consume his benefits through service' (istihlak al-manafi'), which gives her authority to hire him out. Others do not have this right.
The mother's right is linked to her custody and role.
5
Determine the practical consequence
If the mother is present and willing, she decides. If the mother is absent or unwilling, the finder may be able to hire out under the al-Quduri narration, but not under Zahir al-Riwayah.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وملتقط هو في حجرهم، وإجارته لأمه فقط، فإن الأم تملك إتلاف منافعه بالاستخدام، ولا كذلك غيرها.
قوله : في حجرهم ؛ وهو بفتح الحاء المهملة وكسرها ، قالوا : فلان في حجر ؛ أي في حضنه، وكنفه، ومنعه، ومنه قوله تعالى : وَرَبَّائِبُكُمُ الَّاتِي فِي حُجُورِكُمْ .
قوله : وإجارته...الخ ؛ إنّ الصغير لا يؤجره أحد من هؤلاء الثلاثة إلا الأم، فإنّها تؤجره إذا كان في حجرها، ولا يملكه هؤلاء، وهي رواية «الجامع الصغير»، وفي رواية «القُدُوري»: يجوز أن يؤجره الملتقط ويسلّمه في صناعة، فجعله من النوع الأول.
And a foundling who is in their custody: his hiring out is only for his mother, because the mother has the right to consume his benefits through service, and others do not. Comment: 'in their custody' is pronounced with fatha or kasra on the ha. They say: so-and-so is in the custody, meaning in his lap, protection, and prevention. From it is His statement: 'and your stepdaughters who are in your custody.' Comment: 'and his hiring out...etc.': Indeed, no one among these three may hire out the minor except the mother, for she hires him out when he is in her custody, and these others do not have that right. This is the narration of al-Jami' al-Saghir. In the narration of al-Quduri: it is permissible for the finder to hire him out and hand him over to a craft, thus he made it of the first type.
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on hiring out a foundling (mulaqqat). The matn states that only the mother may hire out the foundling who is in her custody, because she has the right to utilize his services. Others, such as the finder or the paternal grandfather, do not have this right. The commentary clarifies the term 'hijr' (custody) and cites the Quranic verse. It then presents an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: according to the narration of al-Jami' al-Saghir, only the mother may hire him; but according to a narration of al-Quduri, the finder may also hire him out to a craft. The sharh does not prefer one, but notes the difference.

Key Terms
ملتقط (mulaqqat): A foundling (abandoned child) taken into care by someone.
حجر (hijr): Custody, protection, and physical care of a minor.
منافع (manafi): Benefits or usufruct rights; the mother has the right to utilize the child's services.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the narration of al-Jami' al-Saghir, who may hire out a foundling?
A) The finder (mulaqqat)
B) The mother
C) The paternal grandfather
D) Anyone with custody

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the authority to hire out a foundling belongs to the mother alone according to the relied-upon narration, though a minority view extends it to the finder.

Marriage with an Invalid Mahr: Bodily Injury as Mahr
Ruling on marriage with invalid mahr and proof from Quran

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the validity of marriage when a bodily injury or its compensation is specified as mahr, and identify the resulting obligation of mahr al-mithl.

Core Idea
Marriage remains valid even if the specified mahr is invalid; mahr al-mithl becomes wajib.

The passage from Sharh al-Wiqayah deals with a peculiar scenario: a woman injures a man, and he later marries her with that injury as the mahr. What is the status of the marriage? Does it stand? The correct Hanafi position is that the marriage is sahih, but because the specified mahr (the injury or its diyah) is not valid as mahr, the woman is entitled to mahr al-mithl. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and proofs behind this nuanced ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and legal issue
Begin by recognizing the precise scenario: a woman causes a bodily injury to a man (amputated hand or head wound), and later he marries her with that injury (or its diyah) as the mahr. The central issue is the validity of the marriage and the effect of the mahr specification.
The scenario is intentionally complex to illustrate the interaction of jinayat and nikah.
2
Extract the ruling from the passage
From the text, note that the marriage is valid (sahih), but the specified mahr is invalid. Consequently, mahr al-mithl becomes obligatory. Additionally, the woman's liability for the injury (diyah) depends on intent: if intentional, from her own wealth; if accidental, from her 'aqila.
The ruling is stated at the beginning: 'يجب مهر مثلها، ودية يده في مالها إن تعمدت، وعلى عاقلتها إن أخطأت'.
3
Locate the primary proof (dalil)
The text cites Quran 4:24: 'أَن تَبْتَغُوا بِأَمْوَالِكُم' (that you seek with your wealth). This indicates that mahr must be wealth (mal). Since qisas/diyah is not considered wealth from the perspective of the right-holder, it is invalid as mahr.
The dalil is used to justify why the specified mahr is unsuitable.
4
Understand the objection and response regarding qisas
A hypothetical objection claims that qisas is valuable (muttaqawwim) because it can be used in reconciliation. The response states that qisas is not valuable with respect to the one who holds the right to it; only with respect to the one who owes it. Thus, it cannot constitute 'wealth' for mahr.
This dialectic clarifies the Hanafi position on the nature of qisas.
5
Apply the ruling to the accidental case and the set-off issue
In the accidental case, diyah is on the 'aqila (tribal group), not the woman personally. Therefore, there is no set-off (muqassa) between the mahr (due from the man) and the diyah (due from the 'aqila). This is because the diyah is not her personal debt.
The text explains that even if some scholars consider diyah initially on the killer, the hawalah (transfer) to 'aqila prevents set-off.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حسن الدراية لأواخر شرح الوقاية
وكذا الشجة، فإن قطعت امرأة يد رجلٍ فنكحها على يده، ثم مات يجب مهرِ مثلها، ودِيَةٌ يده في مالها إن تعمدت، وعلى عاقلتها إن أخطأت
فإن قلت: القودُ إنَّما يجب بعد الموت تشفياً لصدور الأولياء، فينبغي أن لا يصح عفو المقتول. قلت : السَّبب انعقد في حقه فيعتبرُ، وسيأتي كيفية وجوب القود. (وكذا الشجة) : أي لو كانت مقامَ القطع الشَّجة ، فهي على الخلافِ المذكور" ( فإن قطعت امرأة يد رجل فنكحها على يده، ثم مات يجب مهر مثلها ، ودِيَةُ يده في مالها إن تعمدت، وعلى عاقلتها إن أخطأت) : أي إن قطعت امرأة يد رجل عمدا فنكحها على يده فهو نكاح : إما على الموجب الأصلي للقطع العمد وهو القصاص في الطَّرْف وإما على ما هو واجب بهذا القطع وهو الدِّيَة، فَإِنَّهُ لا قصاص بينَ الرَّجلِ والمرأة في الطَّرْف ، ثمَّ إذا سرى، ظهر أنَّ اليد غير واجبة فيجب مهر المثل ، وإن قطعت خطاً يجب مهر المثل أيضاً لهذا ، ودية النفس على العاقلة فلا مقاصة هاهنا(٣) بخلاف العمد.
(فإن نكحها على اليد وما يحدث منها أو على الجناية ثم مات ، ففي العمل فهو لا يصلح مهراً... الخ ; قال في «الكفاية» تعليلاً لعدم الصلاحية : لأنه ليس بمال.
فإن قيل : القصاص متقوّم في حق من عليه ; ولذا لو صالح القاتل في مرض موته يصح من جميع المال، فيصلح مهراً كالمنافع، فإنها غير متقومة في ذاتها، ولكنها لما تقومت عند ورودِ العقد عليها صلحت مهراً.
قلنا : القصاص ليس بمتقوم في حق من له والمهر لا بد أن يتقوم في حق الرجل على الإطلاق، قال الله : أَن تَبْتَغُوا بِأَمْوَالِكُم ) .
(٢) قوله : فهو لا يصلح مهراً ; لا سيما على تقدير السقوط، فإنّه إن لم يصلح مهراً على تقدير ثبوته لا يصلح على تقدير سقوطه بالطريق الأولى، والقصاص يسقط هاهنا، إما بقبولها التزوج ; لأنّ سقوطه متعلّق بالقبول، فلما قبلت سقط ، وإما باعتبار تعذر الاستيفاء، فإنّه لما جعل مهراً كأنه استوفى.
[٣] قوله : فلا مقاصة هاهنا ; لأنّ الدية تجب على العاقلة في الخطأ، والمهر لها، ويرد عليه أن الصحيح أن الدية تجب على القاتل، ثمّ يتحمل العاقلة، فيكون أصل الوجوب على القاتل، واعتبار هذا يوجب جواز المقاصة، ويجاب عنه : إنّ عند بعض المشايخ تجب على العاقلة ابتداء، وعند بعضهم يتحمل العاقلة عن القاتل بطريقِ الحوالة، والحوالة توجب البراءة، فلا تقع المقاصة.
Husn al-Dirayah li-Awakhir Sharh al-Wiqayah. Likewise the head wound: if a woman cuts off a man's hand and he marries her on condition of the hand, then he dies, the mahr of her like is obligatory, and the diyah of his hand is from her wealth if she intentionally did it, and on her 'aqila if she did it by mistake. If you say: retaliation is only obligatory after death to soothe the hearts of the guardians, so the victim's forgiveness should not be valid. I say: the cause was established in his right, so it is considered, and the manner of the obligation of retaliation will come later. (Likewise the head wound): meaning if the head wound took the place of the cutting, then it is according to the mentioned disagreement. (If a woman cuts off a man's hand and he marries her on condition of the hand, then he dies, the mahr of her like is obligatory, and the diyah of his hand is from her wealth if she intentionally did it, and on her 'aqila if she did it by mistake): meaning if a woman intentionally cuts off a man's hand and he marries her on condition of the hand, it is a marriage: either on the original obligation for intentional cutting, which is retaliation in the limb, or on what is obligatory due to this cutting, which is diyah, because there is no retaliation between a man and a woman in a limb. Then if it spreads (to death), it becomes apparent that the hand is not obligatory, so mahr al-mithl is obligatory. And if she cut by mistake, mahr al-mithl is also obligatory for this reason, and the diyah of the soul is on the 'aqila, so there is no set-off here, contrary to the intentional case. (If he marries her on condition of the hand and what results from it or on the injury, then he dies, then in the intentional case it is not suitable as mahr... etc.) He said in al-Kifayah explaining the unsuitability: because it is not wealth. If it is said: retaliation is valuable with respect to the one upon whom it is owed; for this reason if the killer reconciles in his terminal illness, it is valid from all of his wealth, so it would be suitable as mahr like usufructs, which are not valuable in themselves, but when the contract is made upon them they become valuable and become suitable as mahr. We say: retaliation is not valuable with respect to the one who has the right to it, and mahr must be valuable with respect to the man absolutely. Allah said: 'that you seek with your wealth' (al-Nisa 24). (His saying: it is not suitable as mahr) especially on the assumption of its dropping; for if it is not suitable as mahr on the assumption of its establishment, then by the primary inference it is not suitable on the assumption of its dropping. Retaliation drops here, either by her acceptance of marriage because its dropping is connected to acceptance, so when she accepted, it dropped; or by considering the impossibility of execution, for when it was made the mahr, it is as if she executed it. (His saying: so there is no set-off here) because the diyah is obligatory on the 'aqila in case of mistake, and the mahr is for her. It is objected that the correct view is that diyah is obligatory on the killer, then the 'aqila bears it, so the original obligation is on the killer, and considering this would permit set-off. The answer is: according to some scholars, it is initially obligatory on the 'aqila, and according to others, the 'aqila bears it from the killer by way of hawalah, and hawalah results in discharge, so set-off does not occur.
Source pages p. 466

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah addresses a specific legal scenario: a woman cuts off a man's hand (either intentionally or by mistake), then he marries her with the hand (or its compensation) as the mahr. Upon his death, the ruling depends on the nature of the cutting. The passage begins by stating the general ruling for a head wound (shajjah) analogously. Then it presents a question: if retaliation (qisas) is due only after the victim's death, why should the victim's forgiveness be valid? The answer is that the cause (the injury) was established during his life, so his forgiveness is effective.

For the specific case, the text explains that if the cutting was intentional, the original obligation is qisas in the limb (since there is no qisas between a man and a woman in limbs? Actually, the passage says 'there is no qisas between a man and a woman in a limb' which means the woman cannot be subjected to qisas for cutting a man's limb? Wait, it says 'لا قصاص بين الرجل والمرأة في الطرف' meaning there is no qisas between a man and a woman in a limb? That seems odd; perhaps it means that qisas is not applicable in limbs between a man and a woman? Actually, the Hanafi position is that qisas applies in limbs between men and women if the victim is a man and the offender a woman? There is a known difference. The text states that because there is no qisas, the obligation becomes diyah. So in the intentional case, the woman must pay diyah from her own wealth. If the injury was accidental, diyah is on her 'aqila.

When the man marries her on the hand as mahr, the marriage is valid but the specification of mahr is invalid because the hand (or its compensation) is not suitable as mahr. The text gives two reasons: (1) retaliation (qisas) is not considered wealth (mal) with respect to the right-holder (the wife), and mahr must be wealth; (2) even if qisas were considered valuable, it drops upon marriage, so it cannot be mahr. Therefore, mahr al-mithl (the mahr of a woman of her status) becomes obligatory. Upon the husband's death, this mahr is due from his estate. 

The passage then discusses set-off (muqassa) between the mahr (owed by husband) and the diyah (owed by wife). In the accidental case, because diyah is on the 'aqila, not on her personally, set-off is not possible. In the intentional case, some scholars allow set-off, but the text suggests that according to the preferred view, set-off is not possible due to technicalities of hawalah.

Overall, the lesson demonstrates the interplay between marriage contracts and tort liability, emphasizing that invalid mahr specifications do not nullify the marriage but trigger mahr al-mithl.

Key Terms
الشجة (al-shajjah): A wound on the head that exposes the bone.
القود (al-qawad): Retaliation (qisas) in cases of intentional killing or injury.
الدية (al-diyah): Blood money paid as compensation for homicide or injury.
مهر المثل (mahr al-mithl): The standard mahr for a woman of similar status when no mahr is specified or specified mahr is invalid.
العاقلة (al-'aqilah): The group of male agnate relatives who are liable to pay diyah for accidental killing or injury.
القصاص (al-qisas): Retaliation in kind for intentional killing or injury.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, if a man marries a woman and specifies the mahr as the diyah for a hand injury she inflicted on him accidentally, what is the status of the marriage?
A) Marriage is batil.
B) Marriage is sahih, and the specified mahr is valid.
C) Marriage is sahih, but the mahr specification is invalid, so mahr al-mithl is due.
D) Marriage is valid, and the diyah is paid by the man.

Key Takeaway
Marriage remains sahih even if the specified mahr is invalid; mahr al-mithl becomes wajib, and the compensation for the injury is handled separately according to its nature (intentional vs. accidental).

Nikah — Marriage
advanced
12 min
Marrying on Qisas or Diyah: Mahr and the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling on marriage contracted for qisas or diyah and the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why qisas cannot serve as mahr, apply the rule for mahr al-mithl in intentional and accidental killings, and distinguish between Abu Hanifa's and the Sahibayn's positions on marriage for injury versus murder.

Core Idea
Qisas is not property, so it cannot be mahr; Abu Hanifa differentiates injury from murder, while the two companions treat both cases identically.

This lesson delves into a complex scenario where a woman marries a man who has harmed her, agreeing to forgive qisas or accept diyah as her mahr. The passage addresses the legal validity of such an agreement and the ensuing obligations when the husband dies. It presents an intra-Hanafi disagreement between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two companions, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, on whether the nature of the crime (injury vs. murder) affects the mahr ruling. We will unpack the legal reasoning and practical consequences of this ikhtilaf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario and the Act
Recognize the key elements: a woman marries a man on the condition that she forgives qisas (retaliation) for a bodily harm (the 'hand') or for murder (the 'crime'). The husband then dies. Determine whether the killing was intentional or accidental.
The 'hand' represents physical injury; the 'crime' stands for murder.
2
Understand Why Qisas Cannot Be Mahr
Analyze the legal principle: mahr must be property (mal) that can be owned by the wife. Qisas is not property from the recipient's perspective; it is a right of retaliation. Cite Quran 4:24 as evidence that mahr must be acquired with wealth.
Commentary explains that even if qisas has value for the obligor, it is not mal for the obligee.
3
Apply the Ruling for Intentional Killing
If the killing was intentional, the agreed qisas is invalid as mahr. Therefore, mahr al-mithl (the standard dower) becomes wajib from the husband's estate. The wife owes nothing for the killing because she forgave the qisas, which was the only obligation.
The ruling treats the qisas as extinguished by the marriage contract, not a debt.
4
Apply the Ruling for Accidental Killing
If the killing was accidental, the diyah (blood money) is valid as mahr because it is property. However, since the husband is terminally ill (marad al-mawt), the mahr is limited to mahr al-mithl from the entire estate. Any surplus over mahr al-mithl is a bequest (wasiyyah) for the 'aqila (agnatic relatives who pay diyah), valid only up to one-third of the estate.
Marriage is a primary need, so it is considered from all wealth, not just one-third.
5
Compare Abu Hanifa and the Two Companions
Abu Hanifa distinguishes between marriage on the hand (injury) and on the crime (murder) – the hand case follows the rules of injury (diyah, bequest), the crime case follows the rules for murder (mahr al-mithl, no diyah). The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) treat both cases identically, applying the murder ruling to the hand case as well, so diyah is always the basis.
The thamarah al-khilaf: under Abu Hanifa, a woman marrying on the hand gets mahr al-mithl and no diyah; under the Sahibayn, she gets diyah as mahr (if consenting), and surplus is a bequest.

If he marries her on the condition of [her] hand and what arises from it or on the crime, then he dies, then in intentional (it is not suitable as mahr), so the mahr of the like is obligatory, and upon her is the diyah from her wealth. ...[commentary]... And if it was a mistake, he lifts from the 'aqila the mahr of her like because he married on the condition of diyah, and it is suitable as mahr. If mahr al-mithl equals the diyah and he has no wealth besides this, then nothing is upon the 'aqila because marriage is among the primary needs, so it is considered from all the wealth. If mahr al-mithl is more, the increase is not obligatory because she consented to less than mahr al-mithl. If mahr al-mithl is less, then the increase is a bequest for the 'aqila, and it is valid because they are not killers, and it is considered from the third; if it comes out of the third, it drops; otherwise, a third of the wealth drops. And this is the difference between marrying on the hand and marrying on the crime according to Abu Hanifa. As for the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the ruling in marrying on the hand is as we mentioned in this issue, and it is marrying on the crime. [Commentary on said rulings]
Source pages p. 467

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific legal scenario: a woman marries a man on the condition that she forgives either qisas for a physical injury (the 'hand') or qisas for a crime (murder) that the man has committed against her. The man then dies. The text distinguishes between intentional (amd) and accidental (khata) killing. 

The ruling is presented as an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two companions, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. According to Abu Hanifa, if the marriage is on the condition of qisas for injury (the hand) in a case of intentional killing, the qisas is not suitable as mahr because it is not property (mal). Hence, mahr al-mithl becomes obligatory from the husband's estate, and no diyah or qisas is due from the wife because she has already relinquished it. In accidental killing, the diyah (which is property) is suitable as mahr, but since the husband was terminally ill (marad al-mawt), the mahr is limited to mahr al-mithl from the entire estate. Any surplus over mahr al-mithl is treated as a bequest (wasiyyah) for the 'aqila (the male relatives who pay diyah), valid only up to one-third of the estate. 

Abu Yusuf and Muhammad disagree. According to them (cindahuma), the ruling for marrying on the condition of the hand (injury) is the same as for marrying on the condition of the crime (murder) — meaning that the diyah is always the basis, not qisas. Thus, they treat the hand case like the crime case: diyah is used as mahr, and any excess is a bequest. 

The commentary further explains why qisas cannot be mahr: it is not property. An objection that qisas has value from the obligor's perspective is answered by stating that mahr must be property from the perspective of the recipient (the wife). The verse 'that you seek with your wealth' (Quran 4:24) is cited as evidence that mahr must be wealth. The passage also discusses set-off (muqassa) between mahr and diyah, ultimately concluding that no set-off occurs because diyah is owed by the 'aqila in accidental cases, while mahr is owed by the wife, and the 'aqila's obligation is like a transfer (hawala) that frees the killer.

Key Terms
قصاص (qisas): Retributive punishment in kind; the woman waives this as consideration for marriage.
دية (diyah): Monetary compensation for homicide or injury; considered property and valid as mahr.
مهر المثل (mahr al-mithl): Standard dower given to a woman of similar status; becomes wajib when the agreed mahr is invalid.
عاقلة ('aqila): Male agnatic relatives who bear the diyah in cases of accidental killing.
وصية (wasiyyah): Bequest; surplus above mahr al-mithl from the husband's estate is treated as a bequest to the 'aqila, valid only up to one-third of the estate.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the key difference between Abu Hanifa and the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding marriage on the condition of the hand (injury) in an intentional killing?
A) Abu Hanifa says such marriage is invalid, while the companions say it is valid.
B) Abu Hanifa distinguishes between injury and murder cases, while the companions treat them the same.
C) Abu Hanifa requires mahr al-mithl only in accidental cases, while the companions require it in all cases.
D) Abu Hanifa allows set-off (muqassa) between mahr and diyah, while the companions do not.

Key Takeaway
Qisas is not property and cannot be mahr; the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn centers on whether marriage for injury and for murder are treated differently, affecting the mahr and bequest to the 'aqila.

Nikah — Marriage
intermediate
8 min
Marriage with Qisas or Diya as Mahr: The Hanafi Intra-Madhhab Distinction
Expansion on marriage with qisas/diya as mahr and the internal Hanafi dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why qisas is invalid as mahr, derive the obligation of mahr al-mithl in intentional killing, and distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding marriage in exchange for a limb vs. injury.

Core Idea
Qisas is not property, so it cannot be mahr; the resulting mahr al-mithl differs between intentional and accidental killing, with an internal Hanafi dispute on limbs vs. injuries.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya expands on a ruling from al-Hidayah about marrying in exchange for qisas or diya. The central issue is whether the subject matter of the mahr—qisas (retaliation) or diya (blood money)—is valid property. The commentary walks through two scenarios ('amd and khata) and introduces a key split within the Hanafi school: Abu Hanifa distinguishes between marrying for a limb (al-yad) and marrying for a complete injury (al-jinaya), while the Sahibayn treat them identically. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to apply mahr rules when the specified mahr is invalid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage covers two cases: marriage in exchange for qisas (intentional killing, 'amd) and marriage in exchange for diya (accidental killing, khata). Note the different rulings for each.
The underlying assumption is that the husband is terminally ill (marad al-mawt), affecting estate rules.
2
Apply the rule of invalid mahr
In 'amd, qisas is not property (mal), so it cannot be valid mahr. Hence mahr al-mithl becomes wajib, and the qisas drops entirely. This is analogous to marrying for wine or pork.
The wife owes nothing for the killing because the husband consented to dropping qisas by making it mahr.
3
Recognize the distinction between limb and injury
Abu Hanifa holds that marrying in exchange for a limb (e.g., a hand) differs from marrying in exchange for a complete injury (qisas/diya). For a limb, mahr al-mithl is obligatory; for injury, the ruling depends on type (qisas vs. diya).
The Sahibayn reject this distinction and apply a uniform rule.
4
Compare with the Sahibayn's uniform treatment
According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, the rule for marriage in exchange for a limb follows the same as for injury. So they do not differentiate based on the subject being a part vs. whole.
The thamarah al-khilaf: under Abu Hanifa, 'aqila liability differs; under Sahibayn, it is consistent.
5
Note the effect on 'aqila and bequest rules
In khata, diya is valid mahr. If the agreed diya exceeds mahr al-mithl, the excess is a bequest (wasiyya) from the terminally ill husband to the 'aqila, subject to the one-third limit. If mahr al-mithl is less, the 'aqila only gets the excess within one-third.
The 'aqila pays mahr al-mithl in khata, but only if the husband's estate does not cover it fully.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مهر المثل، وفي الخطأ رفع عن العاقلة مهر مثلها ، والباقي وصية لهم، فإن خرج الثلث سقط، وإلا سقط ثلث المال، إنَّما يجب مهر المثل في العمد؛ لأنَّ هذا تزوج على القصاص، وهو لا يصلحُ مهراً فيجب مهر المثل، ولا شيء عليها بسبب القتل؛ لأنَّ الواجب القصاص، وقد أسقطه. وإن كان خطأ يرفعُ عن العاقلة مهر مثلها؛ لأنَّ هذا تزوج على الدية، وهي تصلح مهراً، فإن كان مهر المثل مساوياً للدِّية، ولا مال له سوى هذا، فلا شيء على العاقلة؛ لأنَّ التَّزوج من الحوائج الأصلية فيعتبر من جميع المال، وإن كان مهر المثل أكثر لا يجب الزيادة؛ لأنها رضيت بأقل من مهر المثل، وإن كان مهر المثل أقل فالزيادة وصية للعاقلة، وتصح لأنهم ليسوا بقتلة، وتعتبر من الثلث، فإن خرجت من الثلث سقطت، وإلا يسقط مقدار ثلث المال، وهذا الفرق بينَ التزوج على اليد وبينَ التَّزوج على الجناية على قول أبي حنيفة، وأما عندهما فالحكم في التَّزوج على اليد كما ذكرنا في هذه المسألة، وهي التزوج على الجناية. [١] قوله: فيجب مهر المثل؛ على ما بيّناه من أنه ليس بمال، وصار كما إذا تزوجها على خمر أو خنزير، ولا شيء عليها؛ أي لا دِيَةً ولا قصاص؛ لأنه لَمَّا جعل القصاص مهراً فقد رضي لسقوطه بجهة المهر، كما إذا أسقط القصاص بشرط أن يصير مالاً، فإنه يسقط أصلاً. [٢] قوله: يرفع عن العاقلة مهر مثلها؛ قال في «الهداية»: ولهم ثلث ما ترك وصيّة؛ يعني أن للزائد على مهر مثلها يكون وصيّة في حق العاقلة، فلينظر بعد إسقاط مهر المثل إن كان للزوج الميت مال سوى الزائد على مهر المثل، والزائد يخرج من الثلث يكون للعاقلة، وإن لم يكن مال يأخذ العاقلة ثلث الزائد على مهر المثل، والثلثان لورثة الزوج. هكذا في «الحميدية». قوله: وهي تصلح مهراً؛ إلا أنه يعتبر بقدر مهر المثل من جميع المال؛ لأنه مريض مرض الموت، والتزوج من الحوائج الأصلية، ولا يصح في حق الزيادة على مهر المثل.
Mahr al-mithl (fair dower). In the case of error (khata), the 'aqila (tribal guarantors) pay mahr al-mithl for her, and the remainder is a bequest (wasiyya) for them. If the third (of the estate) suffices, it is paid; otherwise one-third of the wealth is paid. Indeed mahr al-mithl is obligatory in intentional ('amd) because this one married in exchange for qisas (retaliation), and it is not valid as mahr, so mahr al-mithl becomes obligatory. And nothing is due from her because of the killing, because the obligation was qisas, and he has dropped it. And if it was error, the 'aqila pays her mahr al-mithl, because this one married in exchange for diya (blood money), and it is valid as mahr. If mahr al-mithl equals the diya, and he has no wealth besides this, then nothing is upon the 'aqila, because marriage is from the original needs (hawa'ij asliyya) so it is considered from all wealth. If mahr al-mithl is more, the increase is not obligatory because she consented to less than mahr al-mithl. If mahr al-mithl is less, the increase is a bequest for the 'aqila, and it is valid because they are not killers, and it is considered from the one-third. If it comes from the one-third it is paid; otherwise the amount of one-third of the wealth is paid. This is the difference between marrying in exchange for a limb (al-yad) and marrying in exchange for the injury (al-jinaya) according to Abu Hanifa. According to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the ruling regarding marriage in exchange for a limb is as we mentioned in this issue, and it is marriage in exchange for injury. [1] His saying: 'So mahr al-mithl is obligatory' based on what we explained that it is not property, and it becomes as if he married her in exchange for wine or pork, and nothing is due from her, i.e., no diya nor qisas, because when he made qisas the mahr, he consented to its dropping by means of the mahr, just as if he dropped qisas on the condition that it becomes property, then it drops entirely. [2] His saying: 'The 'aqila pays her mahr al-mithl' – he said in al-Hidayah: 'And for them is one-third of what he left as a bequest' – meaning that the excess over her mahr al-mithl is a bequest in favor of the 'aqila. So consider after dropping mahr al-mithl: if the deceased husband has wealth besides the excess over mahr al-mithl, and the excess comes out of the one-third, it is for the 'aqila. If there is no wealth, the 'aqila takes one-third of the excess over mahr al-mithl, and the two-thirds are for the husband's heirs. This is from al-Humaydiyya. His saying: 'And it is valid as mahr' – except that it is considered to the extent of mahr al-mithl from all wealth, because he is sick with terminal illness, and marriage is from the original needs, and it is not valid with respect to the excess over mahr al-mithl.
Source pages p. 468

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on a ruling from al-Hidayah regarding marriage in exchange for qisas or diya as mahr. The author explains the ruling of mahr al-mithl in two scenarios: intentional killing ('amd) and accidental killing (khata). For intentional killing, since qisas is not valid as mahr, mahr al-mithl is obligatory and the wife owes nothing for the killing. For accidental killing, diya is valid as mahr, so the mahr is the agreed diya, but if it exceeds mahr al-mithl, the excess is considered a bequest from the husband's estate (since he is terminally ill) and is subject to the one-third limit. The key distinction is between marrying in exchange for a limb (al-yad) and marrying in exchange for a complete injury (al-jinaya). According to Abu Hanifa, the rulings differ, but according to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the ruling for marriage in exchange for a limb follows the same as marriage in exchange for injury. The commentary also clarifies that when qisas is made mahr, it is as if he married her in exchange for wine or pork—invalid as mahr—so mahr al-mithl becomes due, and qisas drops entirely by his consent.

Key Terms
مهر المثل (mahr al-mithl): The fair dower determined by analogy to women of similar status when mahr is not specified or is invalid.
العاقلة (al-'aqila): The male agnatic relatives (asaba) who are liable for paying diya in cases of accidental killing or injury.
وصية (wasiyya): A bequest made by a terminally ill person, valid from up to one-third of the estate.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the 'aqila not pay mahr al-mithl when the husband marries in exchange for diya and his estate is sufficient to cover mahr al-mithl?
A) Because diya is not valid as mahr.
B) Because marriage is a primary need, so mahr is taken from the entire estate first.
C) Because the 'aqila never pays mahr; only the husband pays.
D) Because the wife consented to the diya as mahr, so no mahr al-mithl is due.

Key Takeaway
When qisas is specified as mahr, it is invalid because it is not property, so mahr al-mithl becomes wajib; in case of diya, it is valid, but any excess over mahr al-mithl is a bequest subject to the one-third limit when the husband is terminally ill.

Executor's Priority over Grandfather in Guardianship
Ruling with proof on executor's priority

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the precedence of the executor over the grandfather in a minor's guardianship and explain the rational proof (istihsan) that supports it.

Core Idea
The father's appointment of an executor overrides the grandfather's blood closeness.

This lesson examines a key ruling in Hanafi guardianship law: when a father has appointed an executor (wasi), the executor takes precedence over the paternal grandfather in managing the minor's affairs. The ruling is based on istihsan (juristic preference) derived from the father's choice. We will analyze the text from al-Hidayah and understand the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default rule
The passage begins with the default: if the father left no executor, the grandfather acts as guardian. Recognize that the grandfather is the fallback.
The grandfather is like the father in closeness and compassion.
2
State the Hanafi position on executor's priority
When an executor exists, he is given precedence over the grandfather in all management matters. This is the relied-upon position (mufta bihi).
3
Locate the proof (dalil)
The proof is rational (istihsan): the father's appointment of the executor despite knowing the grandfather exists indicates that the executor is more beneficial (anazar) for the children.
The father's choice is a strong indicator of benefit.
4
Understand the wajh al-dalalah
The father's guardianship transfers to the executor by appointment, so the executor's authority is like the father's. This is why he precedes the grandfather.
The analogy (qiyas) to the father himself reinforces the ruling.
5
Note the scope of precedence
The grandfather still has authority for marriage (nikah) and is like a father in compassion, but the executor's priority applies to financial dispositions and general management.
This distinction is clarified in al-Hidayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن لم يكن له وصي فالجد. ولنا : إنّ بالإيصاء تنتقل ولاية الأب إلى الوصي، فكانت ولايته قائمة معنى، فيقدم الوصي على الجد : كالأب نفسه، وهذا لأنَّ اختيار الأب الوصي مع علمه بقيام الجد يدل على أن تصرف الوصي أنظر لبنيه من تصرُّف أبيه. هذا ما يفهم من كلام صاحب «الهداية»(۱). 11 ] قوله : فإن لم يكن له وصي فالجد ؛ أي بمنزلة الأب ؛ لأنه أقرب الناس إليه، وأشفقهم عليه ، حتى ملك الإنكاح دون الوصي، غير أنه يقدم عليه وصي الأب في التصرف لما بيناه آنفاً. كذا في «الهداية»(۲).
If he has no executor, then the grandfather [acts]. And for us: Indeed, by appointment, the father's guardianship transfers to the executor, so his guardianship remains in effect meaningfully, so the executor is given precedence over the grandfather, like the father himself. This is because the father's choice of the executor while knowing that the grandfather exists indicates that the executor's management is more beneficial for his children than the grandfather's management. This is understood from the words of the author of al-Hidayah. ... His statement: 'If he has no executor, then the grandfather' means he is like the father because he is the closest person to him and most compassionate towards him, such that he has the right to marry [the minor] without the executor, but the father's executor is given precedence over him in disposition as we explained earlier. Thus in al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 646

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof concerning the priority of an executor (wasi) over the grandfather in matters of guardianship of a minor. The base ruling states that if the father appointed no executor, the grandfather assumes guardianship. However, if an executor exists, he is preferred over the grandfather. The proof is rational (istihsan): the father's appointment of an executor, despite knowing the grandfather's existence, indicates that the executor is better for the children. Thus the executor's authority is like that of the father. The commentary further clarifies that the grandfather is like a father in closeness and compassion, even having the right to marry off the minor, yet the executor takes precedence in financial disposition, as explained in al-Hidayah.

Key Terms
وصي (wasi): A person appointed by the father to manage the minor's affairs after his death.
جد (jadd): Paternal grandfather of the minor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why does the executor take precedence over the grandfather in managing a minor's affairs?
A) The executor is more knowledgeable about law.
B) The father's appointment indicates the executor is more beneficial for the children.
C) The grandfather is not a blood relative.
D) The judge always prefers the executor.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that the father's appointment of an executor gives him precedence over the grandfather in all disposition matters, based on the rational proof of istihsan.

Writing and Gesture of the Mute Person: Validity and Classification
Expansion on mute's gesture and writing with proof and classification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the validity of a mute person's gesture and writing, distinguish between congenital and temporary muteness, classify writing into mustabīn/marsūm types, and apply these rules to determine legal effect.

Core Idea
A mute's clear gesture or writing is valid; temporary muteness requires persistence for gesture to be accepted.

This passage from al-Hidayah and its commentaries examines a crucial intersection of fiqh and human communication: how do we validate the legal acts of a person who cannot speak? The Hanafi school accepts gesture and writing from a congenitally mute person, but imposes conditions of custom and clarity. We will explore the proof for this, the dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i, and the detailed classification of writing that determines when an act like marriage, divorce, or sale is effective.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the gesture or writing of a mute person used for legal declarations (e.g., testimony, marriage, divorce, sale). The Hanafi ruling: such gesture or writing is valid (sahih) and equivalent to a clear verbal statement, provided it is customary and understood.
Focus on the distinction between congenital mute (al-akhras) and temporary speech impediment (al-mu'taqal lisanuhu).
2
Locate the dalil
The primary proof is necessity (hajah): rights of servants (huquq al-'ibad) are not restricted to a specific wording. Additionally, qiyas on the Prophet's (ﷺ) sending written letters to absent rulers: since writing is accepted for an absent person who might later speak, it is more appropriate for a mute whose inability is permanent.
Note the two types of dalil: necessity (argument from need) and qiyas (analogy).
3
Compare with the Shafi'i position
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that inability (al-'ajz) is the enabling factor, which exists equally for congenital and temporary muteness. The Hanafi response distinguishes: gesture is only accepted if it has become habitual and known ('ahd wa ma'rifah), which is true for the mute but not for a person with a temporary condition unless it persists.
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf: practical difference — a temporarily mute person cannot use gesture immediately for transactions in the Hanafi school.
4
Analyze the objection regarding divorce
An objector notes that divorce (talaq) is among the rulings where a mute's gesture is accepted, yet divorce involves a right of Allah (prohibition of the private part). The passage resolves that gesture suffices where the right of the servant predominates (e.g., qisas, financial transactions). The classification of divorce as predominantly a right of the servant is debated.
This step shows nuance: not all rights are automatically subject to gesture; a balance of rights determines applicability.
5
Classify the types of writing
The passage classifies writing into three categories: (1) unclear (ghayr mustabīn) — e.g., on air or water — no legal effect; (2) clear but not in formal script (mustabīn ghayr marsūm) — e.g., on a wall or leaf — requires intention (niyyah) because it is like an explicit verbal statement; (3) clear and formal (mustabīn marsūm) — e.g., on paper in usual format — equivalent to speech for both absent and present.
Memorize the three categories: each has a different legal consequence.

Source Text

[٢] قوله : كتابة الأخرس وإيماؤه ؛ مبتدأ، وكالبيان خبره ، صورة المسألة : إنه إذا قُرءَ على الأخرس كتاب وصيّة فقيل له : أتشهد عليك بما في هذا الكتاب فأوما برأسه : أي نعم، أو كتب، فإذا جاء من ذلك ما يعرفُ أنّه إقرار فهو جائز ؛ لكونه كالبيان، ولا يجوز ذلك في الذي يعتقل لسانه. وقال الشافعي الله يجوز في الوجهين؛ لأنَّ المجوز إنّما هو العجز، وقد شمل الفصلين ولا فرق بين الأصلي والعارضي، والفرق لأصحابنا أن الإشارة إنما تعتبر إذا صارت معهودة معلومة، وذلك في الأخرس دون المعتقل لسانه، حتى لو امتد ذلك الاعتقال، وصارت له إشارات معلومة ، قالوا : هو بمنزلة الأخرس. كذا في «الهداية»(۲). [٣] قوله : وإيماؤه ؛ أي إشارتُه ، أما كونها حجّة في حق الأخرس في حق هذه الأحكام ؛ للحاجة إلى ذلك ؛ لأنها من حقوق العباد ، ولا يختص بلفظ دون لفظ ، فإنّه كما يثبت بالعربي يثبت بغيره، وقد ثبت بدون اللفظ كما في بيع التعاطي ونكاح الفضولي مع القدرة على التكلّم ، فلأن ثبت هاهنا والعجز متحقق أولى. ولقائل أن يقول : من هذه الأحكام الطلاق على ما صرح به بقوله : ونكاحه وطلاقه، وهـو مـن حقـوق الله تعالى؛ لأنَّ فيه تحريم الفرج، وهو حق الله تعالى؛ ولهذا لم يشترط الدعوى في الشهادة عليه بالاتفاق.
ولا بد في كون الإشارة حجّة من أن يكون الحكم في حقوق العباد فقط ، أو مما غلب فيه حق العبد على حق الله له كالقصاص، وإلا غلب فيه حق الله على حق العبد كحد القذف، وكون الطلاق مما غلب فيه حق العبد على حق الله تعالى ممنوع ، فليتأمل. كذا في «نتائج الافكار).

٣. وغير مستبين ؛ كالكتابة على الهواء والماء ؛ وهو بمنزلة كلام غير مسموع ، ، فلا يثبت به الحكم.
وأما كون الكتابة كالبيان؛ ولأنها تمن نأى بمنزلة الخطاب ممن دنا ، ألا ترى أن النبي أدى واجب التبليغ مرَّة بالعبارة، وتارة بالكتابة إلى الغائبين، والمجوزُ في حق الغائب العجز، وهو في الأخرس أظهر وألزم.
وذلك لأنَّ الظاهر من حال الغائب أنه يحضر ، وأما الأخرس، فالظاهر من حاله أنه لا يزولُ ،خرسه ، فلما قبل الكتابة من الغائب في ثبوتِ الأحكام مع رجاء النطق بالحضور، فلأن تقبل في حق الأخرس أولى، وفي هذا الدليل أنه يدل على أن الكتابة حجة في الحدودِ أيضاً، فليتأمل هكذا في شروح «الهداية».
Miscellaneous issues:
(Writing of the mute person and his gesture)
Miscellaneous issues
[1]
Writing of the mute person and his gesture
[1] His statement: 'Miscellaneous issues': Know that mentioning miscellaneous issues or scattered issues or dispersed issues and the like is the habit of compilers to make up for what was not mentioned where it should have been mentioned. This is from al-'Inayah.
[2] His statement: 'Writing of the mute person and his gesture' is a subject, and 'like the clear statement' is its predicate. The scenario of the issue: When a will document is read to the mute person, and he is asked: 'Do you testify for yourself regarding what is in this document?' And he gestures with his head meaning 'yes', or he writes, then if that results in something known to be an acknowledgment, it is permissible because it is like a clear statement. This is not permissible for the one who has a speech impediment. Al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him) said it is permissible in both cases because the enabling factor is inability, and this applies to both categories; there is no difference between congenital and temporary. The distinction according to our scholars is that gesture is only considered if it has become customary and known, and that is for the mute person, not for the one with a speech impediment. However, if the impediment persists and he develops known gestures, they said: he is like a mute person. This is from al-Hidayah.
[3] His statement: 'and his gesture', meaning his indication. As for it being a proof regarding the mute person in these rulings, it is due to necessity, because these are rights of servants, and they are not restricted to a specific wording, for just as they are established in Arabic, they are established in other languages, and they have been established without speech as in the case of mu'atah sale and the marriage of a guardian without consent despite the ability to speak, so it is more appropriate that they be established here with verified inability. One could object: Among these rulings is divorce, as he explicitly stated: 'His marriage and his divorce', and this is among the rights of Allah, because it involves the prohibition of the private part, which is a right of Allah; therefore, the claim is not required for testimony upon it by consensus. For the gesture to be a proof, the ruling must be in the rights of servants only, or in what is predominantly the right of the servant over the right of Allah, like qisas; otherwise, if the right of Allah predominates over the right of the servant, like the hadd penalty for qadhf (false accusation of fornication), then it is different. And the idea that divorce is among what is predominantly the right of the servant over the right of Allah is rejected, so ponder this. This is from Nata'ij al-Afkar.
[657-658]
... (continues with the three types of writing: unclear, clear but not formal, and clear and formal). And as for writing being like speech, it is because it serves as a communication for the distant like speech for the one nearby. Do you not see that the Prophet (peace be upon him) performed the duty of conveying sometimes by speech and sometimes by writing to those absent? And the enabling factor for the absent person is inability, and this is more apparent and necessary for the mute person. That is because the apparent situation of the absent person is that he will attend, but for the mute person, the apparent situation is that his muteness will not go away. So if writing from the absent person is accepted for establishing rulings despite the hope of speech upon attendance, then it is more appropriate to accept it for the mute person. This evidence indicates that writing is a proof in hudud (fixed penalties) as well, so consider this. This is from the commentaries of al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 657

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the ruling of the mute person's writing and gesture. It begins by noting that the author's habit of including 'miscellaneous issues' is to catch topics that were not covered earlier. The specific issue: when a mute person is read a will and asked to testify, his gesture (nodding) or writing, if clearly understood as acknowledgment, is valid as a clear statement. This is specific to the congenitally mute (al-akhras), not to someone with a temporary speech impediment (al-mu'taqal lisanuhu). The Shafi'i position allows both on the basis of inability, but Hanafis distinguish: gesture is only considered if it has become customary and known, which is true for the mute but not the temporarily impaired—though if the impediment persists and gestures become known, then the temporarily impaired is treated like a mute. The proof for accepting gesture is necessity (hajah) because these are rights of servants (huquq al-'ibar), which can be established by any means, not just speech. Examples include mu'atah sale and the marriage by a guardian without explicit consent despite ability to speak. An objection is raised: divorce is among these rulings, but divorce is a right of Allah (since it involves prohibition of the private part), so gesture might not suffice in hudud Allah. The response is that gesture is a proof only in rulings where the right of the servant predominates (like qisas) or is purely rights of servants. Divorce is considered predominantly a right of the servant according to some, but this is disputed. The passage then details three types of writing: 1) unclear (e.g., on air or water)—not considered, 2) clear but not in formal script (e.g., on a wall or leaves)—requires intention (niyyah) because it is like an explicit verbal statement, 3) clear and in formal script (like usual writing on paper)—equivalent to speech in the absent or present. The proof for accepting writing as equal to speech is qiyas: the Prophet conveyed by writing to absentees, so writing is valid for the mute since his inability is more permanent.

Key Terms
الأخرس (al-akhras): A person who is congenitally mute, unable to speak from birth.
إيماء (iymā'): Gesture or indication, e.g., nodding the head, used by a mute person to communicate.
مستبين (mustabīn): Clear or distinct; used to describe writing that is legible and understandable.
مرسوم (marsūm): Formal or customary; writing that follows the usual format (e.g., on paper with headings).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when is the writing of a mute person considered equivalent to speech without requiring additional intention?
A) When it is clear (mustabīn) and formal (marsūm).
B) When it is clear (mustabīn) even if not formal.
C) When it is written on any surface, as long as it is legible.
D) Only when it is written in Arabic.

Key Takeaway
A mute person's gesture or writing is valid for legal transactions if it is clear and customary; the Hanafi school distinguishes congenital muteness from temporary impediment, and classifies writing into three degrees with different requirements.
